The Red Page
Australian
Drama
The Australian Drama (which isn’t) per-
sists in print. Montague Grover publishes
The Minus Quantity and Other Short Plays
(The Theatre Magazine, Sydney, Is.).
And worth it. The five short plays range
from the surfragette humor of “The Minus
Quantity,’” originally published in The
Lone Hand, through the emotional moments
of “The Traitress” (A shot sounds and he
falls back into- the r00m ....), and the melo-
drama of “The Boomerang” ( Earle frees
himself from Helen and fires. The man
falls, moaning ....), and of “The Return of
Hagar” {The nurse rushes forward: Hoyte
falls back... .“Twenty years ago he stole a
human soul; I called to collect”), to the
genuine drama of “The Human Document.”
“The Minus Quantity”—which is clothes
—is not meant to be produced, since the
stage direction requires “a thousand spec-
tators on the beach, and hundreds of- bathers
shooting the breakers,” also the services of
a property wave which “lifts Beryl up and
pitches, her right on top of Jimmy, nearly
drowning him,” and subsequently sends
Beryl “scuttling 30 yards towards the
beach.” It is a pleasant short story—in its
right place in a collection of short stories.
In “The Human Document” Montague
Grover has written a play. Zack Brown, a
dealer in old wares —and stolen goods—was
sent to gaol by Detective McMurtrie for
three years. When the play opens Zack
sees the chance of paying McMurtrie back.
Chook Pengelly, a thief, arrives with a
stolen locket, which he sells to Zack. Zack
gets his daughter to light the fire under the
crucible that he keeps handy for melting
down inconvenient golden ornaments. But
he does not throw the locket in; for Chook
tells him that McMurtrie had searched him
some months before, and finding the locket
on him, had handed it back! Why? His
daughter, Lottie, reasons it out. McMurtrie
is watching Chook to discover where he dis-
poses of his stolen goods. Yes, but... .Lottie
recognises the locket: it was concerned in a
murder case, for which already a man had
been- found guilty and hanged. What was
Chook doing with it? Zack guesses the
missing links....and knows he has the evi-
dence now to send the detective, himself, to
gaol. And the detective arrives.
“You sent me to gaol,” the father says,
“to gaol for three years, when that girl there
was fourteen. Lottie was all I had. I was
crook; I’ve been crook all my life; but
Lpttie’s mother was a straight woman, and
I made up my mind that she’d be the same.
I spent all I had on that girl All I
done was done for that girl, and then you
come along and fit me for some rotten job
and send me up for three years. And when
I come out, where’s Lottie? Where was
you, Lottie?”
Lottie casually remarks, “Madame’s.”
“See how she says it,” says her father,
bitterly. “She’s a good girl at heart —-a
good girl still —but she’s lost one thing. She
don’t look on Madame’s like she would if
she’d been kept away from harm, as she
would have been if I hadn’t got into trouble
—if you hadn’t put me in. You took a
man’s body and made it a machine for three
years; but you did more, you took a woman’s
soul and wrung the sense of right and wrong
out of it and left her the wreck, body and
soul, that she was till her old dad done his
time and come out to look after her again.”
So now he has got McMurtrie. The
detective, finding that locket on Chook, knew
that it belonged to the murdered man, knew
that the other man was not the murderer,
knew that Chook was guilty. Yet he let
that other man be convicted and hanged.
But McMurtrie unconcernedly smokes his
cigar. He admits that he let the wrong
man be hanged. Why? Because the man
who was hanged was a man who infinitely
more deserved to be banged than Chook, the
murderer. He tells the story of the con-
victed man....and Lottie shows a growing
interest. He tells the story of the seduction
of a child of fourteen... .It is Lottie’s story.
That was why McMurtrie hanged him. Her
father cries: “God, I wish you’d given me
the chance to pull the drop.”
It is a telling little play, theatrically
effective, with considerable cleverness in the
construction, a fine “set”.... sure to be seen
soon on some Repertory stage. Which
sounds almost an anti-climax, when there
are so many keen business theatre men
looking everywhere (except in Australia)
for the theatrically effective.
The Trust
Prof. W. Jethro Brown was appointed
on the resignation, through ill-health, of
John Gordon, chairman of the Australian
Sugar Commission—-“the most searching
investigation that has yet been made into
any Australian industry.” The result of
his experiences is the solidly and fairly
written The Prevention- . and Control of
Monopolies (Murray, 65.). Brown defines
a monopoly as existing where, “as a result
of agreement, consolidation or dominant
control, there is an absolute or virtual
abeyance of competition as a factor in the
determination of the price of a commodity
or service.” A trust is “any large consoli-
dated business which enjoys a monopolistic
control”; and a combine is “a union of
several more or less independent concerns
for the purpose of excluding or limiting com-
petition.”
The tendency toward the formation of
monopolies, trusts and combines is the out-
standing feature of modern capitalism.
While it may eliminate the wastes of com-
petition, it deprives the producer, the
employee and the consumer of the protection
which competition has afforded them in the
past. And now, what new safeguard can
be devised? This difficult problem leads
Brown to consider the syndicalist move-
ment, to which he attaches extreme import-
ance. He clearly shows the cardinal differ-
ences between syndicalism and socialism.
“The social unrest of our times arises not
so much from hunger as from thinking.”
And “neither the capitalist, as such, nor the
trade unionist, as such, is the enemy to
society. The enemy to society is the para-
site in the one class, and the revolutionary
extremist in the other... .Both are enemies
to the class to which they belong rather than
to the class to which they are opposed.”
The trust prefers to make its profits out
of high prices to consumers, rather than out
of low wages to employees. Consumers
grumble; but workmen go out on strike.
And between these alternatives the latter
is more dreaded in the policy of trust
finance.
Brown insists that there is no single
remedy against trusts. He mentions and
critically examines the following remedies :
Limitation of the size of corporate busi-
ness ; publicity; legislative regulation and
administrative supervision of competition;
limitation of profits, public regulation of
prices, public competition, and nationalism.
And he finds no one policy adequate to the
conditions which either exist to-day or are
likely to exist in the near future. For one
thing, the economic and political conditions
of various countries are divergent. There
is no easy solution to “the most difficult
problem of our time.” The author believes
that in most industries the maintenance of
competitive industry is theoretically desir-
able and practicable, though he recognises
that monopoly is the natural goal in some
industries. He sees nothing in available
data to justify the assumption that the
whole world of industry is destined to organ-
isation in the trust form: the greater
number of industries will continue to be
carried on by more or less independent con-
cerns. Especially in Australia, where the
increasing rate of wages, and the increasing
difficulty of maintaining the standard of
labor, have given a new encouragement in
certain industries to the small producer. In
these industries, the independent prbduccr,
by the labor of himself and his family,
supplemented it may be by the assistance
of hired labor, is more than able to hold his
own.
Brown does not hope much from the
policy of imposing an arbitrary limit upon
the size of corporate enterprise. But he
does believe in the policy of prohibiting
unfair competition. The big business
should not be attacked simply because it is
big. but it should be made to prove that
its bloatedness is necessary to its efficiency.
For that reason there must be publicity of
its methods. The trust must be compelled
to disclose its profits. For this purpose of
a just publicity the author finds a clear
necessity for a permanent commission to
supervise big business undertakings. Our
permanent tribunal is of little use: it has
so many other things to do. And the Inter-
State Commission should have the power off
giving judicial decisions upon the matters
involved in the definition of unfair competi-
tion, such decisions to be final as to facts.
But publicity has but a limited value. There
must be a policy of monopoly control which
will prevent abuses without imperilling the
standard of efficiency.
Public competition is ruled out as a
remedy for trusts; either we should
nationalise an industry or control it by
other means. Limitation of profits is also
ruled out, except as an integral part of the
policy of the public control of prices. As
regards nationalism, Brown finds certain
industries, dealing, e.g., with transport,
affecting the health of the community, or
the depletion of national resources, that
might be nationalised. But he regards the
nationalisation of monopolies as a policy of
limited application. It is chiefly by the
policy of the public control of prices that
Brown hopes to tame the octopus, assuming
first the adoption of publicity and the public
regulation of competition. If publicity and
public regulation do not render the trust
innocuous, then —nationalise it or control
its prices.
But it has just occurred to us, according
to the Liberal politician and the Liberal
press, there are no trusts in Australia. It
seems a pity that Prof. W. Jethro Brown
bothered.
The War
The Australian cartoonist blushingly
leaves the trenches of the press to attack
the public in the open, Two, Hal Eyre, of
The Daily Telegraph, and D. H. Souter,
of The Stock Journal, proffer collections
of their War Cartoons, price Is. each. Hal
Eyre’s stiff line and pernicketty detail spoil
many oof his bright ideas —and some of his
ideas are not bright. The abortion of a
lion’s head on a sailor’s body, typifying
Britain, is one of his mistakes in artistry,
while his too, too solid angel, with cotton-
wool wings and baggy trousers, can be
excused only by the hurry of daily journal-
ism. His ideas, such as his “Come Over
Here,” from Napoleon on St. Helena to
the Kaiser, and his cheerful Australian tar
putting a feather of the Emden in his cap,
are better than his execution. D. H. Souter’s
budget is a pleasant surprise, since none of
the combatant’s emblems is a black cat, and
he has little chance for filmy draperies or
pleasing feminine curves and the employment
of dress suits where a patch of black is
artistically necessary. Souter’s original ideas
are expressed with originality and a brilliant
craftsmanship. His picture of the Kaiser
choking the Belgian child has the necessary
malignancy; and he has put down, once for
all, the dollar-neutrality of Uncle Sam.
When Unready Sam is asked by armed
Britannia to give satisfactory guarantees re
the exportation of wool to the Neutrited
States, he objects with his usual, “Between
friends” Britannia sternly replies:
“This is business. Sign, please.” “The
Bogey Man” and “The Armed Heel” are
striking cartoons, worthy to rank with the
best of the German cartoons —than which,
alack, there can be no higher praise.
E. George Marks, of Sydney Sun, reprints
(with portrait) a first series of his articles
appearing in that publication, dealing with
Napoleon and the War (J. R. Tyrrell, Syd-
ney, 25.. 6d;). Quotations from The Aus-
tkalian Journalist inform us that the
author is a Napoleonic authority—so that’s
all right. In the preface we learn that
“from a number of Australians, occupying
distinguished positions in the Commonwealth
[names given] Mr: - " Marks received encour-
agement to publish this series” —so that’s
also all right. The articles are good jour-
nalism, aptly seizing upon the briefest of
cablegrams to introduce Napoleon’s head.
Fiction
John Mitchell’s wife, nee Louie Susan
Muix*, ran away from him, and John Mit-
chell pursued her over the globe. He failed
to find her, but does not yet despair. For
he has got “a well-known . author”
to write the true story of the Muir sisters,
with photographs of the originals, in the
guise of a novel. It is entitled Lives of
Romance: a True Novel, by John T. Fran-
cis (Mitchell, London, Is.). By means of
this publicity he hopes to discover the miss-
ing wife, and prosecute her for the recovery
of money and jewellery of his with which
he alleges she absconded. And he offers a
reward of £lOO for her apprehension. Aus-
tralasian readers should be interested in
this curious detective novel, for the parties
are all Australasians. John Mitchell was
in 1895 “one of the best-known _ business
agents in Melbourne,” and the Muir sisters
through him purchased the Otago Hotel in
Flinders-street. They were born at Caver-
sham, Dunedin. John married Louie, “a
fine, tall brunette with grey eyes, and a fine
figure.” According to the author, one of her
sisters married a Hindoo hawker, and
another a M.L. journalist, from whom she
got a divorce and married a well-known and
wealthy Australian station-owner (full
names and photographs supplied by the
obliging author). The married lives of the
sisters are fully described, and the allega-
tions made against the two women are •of
the frankest description. As fiction the book
is negligible; but it is noteworthy as the first
example in literature of an alleged “human
document” used as a detective agency.
Roy Bridges knows how to provide the
average novel of adventure with a local and
historical background. His heroine in The
Fugitive (Hodder and Stoughton) takes the
presumptive guilt of her lover upon her
shoulders, and is transported for murder to
Van Diemen’s Land. In the same ship the
officer in charge of the convicts happens to
be the villain who actually committed the
murder. . The wreck of the convict ship has
the right dash of the gruesome, and the sub-
sequent privations of hero and heroine are
credible enough; but the complication that
comes about owing to the hero being rescued
in a convict’s dress rather strains the reader’s
credulity. But Roy Bridges’ reader will not
mind. She wants adventure and romance,
and will not be disappointed. Bridges, though
his literary work is quite undistinguished,
is to be commended for the consistent use he
makes of our history—a mine of interest too
often regarded as not worth working by the
local author.
Constance Mackness is hereby admitted
to a front seat in the flapperature class. Her
Gem of the Flat (Angus and Robertson,
Sydney; 3s. 6d.) is a pleasant and natural
narrative of the comparatively backblocks, in
which the new school-teacher, the escaped
bushranger, the old gold-miner, and the vari-
ous children of the families at Needy Flat
are presented with an engaging appearance of
real life. The illustration of the novel by
actual photographs of the locality and cha-
racters is well carried out, and the idea is
infinitely preferable to the usual efforts of
the overseas artist to achieve something like
the local color of a place he has never seen.
Ourselves
11. M. Vaughan’s An Australasian
Wander-Year (Seeker, per Dymock’s
Library) is well illustrated and somewhat
entertaining. Vaughan has the usual plea-
sant things to say of Sydney’s ineqm-
parable climate, of surf-bathing, of Sydney’s
Botanical Gardens, and of The Bulletin —
our notorious modesty makes us reluctant to
quote the praise, the more so since there are
references to this little shrinking page, but
the criticism may be pardoned: “....The
facetious and slangy style of expression
which is affected by the regular members of
The Bulletin’s staff. For the journalese
of this weekly paper is like nothing save
itself, since the idioms are not borrowed
either from British or American speech, but
may be regarded as a peculiar brand of
Austral-English. The curious nomenclature,
e.g., ‘Bananaland,’ ‘Big Smoke,’ will cer-
tainly puzzle and irritate the reader in his
early attempts to appreciate The
Bulletin.” Rotorua is “undoubtedly one
of the most fascinating places on the globe” ;
Collins-street is “one of the stateliest streets
in the world, classed in the same category
with Fifth Avenue, the Thames Embank-
ment and the Champs Elysges. Port Jack-
son, however, is not, from the standpoint
of the artist, the most beautiful harbor. Its
shores are too low and too monotonous, and
the commercial development of Sydney has
not allowed much of its original appearance
to survive. Sydney architecture, especially
the incongruous massing of St. Andrew’s
Cathedral, the Town Hall and the Victoria
Markets, comes in for a just condemnation.
Of Sydney citizens, “roughly speaking, the
men are tall, thin and sunburnt; the women
short, plump and rather pallid.” (!)
Smallpox
Judging from the title, The History of
Small-pox in Australia, 1788-1908, compiled
by J. H. L. Cumpston, Commonwealth
Director of Quarantine, small-pox stopped
in Australia in 1908. Dr. Cumpston, how-
ever, explains the lack of information about
our last small-pox visitation. “It is not
unlikely that had access to the official records
in the various State Departments concerned
been possible, a greater amount of informa-
tion would have been obtained ; but the oppor-
tunity for access to official records was not
available, and, accordingly, the facts con-
tained in this history are largely confined to
those facts which have been made public.”
Well, why weren’t those State records avail-
able to the Commonwealth Director of
Quarantine? Is this continent a Common-
wealth or not? We need to know all the
latest facts about this disease; and all that
Dr. Cumpston can tell us, or is permitted to
tell us, dates back to 1908.
•
“V.”: Your examiner’s report upon Aus-
tralian literature of 1914 appears to present
certain openings to criticism on the biblio-
graphic side. If we may take the expres-
sion “were submitted for examination” as
equivalent to “were published,” the report is
faultsome in three ways:—
(1) Books published before 1914 receive
censure, e.g., The Beginnings of Government
and The Ballades.
(2) The number of books mentioned which
are of Australian origin, but printed abroad,
needs enlargement. Names like Wollaston,
Vaughan, Priestley, Newton, Green, Ren-
toul are conspicuous by absence.
(3) The list of Australian books is not
exhaustive. The works of the following
writers are fit to undergo the keen analysis
of your examiner: A. Siissjnilch (second ed.
of Introd. to the Geology of N.S.W.), G.
Chapman, Waterhouse and Lyell, Ernest
Henry Clark Oliphant, J. Smyth, E. Char-
lotte Onians, A. A. Holman.
[ (1) The annual report of Australian liter-
ature is compiled from the opiuions formed
of works reviewed upon the Red Page during
each year. Hitherto no attempt has been
made to confine the works reviewed strictly
to those actually published in each year.
To do so, in the case of overseas publica-
tions, which might not reach Australia till
February of the following year, would be to
delay the annual review too long. Roughly,
each year’s work is reviewed the following
year; and no injustice is suffered if the
basis of inclusion is that of the date of the
review in the Red Page. In the present in-
stance C. J. Brennan’s Poems are deliber-
ately held over for inclusion in the 1915
results. Both The Beginnings of Govern-
ment and The Ballades were thus held over
from the previous year.
(2) Only the more important books are
mentioned by name, but all the others that
were reviewed are considered. Names “con-
spicuous by absence” were either names not
regarded as worth notice or names of authors
whose books were not submitted for review
to this publication. If overseas publishers,
or Australian authors, do not desire the
criticism of this page by forwarding books
for review, they need not be hurt that that
criticism is not given. Nevertheless, efforts
are always made to get hold of such over-
seas publications for review.
(3) Publishers of Australian books who
do not submit their publications for review
stand in the same category. The names men-
tioned include some whose works were not
submitted for review, e.g. Waterhouse and
Lyell, and some whose work was considered,
and not. considered worthy of honorable
mention. Again, in this class, every effort
is made to obtain these works.—R.P.l

shoot a series of shots with this rifle with great rapidity.
This makes it particularly desirable for practicing rapid fire
shooting or shooting at moving game such as ducks, geese,
rabbits, etc. As a means of sport this rifle is not equalled
by any other twenty-two caliber. It handles inexpensive
rim fire cartridges, those of Winchester make being

of four-fifths of the charm that would otherwise be hers. . . .Are your

cellars” that form at your throat make evening dress almost a tragedy?
Are you so thin that your clothes merely hang on you?

lowing letter of her release from the thraldom of poor development
and deficient figure:—

VARIOUS VERS
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Moon Dream.
The moon was all freckled with cloud,
The pipes of the fairies were shrill,
The tree pirouetted and bowed
To the wind sweeping over the hill—-
It was good to be chasing a cloud
With the moon peeping over the hill.
The lake of the sky was so blue,
And the stars were like fish in a net.
Now, what' was night trying to do,
Painting all the world amber and jet?
The sky was a cloud-tufted blue,
And the earth was all amber and jet.
O girl, with the wind in your hair!
There’s a dream running wild in my heart.
The night has a moonbeam to spare
To tie it and hold it apart—
Oh. tie up my dream in your hair,
Lest the nlad thing should try to depart!
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Shannon’s Dream.
Now, this is the dream of Shannon
Told, sometimes, to beguile
The night hours. As we listen
We do not laugh the while.
For. grouped around the red logs,
Within their crimson gleam,
Each nods to each and mutters,
“Can this be all a dream?”
Says Shannon, smoking hugely,
His smoke rings wreathed above,
“There comes a time when all men
Must fend the thing they love.”
“I won her—held her lightly—
Yet, by my natal star,
I swear her eyes, her tresses,
Might stir a world to war!
“You, lad, and you, have seen her;
You know her fair and kind —
Think, then, how blind was Shannon
To think the whole world blind.
“Yet others saw and warned me—
Said one: ‘The woman’s fair;
Fair women win fine courtship.’
Another said : ‘Beware !’
“I laughed, smoked, gamed and sported,
And sang with glass agleam —
Till, somehow—how I know not —
I think I dreamt a dream.
“I stood in Champion’s stockyard
Upon a noon of day;
I stood in Champion’s stockyard,
And she was far away.
“I stood in Champion’s stockyard
(You know old Champion well),
The sun was hot, ’twas Summer,
And there the dream befell.
“The sun grew bleached and wizened,
The plains, vague spaces lay;
I saw her face in shadow,
And she was far away.
“The ice-cold grip of terror
Was tense aoooc my neart;
I felt the chill of peril
And we were wide apart!
“I rode from Champion’s stockyard—
No longer fatuous, blind —
Bent forward in my saddle,
My rifle slung behind.
“With wide eyes, set and staring,
Across the endless plains,
I rode through blazing Summer,
With Winter in my veins.
“I saw her face in shadow,
And down to lull 1 same.
I saw her face in shadow,
And red my rowels drank.
“Yet, ever as I galloped—
Mane clutched, and forward bent—
I pondered on the Shadow,
And what the Shadow meant.
“As one who rides death-harried,
My hurry never ceased;
The ' sun crept ever westward,
And I sped ever east.
“I swore to fight the Shadow,
To front it unafraid,
If speed of hoof could save her,
And strength of arm could aid.
“I threw one look' behind me.
The plains lay still and brown,
And red with sullen menace
The staring sun went down.
“Then fell a chill foreboding
Beneath the pallid skies —
The sun had sunk forever,
And never more would rise.
“The world had run its cycle,
Its day of loving light;
It, too, beneath the Shadow
Was doomed to Ever-Night!
“Then darkness—thick, appalling—
Rushed down upon the plain,
Yet ever on and onward
I rode with reefing rein.
“And then, this thing befell me;
Upon that awful night,
I saw myself a legion . , .
That stretched to left and right!
“I saw myself made many,
My features multiplied.
Ah God! ’Twas weird, uncanny,
That spectral, midnight ride.
“Ten thousand urgent riders,
They rode abreast with me,
In silence grim and earnest,
Knee nigh to touching knee.
“To free her from the Shadow —
Keen-eyed, no longer blind—
We sped, our heads thrust forward,
Our rifles slung behind.
“And then” —he pauses, musing;
We listen, open eyed—
“l woke in Champion’s stockyard,
And she was at my side.
“And that’s the dream,” says Shannon,
And stoops to get a light
Then straightens up, and rises
And peers into the night.
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 11, 1915.
THE SHORTEST WAY TO
TIPPERARY.
The longer the war lasts the clearer it
becomes that it is no cakewalk for the Allies.
Germany has not merely to be beaten on
points ; it must be knocked out and rendered
incapable of discommoding the world for
generations. How is it to be done, and
when? The Kaiser’s women-and-children-
butchers still occupy the richest part of
France. They so pervade Belgium that their
swollen-headed monarch has proclaimed it
a German province. They hold a part of
Boland and hold it hard. The Russian bear
plods gallantly on, but his plodding is ham-
pered by the railway system of the Father-
land. Positions which he arrives at after
weeks of battling through the most horrible
roads on earth are reached in as many hours
by the enemy, who meet them fresh and
well-fed, and so dash into action. The
Russian may weigh his carefully-prepared
foe down by sheer weight of numbers in
course of time, but it may be a very long
time. Before the Gerhog’s western frontier
is reached he will need to be belted out of
France and pushed off the face of Belgium.
Every traversable yard of these regions is
mined, trenched, fortified and in every other
respect known to human and diabolical in-
genuity rendered as nearly impassable as
possible. The atmosphere will have grown
tired of the sensation of having Zouaves
and Sikhs, and pion-pions, and British
Tommies blown through it long before an
Allied soldier enjoys his first wash in the
Rhine.
* * * *
And when the Rhine is reached and
forded, and its mighty fortifications reduced,
and the bloody march to Berlin accom-
plished ; when the Kaiser bolts distractedly
out of one end of the city as the cavalry of
Britain, or Australia, or Italy, or Rou-
mania, as the case may be, hurries in the
other end —what then? Germany will have
been beaten on points. But under Arma-
geddon rules only a knock-out counts. If
ever a nation was utterly flattened out
Belgium —with its army living precariously
in holes on the edge of its territory; with
its heroic king in a state of perpetual
motion; and with its Government trans-
ferred to a foreign country—is that nation.
And if it has any rival for the Affliction
Stakes Servia- —with its capital blown to
pieces its army more than decimated and
its non-combatants starving—is obviously
the one. Yet both are resisting as strongly
as ever What Germany hasn t been able
to do to a stone-broke, war-ravaged country
like Servia, and to an unprepared nation
like Belgium, the Allies are going to find
it hideously difficult to achieve in regard
to Germany.
* * * *
And what is this part of the Empire
doing in the matter? In the early stages
of the war some of its rich men and big
institutions subscribed mildly—very mildly
in many cases —to the Patriotic Funds.
There was a good deal of defiant singing,
also a flood of oratory concerning the Em-
pire and Australia’s obligations to the Flag.
Meanwhile the First Expeditionaries were
allowed to go away with horse-drawn ambu-
lances because there was no cash available
to buy motor-driven ones. The very rich
showed the readiest willingness to en-
courage their women-folk to knit socks arid
Balaclava helmets, but the canniest reluc-
tance to hand out subscriptions big enough
to inconvenience themselves in . the smallest
degree. Having written their trifling cheques
and stated their loyal sentiments in song, they
turned upon the local youth and accused
him of cowardice, and dereliction from duty
in not having come forward more eagerly
to join the colors. In point of fact the local
youth is all right. Thousands of him have
offered and thousands more are still offering
all that they have to give, and without a,ny
great persuasion either. With a few glaring
exceptions the best sort of young men are
doing all they can to hasten the end of the
war. The rotten patriotism is being evinced
by their seniors.
* * * *
Anyone can see it who troubles to look
below the surface of blither with which our
bogus patriots always cloud the real issues.
Take the question of the Tariff. The whole
of the importing classes are fighting strenu-
ously, in, the name of Empire, against any
change in the direction of higher duties.
They want the Commonwealth to persist
in the pernicious old business, of indenting
cheap foreign goods and paying for them
with borrowed money and raw products.
The importer who is allowed to go on in-
definitely dragging goods built by . nigger
or other sweated labor over a low tariff wall
and selling them at white men’s prices, is
obviously in a sound position to give freely
to Patriotic Funds. But he is not doing any-
thing for patriotism. The Germans have a
saying that war may be made into a busi-
ness. They are making a business of it
now. Never, it is declared, was there less
unemployment in Germany. All available
hands are hard at work producing some-
thing or other. It is the same in the
Allied countries which are in a position. to
continue manufacture. The articles which
are being specialised in are, of course, guns,
clothing for the troops, foodstuffs, medical
appliances, machinery and the innumerable
requirements of armies in the field. One
of the aims of the German generals on their
eastern frontier is understood to be to ex-
haust Russia’s supply of arms and ammu-
nition. Britain and France have the duty
of seeing that this .does not happen. Servia
and Belgium are, in the nature of things,
hard up. London and Paris will have to
find cash and munitions and food for them.
Turn from that to consider the position, of
the Commonwealth in this crisis. Owing
to a fiscal policy arranged largely with an
eye to the importing interests, Australia is
making nothing worth mentioning that is of
use in warfare. It is giving no help of this
sort to Britain. It is true that Australia
has clothing factories —and true, likewise,
that only a few days ago, a number of them
refused to tender to supply the troops with
uniforms because of the insertion of a
“walking delegate” clause in the Govern-
ment contracts. Australia also makes boots
on a small scale, and assembles .the parts
of warships, and does a little in the way
of saddle and harness-making. But what
it should be doing, and what it would be
doing if it had not listened to. the voice of
the importer, includes the building of its
own big guns, its own warships and mer-
chant vessels, its own motoi’-cars, lorries
and ambulances, and every detail of. army
equipment; and by so much removing a
burden from the heavily-taxed shoulders of
Britain.
* * * *
There are two schools of thought in the
Commonwealth at the present time. One of
them is accused, by the same people who
used to talk of the Australian navy project
as a “madman’s dream.” of an outlook which
is wild, experimental, unpractical and vision-
ary. This school says that the war is likely
to las-t for years; that, in the circumstances,
it is poor patriotism to continue draining
Britain of munitions and money; and that
the better way t is to start to make a business
of war by constructing field guns, ships and
all the other service necessaries which it has
been accustomed to import. The other school
gives three cheers for the Empire, addresses
a recruiting meeting, sings the National
Anthem, says Germany will be beaten to its
knees and eating out of the hand by August,
and asks that everything shall continue as
before. These people don’t want to see
factories arise if the price of them is a tariff
which will interfere with the indent trade,
and they don’t want London borrowing to
stop, since that may result in a slump in their
line of business. They want victory without
financial tears, the swift ending of the war
by the complete effacement of the greatest
military power in the world and no damage
done to their shops and warehouses. It is
not to be managed. The shortest route to
Tipperary, so far as we have the building of
it, is a causeway strewn with heroic sacri-
fices not only of men, but of other things.
The Commonwealth has poled on the har-
assed old Motherland for too long. If its
rich men want to do service as valuable as
that which the Expeditionaries are offering,
they will get to work, and by every possible
means organise and build up all the local
industries that -the country can hold. And
thev won’t waste a needless minute over it
either.
THE PROBLEM OF NICHOLAS
MUTTON AND OTHERS.
Nicholas Mutton, aided by, or connected
with. Drew and Maloney, and indirectly
mixed up with, or abetted, or alleged to have
been in the company of, or found sleeping in
the same paddock as the N.S.W. Government,
has made a little bit of history or mystery.
Probably the thing is mystery. It is mixed
mystery, and hard to decipher, but it is
interesting so far as it goes.
Some time back the Ministry of All the
Talents rushed into the breach, or what it
thought was the same thing, but probably
wasn’t; and annexed, or professed to annex,
all the wheat in the State at a fixed price
of ss. per bushel. There being abundance
of wheat in the State for local requirements
this seemed rather like the hurried action
of a crowd which must keep itself before the
public eye or burst. It recalls the position
of a certain “buck” in the days of George
IV. who owed his social importance solely
to the fact that he could get round a large
and fashionable drawing-room by jumping
from one chair to another, and without once
touching the floor, though he occasionally
leaped on the mantel-piece. He wasn’t use-
ful, but he was unusual. The wheat thus
annexed was mostly re-sold to. Private
Enterprise in the shape of the miller, and
as he was under no restrictions in the
matter of price the poor old loaf showed
no visible diminution in the figure charged.
A considerable row was made over the fact
that three very rich Private Enterprise
city firms got the sole right to buy wheat
for the Government. It was alleged (1)
that the commission paid to them was ab-
surd and enormous, and unprecedented;
(2) that having a monopoly of the business
they felt no need to be even reasonably
punctual; (3) that their appointment
wiped out the business of a lot of equally
deserving country firms; (4) that the whole
affair was unpleasant. Then there was . a
row because existing contracts with Vic-
toria (which represented border trade) were
cancelled, while the State Government
gladly entered into new arrangements, for
which there were no contracts, to supply
Tasmania and Maoriland (this, of course,
represented Sydney trade). Certain people
were wild: (1) The individual who had
undertaken to buy wheat at a certain price
and to deliver flour at a certain price, and
who found his supply of wheat cut off while
his liability to hand over the flour remained ;
(2) the man who had contracted to sell
wheat in Victoria and found his N.S.W.
supply cut off while Ms Victorian liability
remained; (3) the farmer who hadn’t sold
his wheat and who had to take ss. per
bushel, payable whenever the agent had
time to come round, whereas he had en-
joyed golden visions of Gs. Gd. And one
man was glad—the speculator who had con-
tracted, in the early pa'rt of the season,
when the prospects looked good, to deliver
wheat at 4s. or 4s. Gd., and who had the
pleasure of seeing his disastrous bargain
declared null and void.
* * * *
Somewhere here come in Maloney and
Drew and Nicholas Mutton. The story
propounded is that they were on the wrong
side of the market, or represented people
who were on the wrong side of the market.
The anecdote goes that it would have been
worth 2d. or a heap of money to them or
the people they stood for, if the Govern-
ment , cancelled all existing contracts for
the sale of wheat in N.S.W., otherwise they
would have to buy at a high war-and-
drought price and sell at a low figure. The
narrative runs or straggles that money was
put up handsomely by certain people, in-
cluding Nicholas Mutton, or folks repre-
sented by him, and was handed to Drew,
and Drew was supposed to peg in vigor-
ously, and to render no accounts. Also that
the existing contracts were cancelled. Also
that Nicholas Mutton was quite satisfied.
Also that Maloney didn’t say that any-
thing was wrong. Also that Drew
didn’t become wild and fly off the
handle. Still, there has arisen a
question as to what, exactly, Drew did to
earn the money and applause of Maloney
and Nicholas Mutton, assuming that they
applauded as much as alleged, _ and that
they were as deeply imbedded in an im-
poverished speculation as alleged. A vague
report is ' circulated that somebody who
might be neither Mutton nor Maloney,
thought Drew —if there is any such person
as Drew —could bring influence to bear on
Somebody. But the one individual whom
Nobody can find at short notice is Some-
body.
POTSDAM’S CALL ON THE SPHINX.
The German commander who is invading
Egypt and giving the Australian expedi-
tionaries some pleasurable expectations, has
before him one of the big jobs of history.
The British armies there stand behind 130
miles of desert, parts of which have been
flooded by the sea. They are also shielded
by the Suez Canal, which is broad and deep,
and can be used for concentrating the
heaviest artillery on any required point at
a speed of 10 miles an hour. Lighter guns
or smaller ships can be concentrated -at no
less than 15 miles an hour. / ' r The British
have an unlimited supply of- fresh water
from the Fresh Water Canal, arid a railway
system, running the entire length of the
canal, which can be used for' concentrating
any but the heaviest artillery at 50 miles
an hour. Deb Goltz, operating from the
desert side, has to first beat down the defend-
ing forces, bridge the canal, and seize the
Fresh Water Canal. Such part of the raiW
way as he would in these
acquire would be of no use to him beeausb--
he would have no railhead. He could-
not * approach either Port Said or Suez
because of the warships. Neither would
be of use to him in any event. He cannot
blow up the canal, because by so doing he
would destroy his own mobility. If he
leaves it intact war vessels may cut his
lines.
* * * *
The army which he leads is called Turkish,
but SO per cent, of it is Arab. Such as it is,
it is now mostly in the neighborhood _ of
Lake Tiberias, which is roughly 300 miles
from the canal. As the troops advance a
light railway has to be built to supply them
with water. If that line is cut by the
British, the invaders are lost. The protect-
ing artillery is limited to mountain batteries,
which are camel-borne. These guns should
have been supplemented by ' better ones
brought to Haifa on a fugitive German
steamer, but the latter was taken by the
British destroyer Racoon just outside the
port. In matters of this sort the British
fleet is most inconsiderate.
* * * *
The loyalty of the army is confused. The
Turks reciprocate the hatred of the Arab
elements. To conciliate the Syrians dek
Goltz re-appointed a number of Arab offi-
cers who had been cashiered by the Turks.
He also appointed an Arab as commander
in Syria—an unheard-of proceeding which
has bitterly antagonised the Turks. Then
he destroyed the conciliatory effect of his
earlier action by compelling every Arab in-
habitant to supply the army with, a sack
and a tin can. The can and sack constitute
the total transport. A bridge for the canal
has been built in sections, and was last
heard of definitely at Lake Tiberias. To
reach the canal it will have to be hauled
through deserts of sand, under a cascade of
shells, by an army balancing a can of water
on its head. Meanwhile a certain amount
of disaffection admittedly prevails because
the German language has been made com-
pulsory in the Syrian schools, and many of
the coastal inhabitants are begging the
allies to come and occupy the coastline,
where the Yalis have renounced allegiance
to the Porte. On top of all this there is
news of Britain’s acceptance of Roumania’s
Treasury Bills, which suggests that Rou-
mania. is about to enter the war. If so the
road between Germany and Turkey will be
closed. This will cut off Von pER Goltz
and leave him alone in the dark with Turkey.
And Turkey isn’t a pleasant person to be
left in the dark with.
* * * *
In short, Von der Goltz seems up against
it. The cable mentioned last week that he
had been shot. That seems to be untrue.
The fact probably is that he merely wishes
he had been.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Fetich of Property.
A contributor recently told The Rulletin
that the High Court decision in Eather’s
case is “one of the most momentous since
the creation of that tribunal,” and he was
right. For the Court therein definitely
refused to take part in the administration of
the criminal law of the
attitude represented one more effort to “play
the sedulous ape” to the Privy 'Council —a
body with which it has nothing ijU common.
The Privy Council defined its attitude
towards criminal appeals in Arnold’s case.
It said in effect that, because the systems of
criminal administration obtaining among the
hundreds of millions of people of all races
and colors whose affairs it governed were so
infinitely various, it would refuse to inter-
fere with local tribunals unless an obvious
miscarriage of justice had occurred.
Arnold’s case —one in which the prosecutor
had also acted as judge, at a trial in one of
Britain’s Asiatic possessions—was indicated
as the type of such a case. The High Court
now applies this principle to Australia,
though Australia’s various criminal systems,
subject to trifling technical variations, are
identical. In Eather’S case a point of law
was involved which was as pre-eminently
fitted for the consideration of the High
Court as any that has yet come before it,
inasmuch as it was difficult, novel and
important. The High Court has delivered
profound and considered judgments in a
dispute about the question of property in a
two-headed baby, and 'it has likewise gone
exhaustively into the rights of the owner
of a slot machine in relation to the observ-
ance of the Sabbath. But these were civil
cases. Eather’s was only a criminal case.
And as the Chief Justice frequently
observed: “If we are to entertain appeals
in this sort of way, we will become a Court
of Appeal from every criminal court in the
Commonwealth.” Mere taxpayers, who find
these Judges their salary, will, of course,
answer “Why not?” They forget. the
instinctive disdain of the high-claSs lawyer
for criminal work. They overlook the fact
that his mind is congenitally bent towards
commercial cases, and that the coarse and
muddy boots of the burglar and the. burglar’s
unsightly attorney are hateful to him. Now,
this is the lawyer’s own affair while he is
still in private practice. Once he goes on
to the Bench, though, he loses this right to
disdain. The community is as much entitled
then to exact his construction of its criminal
statutes as it is of its civil ones. Above
all in Australia is it necessary that a really
sound tribunal should be cheaply and readily
available for every purpose. The com-
mixture of good, bad, indifferent
and even rotten law which steadily flows
from the Supreme Court Benches of the
various States needs constant and careful
supervision by thoroughly capable men. In
this very case of Eather’s, Justice Isaacs,
Who dissented, said there had been a misin-
terpretation of the law by the lower Courts
so grave as to lift the case far beyond the
regions of mere legal error. The High Court
excuses itself from dealing with so
unpleasant a matter by pointing out that
ic has power to make “exceptions” and
“regulations” for its own governance.. But
such a question as that of imposing limita-
tions on the right of Criminal Appeal is not
to be decided so cavalierly,*, One way of
settling the matter acceptably is for the
Attorney-General to see to it that the terms
of the "Court’s limitation, if any, are fixed
with precision by the Federal Parliament.
Another apd perhaps better way is for the
High Court to give up being so. naughty,
and to attend to one of the jobs it is paid
to attend to.
A Rational Fighting Policy.
Dear Bulletin, —Mr. Ernst LissAuer has
written sa “Hymn of Hate” expressive or
German feelings towards the British
Empire. It is being taught in the schools
of the intensely teachable Fatherland ; it is
chanted and declaimed at places of entei-
tainment; the insane Butcher has even con-
ferred the Order of the Red Eagle upon the
author. And still the Englanders whom the
impressionable Germans are told to hate
evermore are treating the great na 10 <
movement as a joke, even as they treated
German aspirations for world c 2 nc * „
before the war began. When a German
warship is sunk in a naval engagemen


Englanders assist the struggling crew to
escape drowning, whilst German aviators
drop bombs on the damp strugglers in the
hope that they are English. Also German
gunners fire upon the men in the water, not
caring how many of their own countrymen
they kill if they can perchance pot a rescue
party. Good ! This is war of the German
brand —War of Hate. A rescued German
naval officer is welcomed as a prisoner
aboard a British ship—and he spits in the
welcomer’s face. Good! He is entitled to
his Hate. But what are the British doing
towards equalising matters in a non-bar-
baric way? Nothing! Here in Australia
we are taught that it is wrong to suspect
resident Germans of the hate which the
Fatherland Government is teaching in its
schools. Unfair to suspect them of
treacherous tendencies towards the country
which was included in the German scheme
of \Vorld conquest; wrong to hold them
responsible for their gory national ideals, as
though national ideals were mere Govern-
ment creations and German socialism were
a juicy cocoanut full of the milk of human
kindness. Here in Melbourne we have
truculent Germans who, being nominally
“interned,” are released on parole when they
profess love for Australia, and are left to
earn salaries in public institutions. Even
the gas co. employee who calls at my house
every month is a German who cannot con-
ceal his nationality. I don’t return him
hate for hate, but when an inoffensive Aus-
tralian comes on the other’s heels with a
tale of unemployment—well, I wish he had
the German fellow’s billet. A reasonable
answer to Mr. Lissauer’s “Hymn of Hate.”
so dear to the. Kaiser and his people, would
be the expulsion of all unquestionable and
questionable Germans from British coun-
tries where they have prospered in the belief
that they were the advance guards of a
coming German conquest. They shouldn’t
have the tendons of their trigger finger cut,
or be bombed, or spat at, or otherwise in-
sulted. They should be given a cold, formal
acknowledgment of the justice of their
national ideals, and then be shipped off to
Germany to fight for their Hate, if they
want to. This is a life and death struggle
between German and British national ideals,
and every able-bodied man deserves a fair
chance to fight for his Cause. The Germans
living in enemy countries, like the British
similarly situated, are denied a fair chance.
They all —German and British —should be
exported to their respective Fatherland or
Motherland, free of charge. It will come to
this, probably before the war is a year older.
Why delay it? —Yours, E. F.
The Triumphs of Mr. Smith.
Bruce Smith has written another 10-ton
treatise to prove that the whole Labor policy
is wrong: in particular, that Public Enter-
prise and the White Australia doctrine are
the Devil, or possibly two devils. The
tremendous Mr. Smith is, however, always
too vague. Instead of thundering at large
against Public or Collective Enterprise, why
not be practical and show the unfailing mag-
nificent results of the only other sort of
Enterprise there is? For instance, the fruit
and vegetable business of Bruce Smith’s
own Sydney—what more perfect illustration
could there be than that? Within 30 miles
of Sydney hundreds of tons of peaches lie
rotting under the trees. Scores of orchards,
having ruined the orchardist, have been
abandoned. Those who continue at the
business make a beggarly'living at it. Yet
in Sydney Bruce Smith can’t get a dozen
decent peaches in a bag for less than a shil-
ling; and other fruit is even dearer, unless
Bruce takes his chance at a surreptitious
barrow. Hundreds of tons of it rotting
within 30 miles of Sydney, and in Sydney
itself the stuff regarded not as a food but as
a luxury ! So much for the superior quali-
ties of Private Enterprise. Admittedly, Pub-
lic or Collective Enterprise may often make
a mess of things. But, all the same, it is
the only sort of enterprise that even Bruce
Smith, consistent individualist though he
thinks himself, is prepared to trust in times
of crisis. How, for instance, would he
feel about it if, Bill of Berlin having
broken loose, word went out in England that
Belgium and France wanted help, and excur-
sion boats would carry across anybody who
had a gun and cared to use it —fare, 17s. 6d. ?
How would Bruce Smith feel about it if
Private Enterprise had had the job of protect-
ing the Australian capitals and Australian
shipping from the marauding German cruisers
that were recently in these waters? As for
the sinfulness of the White Australia policy,
let the Northern Territory be Bruce
Smith’s answer. It is the one part of the
continent where the white settler has been
able to command almost whatever tint in
labor he pleased. It is the one great area
to whose mines cheap Chinese have
been freely admitted. And it is the one
part of Australia where mining is most
thoroughly dead. In other States, if there
have been movements away from certain
country districts, there at least has been a'
growth of population in the aggregate. Only
the Northern Territory, blessed with Bruce
Smith’s discolored brother, has steadily and
persistently slipped back. On the other
hand, in the Queensland sugar districts
the disappearance of Tommy Tanna, the
Kanaka, has been followed by a greater pros-
perity than ever the industry before experi-
enced. In fact, it was more the prosperity
of that industry than anything else which
enabled the Government the other day, when
the Imperial loan was being cut up, to say
that it could do without any of it, thank
you. How is it that Bruce Smith can’t
say the same thing about the Northern Terri-
tory, which has had all the blessings that
he thunders about so tremendously?

Under the Fat Thumb of Cohen.
The event which was long. foretold looks
like a. real happening at last. Australia
has a monstrous debt, and the great bulk of
its loans fall due on fixed dates. Only one
State (Westralia) makes even a plausible
pretence of providing a sinking fund for
repayment, and its pretence is poor and
inadequate. In a general way the great
Australian IOU really reads as follows: —
The State ofi Blank acknowledges the receipt of
£10,000,000 from Abraham Bull-Cohen. This
amount is repayable on January 1 in the year Blank.
Repayment depends entirely on the State’s luck in
borrowing £10;000,000 from Abraham Bull-Cohen
to repay him with. If the aforesaid creditor really
wants his money he can’t have it, but if he doesn’t
want it he can get it.
The first loan of size—at least it tVas only
£2,000,000—which fell due after -the war
strain began to be badly felt in Europe has
just been renewed for five years at a figure
which, including expenses and discount,
works out at 5 per cent, per annum. The
old interest rate was 4 per cent. Thus the
burden of the original £2,000,000 loan
becomes equal to £2,500,000, which is, a big
jump to make in a single day. And when
Queensland comes along next July, by which
time things will probably be a great deal
worse, aqd' announces that it must have
£11,700,000 at any cost or else burst, the
response may easily be : “Pay 71 per cent,
or else burst.” There are exciting possi-
bilities ahead. In fact, the real joy may be
only commencing.

ANOTHER GAMBLING RAID.
' HEBREW PASTIME DISTURBED.
At the Police Court yesterday morning, before
Messrs. Sozzletap and Bdmblefoot, J’s.P., 24
wealthy bookmakers, pawnbrokers, bankers, agents
and squatters were each fined £lO and costs, with
the alternative of three months’ imprisonment, for
having been found in a house occupied (nominally)
by Theodore Gilt edge, and used as a common
gaming-house. Inspector Bullant stated that gam-
bling was greatly on the increase in Sydney, especi-
ally at some of the night-and-day clubs. At 10 p.m.
on Saturday, from information received, he (wit-
ness), with Sub-Inspector ButtersnatchEß, Sergeants
Blue, Green and Black, Senior-Constables Hard-
Case, Swizzle, Jamtin and Freebeer, and 13 plain-
clothes men, raided the premises in question. One
of the accused, Giltedge, tried to prevent the police
from entering, but he was promptly trodden on, and
a rush made for the smoking-room of the house,
which was known as the Pure Merino Club. The
defendants were found seated at various little tables,
playing poker, bridge and ordinary whist; and money
and card-scorers and whisky and soda were found all
over the place. He (witness) had ascertained from
various statements made by some of the accused that
these deplorable and moral-wrecking games were
being played for sovereigns—in the case of the
poker-play he believed that the stakes were very
much higher. As the Bench well knew, the wives
and families of these men often went short of a
motor-car and a grand piano in consequence of this
gaming, and the police were determined to put it
down. (Applause in court, which was immediately
suppressed.) The amount of money lying about the
tables and seized by the police totalled £478 10s.,
while on searching the accused at the police station
the sum of £3492 11s. sd. was found upon them —
mostlv in notes. Every one of the accused also
carried a cheque-book, which, from, the police point
of view, was an extraordinary thing, but he had been
informed by accused Giltedge, who called himself
the secretary of the club, that it was the usual
thing. Giltedge had added voluntarily that nobody
could become a member of the Pure Merino Club
unless he carried one. He (Inspector Bullant)
would ask the Bench to inflict a heavy penalty as a
warning to others. Mr. Mutton, K.C., and Mr.
Wooltop, who appeared for the accused, urged the
Bench to dismiss the , case, at it would create a
very bad impression. So long as the police confined
their unwelcome attention to the Chinese and their
horrible fantan habits —a game at which many poor
men lost as much as 7fd. at a sitting—nobody would
complain. But if this sort of thing was to be
allowed where would people be? Where would it
end ? The accused made no secret of their pastime,
and the club door was open to the whole street,
though, of course, porters, stewards and messengers
were there to prevent any unseemliness. The games
were simply pastimes, and were carried on strictly
among, and by, the members themselves. No police-
constable had ever been permitted to lose or win a
penny in the club. Inspector Bullant insisted on a
conviction, and the Bench therefore decided to con-
vict.
Theodore Giltedge. was then charged with having
kept a common gaming-house, but the charge was
withdrawn on payment of costs, as the accused was
among the 24 persons who had been found on the
premises. As they were leaving the court some of
the defendants were loud in their protests at being
“treated as if they were dirt, or .Chinese.” One pro-
minent member of the club remarked several times
that the country was going to the D —, a statement
that received general assent. The Bench compli-
mented Inspector Bullant and his officers and men
on their action, and added that their bravery in
bearding such desperate criminals in their den of
vice would be brought to the notice of the proper
authorities. After a suitable acknowledgment from
Inspector Bullant, who said that the police would
always do their duty, the proceedings—which had
created some stir—ended.

A Victorian Railway Freak.
“0.K.” on “the maddest thing in Victorian
Railways”:—
The Peacock Government, whilst spending a lot of
money on railways, carefully ignores the scandal of
the old Fern Tree Gully line, and the more recent
and ridiculous narrow gauge to Gembrook. Travel-
ling to the Gully and the fertile district beyond it is
a maddening experience five days of the week, and
even on excursion days. The one train that leaves
Melbourne at 8.50 gets to the Gully (19 miles) at
10.30 or later. At the Gully, which is a popular
summer resort, passengers for places as far as Gem-
brook transfer themselves to toy carriages built for
the narrow winding line which was cut along the
hillsides some 15 years ago. It could have gone
straight to Gembrook, instead of winding like a
wounded worm ; but this would have cost an extra-
twopence 15 years ago, so the ridiculous toy train
continues on the serpentine route to this day. All
the way from Fern Tree Gully to Upwey anew high-
way is being hewn out of the hill-slopes. This road
runs a good few yards inside the boundary fence of
the man who owns all the hills thereabouts. His un-
improved propputy (there may be more than one
hill-owner, but the fattest one will stand for the lot
in this sermon) extends nearly to the railway line,
and for years and years he has been holding these
expanses of eligible residential sites in one of the
loveliest and most fertile parts within 25 miles of
Melbourne —-holding them for a steady rise in value.
After the railway was made the hillsides still needed
a good, wide road to make them very valuable; so
the local shire authorities, or whoever runs the busi-
ness of road-making, arrived and compensated the
land-owner handsomely for invading his propputy in
order to make it valuable. This is a digression from
the scandal of the Melbourne to Fern Tree Gully rail-
way service, but it is not out of the picture. Did
I say how long the Gembrook liners take to return
to town at night? Well, they wind their weary way
to the Gully with long stoppages at the roadside, and
when they get there they find a train for town await-
ing them. There never was and more patient train.
It goes on waiting for another 30 to 40 minutes.
From Upwey to Melbourne is 23 miles, and you can
never do it in less than 130 minutes. And the fare
is subject to variation. A frequent week-end visitor
who had been paying the wild, uncertain fare at a
railway pigeon-hole went to the Tourist Bureau to
see if he could come to a definite understanding with
the Department, and then he learned that, so far as
the Tourist Bureau is concerned, the Gembr.ook line
does not exist. He could buy a tourist tioket for any
station on any and every country line in Victoria
except the one that most tourists want to take 1
Five Thousand a Year.

“Aristide” wants to start a revolution:—
If we must have State Governors (and the Labor
party seems as bound to the system as the Liberals),
why not get the best available goods for our money,
instead of social nonentities, party hacks or indivi-
duals who have a pull in Downing-street ?
We must start on the assumption that the official
has to be imported. For our £SOOO we could get
some of the very best men at the London Bar. The
popular impression' about their' enormous incomes is
wrong. Hilaire Belloc speaks of “that brilliant
young barrister commonly said to be making over
£II,OOO a year, and who actually is making
£3000.” Belloc knew what he was talking about.
At the English Common Law Bar there are only about
10 Silks who are making solid, reliable incomes. These
are men such as Carson, Finlat, Simon and a half-
dozen others. Precarious as is the position of all
barristers, that of the Silk is the most precarious.
Many brilliant and thriving juniors, drawn by the
lure of the front bench, take Silk in the hope of
ousting one of the small band which holds the work.
The project fails, and the tragedy of the briefless
junior is intensified in the tragedy of the briefless
Silk. Yet these are men of brilliant capacity. We
could get them.
Or, if we liked it better, a man of letters 1 What
an episode in the picturesque had George Meredith
ended his days amongst us as a Governor 1 And he
might have done so had we ever had the sense or
the imagination to will it. Suppose that to-day one
of the six States had Chesterton, or Belloc, or
Max Beerboiim. The presence of one of these men
would fix the eyes of the world upon us, and that
is what we want. At present we pay a great wage
to an obscurity. Grant that we get our foundation-
stones laid with unerring accuracy. Assume that it
would be utterly beyond the -capacity of Max Beer-
boiim to open a blind asylum. He could depute one
of his A.D.C.’s, a secretary, or even the butler, and
any one of them would do it as well as it is done
at present. Probably, indeed, the butler would do
it with a profounder touch of the grand manner
than anyone.
In the matter of artists the range would be very
wide. The £SOOO would be simply rushed by almost
any living painter. Probably the unmarried and
superfluously wealthy Sargent is the only one in the
world who would refuse it. If such a man were
wisely chosen we should indeed have one who might
be of use to us, and who would help to replace the
horrors which infest our picture galleries with worthy
things.
The Australian States want to be advertised' over-
seas. The present quality of State Governor doesn’t
advertise either Australia or the States. He is about
as conspicuous as a wombat. The men suggested,
even if they proved useless socially, would be as
conspicuous as lighthouses.

At an “informal private conference” with
Labor representatives in Wellington (M.L.),
Andrew Fisher said that “the democratic
platform must come from the people—not
from any political leader. The people must
pot leave their programmes to be settled by
the leaders, however clever. The people’s
proper political basis of action must be a
platform agreed to by a duly accredited and
representative conference of all Labor or-
ganisations.” Just such a “representative
conference” in Australia agreed upon “the
restriction of public borrowing” and the
provision of sinking funds for the purpose
of paying off the loans. Yet Andrew
Fisher poured contempt upon the Labor
platform, so far as it has reference to public
borrowing and sinking funds, and declared
that “sinking funds for permanent and re-
productive loans are a great absurdity in a
new country.” Furthermore, he had
“always considered” such funds to be
“greatly absurd.” What is the good of
calling conferences for the formation of
platforms, and what is the good of Andrew
Fisher advising his hearers to have no-
thing to do with programmes put forward
by leaders, when he himself has no hesita-
tion in pouring scorn upon the very pro-
posals and demands that have been made by
such conferences, and which he has been
put in power to abide by loyally? It is
time Andrew Fisher “cleared his mind of
cant,” and ceased using conventional Labor-
istic phrases. The cant of the professional
Laborist is becoming even more nauseously
insincere than that of the conventional
wowser. Indeed, the cant phrases of the
wowser are not always insincere; some-
times they are uttered with the most com-
plete and fiery earnestness. This does not
seem to be the case with a Fisher when he
utters the shibboleths of Laborism; for he
tells us—now that by uttering them he has
reached a position of power and affluence —
that he has always disbelieved in some of
them, and those, as it happens, the soundest
and most admirable.

The garbage question of Sydney is said to be
growing more intricate, though why it
should be intricate the Lord only knows.
The trains come down to Sydney carrying
multitudinous tons of wheat, hay, straw,
wool, sheep, bullocks and sundries. They go
back carrying a few cases of imported
drapery, some tinned goods, a boxer hat,
sundry boots, and a letter saying that the
interest on the mortgage is overdue. Obvi-
ously these return trains ought to carry
garbage. With it they could fertilise and
elevate the plains, and by elevating the
plains they could improve the rainfall. Then
the interior would flourish like the shade-
less gum-tree, and grow more hay, and send
it to Sydney, and get back more garbage
as a consequence, and so the thing would go
round in a beneficial circle. The present
idea is to flatten out and impoverish the
country, in order to make refuse with which
to fill up the sea, so that agriculture is
ruined on one side and navigation is
damaged on the other. And in between the
two the pretty girl who goes surf-bathing
finds dead cats and ruins bumping up
against her legs—very shapely legs as a rule.

Months ago, when it was known that Aus-
tralian wheat would be short, and that Vic-
toria would be the chief sufferer, there was
still a chance of getting the grain at a mod-
erate price. But the Peacock Govern-
ment dallied, and the trade either couldn’t
or wouldn’t bog in and provide for the
future. Last month it at last dawned on
somebody that if Victoria didn’t get wheat
from somewhere before June millers would
have to shut down, and there wouldn’t be
any bread for the public, either at lOd. or
at 10s. the 41b. loaf. Then Private Enter-
prise definitely gave up the job and the
Government fossicked round and acquired
about half a million bushels from America
to go on with; and about twice that quan-
tity then remained to be _ secured. The
price is kept secret, but it is safe to sal’ it
won't be less than 75., and may easily be
a good deal more, which is at least Is. above
what it could have been bought for when the
scarcity was first ascertained. Private
Enterprise, however, failed once more in a
crisis, while the inactivity of the Peacock
Government will cost the State a lot of
money. Among other things it will cer-
tainly reduce the area under crop next sea-
son, the nrice of seed wheat being now so
outrageous.
The Captains.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Captains sailed from all the World —-
from all the world and Spain;
And each one for his country’s ease, her glory
and her gain;
The Captains sailed to Southern Seas, and
sailed the Spanish Main;
And some sailed out beyond the World, and
some sailed home again.
And each one for his daily bread, and bitter
bread it was,
Because of things they’d left at home —or
for some other cause.
Their wives and daughters made the lace to
deck the Lady’s gown,
Where sailors’ wives sew dungarees by many
a seaport town.
The Captains sailed in rotten ships, with
often rotten crews,
Because their lands were ignorant and
meaner than the ooze ;
With money furnished them by Greed, or by
ambition mean,
When, they had crawled to some pig-faced,
pig-liearted king or queen.
And when a storm was on the coast, and
spray leaped o’er the quays,
Then little Joan or Dorothy, or Inez or
Louise,
Would kneel her down on such a night beside
her mother’s knees,
And fold her little hands and pray for those
beyond the seas.
With the touching faith of little girls—the
faith by love embalmed —
They’d pray for men beyond the seas who
might have been becalmed.
For some will pray at Christ His feet, and
some at Mary’s shrine;
And some to Heathen goddesses, as I have
prayed to mine;
To Mecca or to Bethlehem, to Fire, or Joss,
or Sol,
And one will pray to sticks or stones, and
one to her rag doll.
But ice are stubborn men and vain, and
though we rise or fall,
Our children's prayers or women's prayers,
God knows we need them all!
And no one fights the bitter gale, or strives
in combat grim,
But, somewhere in the world, a child is
praying hard for him.
The Captains sailed to India, to China and
Japan.
They met the Strangers’ Welcome and the
Friendliness of Sian ;
The Captains sailed to Southern Seas, and
“wondrous sights” they saw—
The Rights of Man in savage lands, and law
without a law.
They learnt the truth from savages, and
wisdom from the wild,
And learned to walk in unknown ways, and
trust them like a child.
(The sailors told of monstrous things that
be where sailors roam....
But none had seen more monstrous things
then they had seen at home.)
They found new worlds for crowded folk
in cities old and worn,
And huts of hunger, fog and smoke in lands
by Faction torn.
(They found the great and empty lands
where Nations might be born.)
They found new foods, they found new
wealth, and newer ways to live,
Where sons might grow in strength and
health, with all that God would give., ; >
They tracked their ways through unknown
seas where Danger still i*-«*mains,
And sailed back poor and broken men, and
pome sailed back in chains.
But, bound or free, or ill or well, where’er
their sails were furled,
They brought to weary, worn-out lands glad
tidings from the World.
The Seasons saw our fathers come, their
flocks and herds increase;
They saw the old lands waste in War, the
new lands waste in Peace;
The Seasons saw new gardens made, they
saw the old lands bleed,
And into new lands introduced the curse of
Class and Creed.
They saw the birth of Politics, and all was
ripe for Greed.
And Mammon came and built his towers,
and Mammon held the fort:
Till one new land went dollar-mad, and one
went mad for Sport.
Where men for love of Science sailed in
rotten tubs for years,
To hang or starve, while nought availed a
wife or daughter’s tears—
Where men made life-long sacrifice for some
blind Northern Power,
Now Science sinks a thousand souls, and
sinks them in an hour.
You would be rich and great too soon —
have all that mortal craves;
The day may come ere you have lived, when
you’ll be poor and slaves.
You heeded not the warning voice, for Self
and Snort prevailed;
You yet might wish, in dust and dread,
those Captains had not sailed.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

During the week the Drunk of Potsdam
smashed a mirror. The King of Saxony
was visiting him, apparently on a temper-
ance mission; but even in royal company
the Drunk was too far gone in his special
brand of d.t.’s to hold himself in. What
breed of contemptible little army he
fancied was crossing the glassware the
cable doesn’t mention; it simply says
that he flew ott tne handle and spoiled
the furniture. All other things of
much consequence that have happened
during the week have been merely
further incidents in the Imperial jim-jams.
In particular Bill has mounted the bar
and told the whole world to come on.
All neutral nations were warned that if
they attempted to do any trading whatever
with Britain their ships would be blown up
without regard for crews or passengers.
Even President Wilson is understood to
have left off nibbling his bath-bun to tell
the demented person on the bar that if he
talked like this and really blew up more than
about 20 United States vessels and crews,
he (Woodrow) would have to give him a
real hard talking-to. Meantime Wilson’s
newspapers begin to remark that if Bill
touches their dollars he touches their dig-
nity, and they will have to punch him;
whereupon Bill has presented the barman
with six Iron Crosses and announced that
he isn’t going to take any notice of any
protests, friendly or otherwise. It is an
interesting situation, and if Bill persists
President Wilson may have to throw the
remnants of the bun at him. It is unfor-
tunate from one point of view that Bill
may not be able to persist, having nothing
to persist with except a few submarines.
For the rest the Drunk’s emissaries in
Palestine and thereabouts continue to push
the Turks, who are mostly Arabs, towards
Egypt, and the Maorilanders and one sec-
tion of the Australians have welcomed them
on the banks of the Canal and brushed
their very torn and untidy clothes. Appar-
ently if the rest of the invaders make any
haste across the desert it will be to get a
square feed from an enemy that they don’t
dread half as much as starvation, aggra-
vated by German stripes. In Europe there
have been the usual small excursions. But
in northern Europe Februarv is a dark
month, full of sleet and misery ; and except
on the east, where nothing matters to hardy
Ivan, the week has been spent mostly in
looking forward to something that is to
happen in the spring.
N; S. Wales has always been the most rabid
State Rights community in Australia, and
when it was proposed to hand over the con-
trol of industrial legislation to the Common-
wealth, the most ferocious opponent was
Joseph Cook. Certainly, he voiced public
opinion as expressed at the Referendum, but
this merely proves that there are a lot of
blind people in N. S. Wales willing to be led
by a one-eyed man. Lately they have reaped
some of the harvest of their folly. The
Commonwealth wanted £19,370 worth of
military saddlery and harness, and called for
tenders. Now, the chief cost to be counted
in making a tender is that of labor. The
firms that tendered already had their plant
and their staffs. The sudden demand of the
military for equipment came as a welcome
windfall. It was merely a case of tendering
according to existing conditions. Naturally,
the question of celerity obtruded itself, and
to ensure it, the contract was offered to as
many firms as possible throughout the States.
But when the tenders were opened the mili-
tary people found that N. S. Wales in every
case had handed in the highest quotations.
The reason was obvious. In the Common-
wealth contracts the tenderers are bound to
pay the wages awarded by the State indus-
trial tribunals. In Sydney the price of
skilled labor in this trade is Is. 3d. per hour
all round, or £3 per week of 48 hours. The
Melbourne tribunal also awarded 60s. per
week, but suspended the award during war
time, or “till normal conditions resumed.”
So the Melbourne manufacturer gets his
labor at 545. per week for men and 245. for
women and girls. The rates in Adelaide
and Brisbane are lower still. The military
people had to do the best they could with
the Commonwealth’s money, and when the
work was distributed it worked out like
this:—
Adelaide, two firms . . . . £7093 13 3
Melbourne, eight firms . . 5316 3 10
Brisbane, four firms . . . . 4913 7 1
Sydney, five firms . . . . 2048 10 1
Discarding the odd money, Adelaide secured
36.6 per cent.; 27.4 per cent.;
Brisbane, 25.3 per cent.,; and silly old Syd-
ney, which upheld State Frights, 10.5 per
cent. The Bulletin is not sorry to see the
other States get a share of the work. It is
not angry because wages are high in Sydney.
It merely rises to point out to the blatant
State Frights employers who spent so much
money in defeating the Referendum that they
are now getting what they asked for.

“High Jinks,” the latest J.C.W. light
musical show, now kicking cheerfully at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, is the usual French
comedy with its fangs drawn. There is
just enough left in it to suggest that, in its
original state, it must have been a death-
adder. There is, for instance, the passage-
at-arms between the pretty actress-nurse
and the elderly rich American, who has been
sent away in her charge by a doctor made
frivolous by a whiff of the wonder-working
“High Jinks” perfume. Finding that she
has registered as his wife at two hotels
(they have just been politely moved on from
the first), he strikes a virtuous attitude and
asks her what she means by it. “Why!”
she drawls amazedly, “I thought it was
expected of me!” The plot is quite simple
and conduces to hilarity. After one whiff
of the “High Jinks” raffing gas, everybody
becomes uproarious and morally irresponsible
and runs away with the other party. It is
the sort of central idea that would become
boresome if done to slow, yearning music
by a lot of staid, easy-going principals with
the fat of middle-age thick upon them. For-
tunately the music—of which there is a
good supply—is nearly all lively and tune-
ful, and the few Jinkers who are not young
and handsome have some other advantages.
Dorothy Brunton is the usual fluffy vision
in a cream-puff: part and a couple of songs,
one of which, a remembersome waltz, is
sung with taking ease. Her careful voice-
production is in delicate contrast to Marie
Eaton’s method of using her high soprano,
which is forced unpleasantly, especially in
a superfluous Faust burlesque several yards
too long. However, Miss Eaton’s acting is
uniformly good, and so is cheerful Florence
Yie’s. The fair and willowy Gertrude Glyn
as usual looms up in one or two gowns that
stun the stalls ; but a wide, sunny smile dis-
arms criticism. She almost succeeds in
being pathetic with a song in which large
rubber balloons are referred to as soap
bubbles. Field Fisher, 0- H. Workman and
W. H. Rawlins, the chief comedians, put
up a remarkably good plain-clothes perform-
ance. Rawlins is the best off for “fat,”
as the moral American invalid mentioned
above. Chris Wren (French waiter) and
Alfred Frith (burlesque Colonel) form a
good comedy second-line. Paul Plunket suc-
ceeds in being the sort of infatuated stage
Frenchman that numberless other actors
have failed to be; and the dainty little
Ylasta Novotna whirls gracefully with
partner Victor Lauschmann in a smart
third-act specialty. The mounting and
dresses are good, and the chorus and orches-
tra do their duty; but the Iron Crosses and
other decorations must be handed to the
14 capable principals.

.Julius Knight, who progressed from bare-
legged religious passion-plays through Napo-
leonic dramas to, Bernard Shaw eccentrics,
seems to be carefully retracing his steps.
The. other day lie had got as far as Napo-
leonic drama. Next Saturday evening he
arrives right back at his holy starting-point,
“The Sign of the Cross.” The patrons who
attend at Sydney Royal this week-end will
see a Marcus Superbus riper, yet still able
to pursue Mercia without the aid of crutches.
There will, moreover, be a new Mercia.
