There is no call in Melbourne for Govern-
ment regulation of melodrama prices these
times. The laws of gravity have lowered
the quartern loaf of melo-food down to 2s.- at
the King’s, and the early doors have gone,
seemingly. That is to say, the doors are
still there, but the bird no longer
uses them to blackmail the early
worm. After the panto, at the King’s
has come “The Confession,” and for
six nights only the Rev. J. J. Bartlett, an
R.C. parish priest, will faithfully perform
the Sacred Offices of his Calling in a rather
assertively self-righteous manner. Llis only
excuse for being so evidently proud of him-
self is the audience’s loud applause, which
falls at his feet in lumps whenever he refers
to his proper sense of duty. “The Confes-
sion” has the crudest little sketch- of a plot
that ever was. In the first act the Rev.
Bartlett’s brother Tom is arrested for a
murder that has just been committed by a
mixed-breed who came to the priest in a hurry
to get absolution for the crime ;' so the audi-
ence knows all about everything from' the
start. After the first act the. prisoner’s old
mother (who sounds no older than 25), and
his girl, and a hunchback lad (played by
our ancient friend Tom Cannam) and
others are engaged in believing Tom to be
as innocent as the audience. knows he is,
whilst the Rev. J.J.B. waits patiently for
the inevitable disclosure r qf the Spanish-
French-Canadian Dago’s guilt. Incidentally,
the drama is a temperance sermon as well as
a note of admiration for Father Bartlett.
Guy Hastings impersonates the highly-
respectable cleric in a moderately earnest
manner. Miss Olive Wilton, as the girl,
needs to be more distinct in her articulation.
Harrison is the lurid boy of the mongrel

This week’s arrival of The Bulletin in
Melbourne was to be celebrated by a profes-
sional matinee of “Bought and Paid For” at
the Royal. The poignant and passionate
drama (advt.) in question continues to
travel on its notoriety, and the management
still offers £5 to the person who can most
tersely express a reasonable opinion on the
merits of Mr. and Mrs. Stafford’s judicious
separation.
At Melbourne Princess’s “The Beggar
Girl’s Wedding” is repeating the parable of
the foolish young man who takes a wife
named Bessie from Thames Embankment
society and suffers severely for his reckless
conduct. The female, it appears, has a gaol
record and a disreputable old father, and these
drawbacks are revealed coincidently with the
discovery of Bess in the arms of a member
of the Society of Polite Criminals. Eor her
indiscretion the damsel is thrown back on the
human ash-tip, whereupon she starts playing
the weeping Magdalen, while the absurd
husband butts into all the mischief that a
hardworking villain and his accomplice can
devise. The sensation of the show is Bess
and hubby’s temporary tenure of “The Cages
of Death,” from which they are rescued by
the strategy of the playwright, who wants
them for some superfluous later scenes. Bob
Inman, as the hero, and Miss Pearl Smith, as
the tearful Bess, pass through the vicissi-
tudes to the inevitable moist reunion ; and in
numerous displays of emotion give passable
imitations of our variable summer weather,
with its hot and muggy moments changing to
thundery conditions and showers. Richard
Bellairs and Miss Jean Robertson, the part-
ners in crime, are united by a common ina-
bility to depict Vice on the higher, plane.
Miss Helmrich and Arthur Albert, are moder-
ate providers of comic relief. Lyle Chris-
tian appears as an ungilded youth; Alf
Scarlett doubles the roles of a lawyer and a
debased remnant of the medical profesh..;
and Harry McLennan and Ward Lyons are
two coves with but a single shirt, two crooks
that pinch as one. “The Beggar GirFs Wed-
ding” finishes to-morrow. On Saturday G.
Willoughby Ltd. will feature another domes-
tic disturbance, “Married to the Wrong
Man.”

At Melb. Tivoli all people and things are as
they were a week ago, except that Odiva and
her slippery companions have now gone from
the place where their tank used to be. The
remaining old identities are mostly putting
in their last nights and matinees, for a
notable and extensive novelty is due on
Saturday. The Follies are coming to Mel-
bourne by way of Sydney, and the Tivoli
Follies, as is generally known among devotees
of vaudeville, combine the madness of a
revue with the insanity of a panto. Their
section of the entertainment comprises a
new assortment of legs, and covers 90
minutes of space in the programme.

“The Great Naval Mystery” is Hoyt’s big
three-part effusion at the National and
Olympia (Melb.) this week. It is the old,
old story of successful plan-filching brought
about. by Adam’s weakness for the apple,
but it contains some unusually exciting
features, and some highly embellished emo-
tion. The next-best item, “The Triumph
of Love,” is a burst of sentiment in a forest;
while a clever comedy picture shows the
\ itagraph actress, Ethel Turner, in several
striking imitations of well-known film iden-
tities.
* * *
The pomp and circumstance of war are
to be presented in kinemacolor at the Mel-
bourne Auditorium. The pictures are from
the Scala (London), are the latest word in
the art of colored motion-pictures, and Lloyd
George _ has testified to their
topical interest. “The Fighting
horces of Europe” on land and
sea are to do their first flit
across the screen, in the Audi,
aforesaid, next Wednesday night.
ihe Naval Combat show, which boomed at
Jijarl s Court (London) in the peaceful days
of yore, is going to strike this country
presently, whilst the iron is red-hot. Dr.
Arthur Russell, of Hoyt’s Pictures (Melb.),
has got the patent and copyright of the Com-
bat for Australia, and a number of convinc-
ing miniature battleships have already been
built for the ingenious show. The scheme
of operations is astonishingly clever. All
sorts of horrors of naval warfare —with the
human casualties omitted —are worked into
the spectacle. When the Combat has been
made ready for action the management’s
trouble will be to find places suitable for
staging it — i.e., floating it —-on an adequate
scale. At Bari’s Court, where naval officers
conducted the warfare, there were 5000
square yards of water for the boats to per-
form on.
Adelaide shows are mostly pictures just
now. The weather has been all against
indoor frolics. Royal and Tivoli are closed,
and even the King’s is taking a vacation,
after a successful week with the football-
playing elephant and the educated ape. At
n est’s is a Napoleonic fighting film, and
Wondergraph imagines a German invasion
ot England. In the open the Strollers and
the Dandies are as they were before—or
more so, if anything.
The first lock and weir planned by South
Aus. in connection with the big scheme for
the improvement of the Murray has struck
a snag at starting. After advertising for
tour months, the Government got only four
tenders, and even the lowest price named was
considered so unreasonably high that the
work will be advertised again.
Prank Pox, one-time a sub-editor of The
Bulletin in his spare moments and an
enthusiastic lieutenant of Field Artillery
at others, has given up his press job at the
front .to take a commission again with
the big guns. As the Morning Post’s
correspondent Fox watched the Gerhogs pour
into. Brussels. He was one of the last
Britishers to leave the city, and what he
saw in those days left him uncomfortable
until he was actually off to the firing-line.
Australia,, by the way, owes something to
I ox for his strenuous advocacy of universal
training at a time when it had very few
friends.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Beaumont Smith believes in the Aus-
tralian drama. He tried it on the dingo
with “Seven. Little Australians,” and the
dingo came in mobs. Now he has secured
the rights of another kiddie play, by Arthur
H. Adams, for production in Sydney at
midwinter. This play, not yet christened,
had its genesis in the urgent request of a
small boy of four. He had howled through
three acts of the usual “children’s” panto-
mime,. the only part of which he confessed
to liking was the thrilling inter-act “where
the kind lady brought round the chocolates.”
The author asked him if he would like a
children’s play written for himself, and the
“BOUGHT AND PAID
POP,” AT MELBOURNE
ROYAL.
Virginia (Muriel Starr) refuses to drink
with her husband.
kiddie, who is the real author, immediately
laid down his ideas about the ideal kiddie
play, and ruthlessly sub-edited the panto-
mime as it was read out to him. The cast
includes animals, children, fairies and dolls ;
and the stage settings and properties, which
will prove the most expensive feature of the
production, aim at carrying out a unique
stage illusion.
“Mrs. Pretty and the Premier,” by
Arthur H. Adams, produced last year by
the Melbourne Repertory Theatre, and sub-
sequently by the Sydney Repertory Theatre,
has been purchased by Arthur Bourcbier,
whose wife is Violet Vanbrugh, for produc-
tion in London.
“Kaniva”: English letters report the
death of Emma Bronton, who appeared in
H. W. Emmet’s panto, of “Sinbad” at Mel-
bourne Opera House in 1884, and after-
wards joined the Brough-Boucicault Co.
and remained with it for a long time. She
was q. hard worker, and with another of the
old brigade, Polly Emery, was engaged in
stage life almost to the end, which came,
after a day’s illness, at 53. One of her sons,
Jack Brandon, has written many sketches
and a couple of short plays.
In a pamphlet issued for the protection of
the charitable, a Sydney church claims to
have got on the track of a master cadger
who “has an extensive repertoire of skil-
fully-constructed and wholly-false tales of
woe, and tells them with an engaging sin-
cerity that would melt the heart of a bronze
statue.” The Bulletin doesn’t know who
the cadger is. but is willing to bet that he
isn’t one of the regular stars. There are no
statue-melters amongst them at present.
Some statistics on the subject of actors
at the front were given at a dinner in
London to thank Herbert Tree for handing
over the profits of the latest run of the
patriotic “Drake” to the War Relief Fund
and the .Actors’ Benevolent Fund—£lol7 to
each. Six hundred English actors have
joined the colors, it was said, and 200 are
in training. This is exclusive of recruits
from the business staffs. Apropos of nothing,
Sir Herbert Tree’s real name is Beerbohm :
he is of German parentage, and has played
in Berlin in the Hogman or Gerhog lan-
guage.
Lily Dampier, Alfred’s elder daughter,
died in Melbourne on Saturday. Born at
Newcastle-upon-Tyne (Eng.) in January,
1869, she made her first appearance in
Dunedin (M.L.) in her father’s drama of
“Saint or Sinner.” She was a mere baby
at the time. Dad came to Australia in
1873, and was for three years at the Mel-
bourne Royal. Then he toured Australia and
Maoriland. During that period Lily married
Alfred Rolfe, who was one of her father’s
young men. She and sister Rose were both
popular as juveniles. Yet Lily at any rate
might have done better had she stuck to
the piano. Her mother (then known as
Miss Alice. Russell) was in a fair way to
become. quite famous when she left for
Australia, and the daughter had large
talents of the same sort.
“Kaniva”: Herbert Tree has suited the
warlike times by producing “King Henry
IV.” (part 1) in London. Matheson Lang
played Hotspur, and Tree was Falstaff.
Half a century ago Barry Sullivan produced
the play twice at the Sydney Royal. The
first time, in September, 1863, he had this
cast: King Henry, G. R. Ireland ; Hotspur,
Robert Heir; Falstaff, Barry Sullivan.
Others were Mrs. Heir. Mrs. A. Phillips,
Alec Fitzgerald and Charles Young. In
July, 1864, Sullivan played Hotspur, and
was excellent in the part; naturally regard-
ing it as the leading one; but J. C. Lam-
bert’s Falstaff captured the audience. His
make-up was. marvellous. He was the em-
bodiment of joviality, bombast, humor and
audacity, irrepressible and resourceful, and
ms acting was so earnest, natural and real-
istic that the spectators were quite carried
away. . The cast otherwise was the same as
m 1863. In 1878 Creswick produced the
Play, he taking Falstaff. It was a rare
performance on his part, but the backing
was not as artistic as Sullivan had.
J- M. Barrie’s latest one-acter, “The
Day, ’ produced at London Coliseum, is a
skit on the German militarists and the toast
they have been drinking for years: “The
Day We Will I,and in Ijjngland.” In peace
times no European mpnarch may be imper-
sonated or burlesqued on the London stage ;
but when one of them becomes an enemy the
censor winks. So Barrie has been allowed
to make the Kaiser a leading character in
his playlet. Besides the Imperial Bandit
the only character that counts is the Spirit
of Culture, impersonated by Irene Vanburgh,
who has some smart satirical lines. America
so.dotes on Barrie that the New York Times
printed “The Day” simultaneously with its
first production in London, and paid Barrie
2s. Id. a word for the privilege.
“J.F.H.”: In J. M. Barrie’s new playlet,
I he Day,” the curtain comes down on Irene
Vanbrugh as the Spirit of Culture handing
something to the Kaiser. What it was
nobody knew at the first performance. Some
said a revolver, others a dagger, and a grace-
less joker in the gallery thought it was a
banana. But the mystery was solved next
morning with the publication of the text.
Barrie’s final stage direction reads: “She
lifts a pistol from the table and puts it in
his hand. She goes away with shining eyes.
Ihe penny dip burns low. The Emperor is
lost in its shadows.” The Pall Mall
Gazette :calls this pistol business a “false
touch, and. William Archer, the Queens-
lander who is dramatic critic of the Stak,
says the same : “It is neither good taste nor
good sense for the Spirit of Culture to hand
the Emperor a pistol as the curtain falls,
and leave him gazing down its blue barrel.”
Certainly it does seem a risk to take He
may funk it.

“U.S.”: Josephine Cohan, Fred Niblo’s
wife, is sister of George Cohan, one of the
leading New York dramatists and producers.
Their parents were Mr. and Mrs. Jerry
Cohan, who came to Australia many years
ago with an entertainment called the Hiber-
nicon, composed of acting, singing, dancing
and panorama of Ireland. There was an
imitative rival, also in Australia, called the
Hibernica, managed by little H. A. B. Wil-
liams. Jerry Cohan, now a grave, spec-
tacled gent., was celebrated for dancing. He
resolved that his wife must have' nothing to
do with the stage, so he took a girl who had
never been to a theatre. But Jerry found
himself in a hole through the secession of
the Hibernicon lady. Said she scornfully:
“Try your wife.” Thus challenged, Mrs.
Jerry went on, having only seen the show
from the front, and stopped on. They had
the luck of never being separated except
once for three months. Mrs. Jerry’s photo,
before me, shows everlasting youth, Yet
they had sometimes to give five shows a day!
“Busk”; “D” writes (B. 21/1/T5) about
Bernhardt and Ellen Terry having retired
from the stage; but really it retired from
them. This is the usual way. Whenever
such veteran favorites make a fitful appear-
ance, for a benefit, they are received with
a shout which they misinterpret into an
idea that they .would be welcome all the
time. Genuine retirements are consequently
few. Forbes-Robertson’s appears to be one.
Yet even he would come to Australia if he
could agree with Tallis, Ward and Meynell
as to his value.
James Dalgarno, a former Under-Sec. of
the P.M.G.’s Department (N.S.W.), died
on Sunday, aged 78. His father, a sea-
captain, sailed the Funchal between Mauri-
tius and Sydney in the ’3o’s. In that decade
the old man gave up seafaring and
“squatted” at Mudgee, then sparsely peopled
by assigned servants, ticket-of-leave men,
and a few others. Severe droughts drove
him back to Sydney, where he got a job as
wharfinger. He had two sons —John, who
was an electrician in the Government ser-
vice ; and James, who was in the postal
service all his working life. Outside that
James took a keen interest in the Historical
Society, and he dabbled in oils with con-
siderable success.
“Zwanzeur,” concerning an unlucky old
lady;—
“R.5.V.P.,” in a recent Bulletin, quotes from
McHugh’s book on modern Mexico that Princess
Charlotte, the widow of Maximilian, “died in a mad-
house some years ago.” This is incorrect. Late in
1911 I was at the chateau, near Brussels, where she
was then confined, and I am confident that had she
died subsequently her death would have been
announced, as she is the last link with the Mexican
tragedy of the ’6o’s. For about 40 years Charlotte
was a carefully-watched resident of the royal
demesne of Jervuereii, then almost a suburb of the
Belgian capital, The castle was destroyed by fire,
and it was whispered that the exalted prisoner was
responsible. She was then removed to the chateau,
where surveillance could be more strictly exercised.
On the site of the ruins of Jervueren Leopold 11.,
her brother, built the beautiful Congo Museum, and
presented it to the nation, together with the delight-
ful park which surrounds it, and is part of the
forest of Soignies. The sympathy of the Belgians
for their king’s daughter, the victim of the Mexican
fiasco, has always been mixed with a bitter feeling
that she was instrumental in the appeal for volun-
teers to swell the inadequate French expeditionary
force under General Bazaine, which failed to subdue
Mexico, though it cost many Belgian lives.
“Binnacle”: The other day N. S. Wales
shipowners called on Minister for Customs
Tudor to obtain certain exemptions for ves-
sels running under the new Navigation Act.
Amongst other things they wanted to escape
the clause which makes the installation of
wireless compulsory, and advanced as a
reason that Morse lamps, used on a head-to-
head run, in conjunction with the light-
houses, would be sufficient for all require-
ments. It is a rotten contention. During
the last five years the Rosedale, Macleay.
Yongala and Koombana have all been lost
on a head-to-head run, and no man knowetli
where their bones bleach. Likewise, Aus-
tralia’s trawler, the Endeavor, was not
fitted with wireless. Doubtless she had
lamps for Morse signalling, but, so far, the
signals haven’t been observed.
From a man with nearly 50 years’ Mel-
bourne press experience behind him :
Mayor Hennessy rises to the occasion. “Nobody
is to be hungry,” he says, and means it. I will
rank that with the best thing ever uttered in Aus-
tralia. This was Mort’s: “There shall be no more
waste,” when Nicolle had shown him how to do
the freezing. Nor does Hennessy propose to trans-
fer his burden to the Salvation Army or make any
sort of religious side-show of it. I never saw the
outlook gloomier. Remember that the wheat har-
vest, which means more to Victoria than to N. S.
Wales, was three times poorer than the Ma State’s.
N. S. 'Wales grew 41 bushels for every 100 it had
in the previous year ; Victoria reaped only 12. The
gold industry is sick; the fruit crop was a rank
failure. An acquaintance, thrown out of work in
Melbourne, went to a country town of third grade
and found 300 out-of-works on the police list. And
we know that things can get no better for most of
the year. It is the biggest thing a Melbourne
Mayor has had to do since December, 1854, when
John Thomas Smith rose to his occasion. News was
brought by horsemen of Eureka Stockade. A rumor
arose that the diggers, thousands strong, were
marching on Melbourne. Mayor Jacky Tommy
Smith issued a Universal Service order, and fol-
lowed the order up. This was what secured him his
seven terms and made .him Melbourne’s Whit-
tington.
THE HIGH-TONED PANTO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cinderella is a high-toned story, wherein all the
characters are connected with the aristocracy.—
“Sundry Shows” (4/2/’l5).
The highbrow sat in his high-priced seat,
Quite near to the orchestra-railing;
To get close up when a panto.’s on
Is ever a human failing!
He glimpsed the high-kickers through his glass,
Though his hair was scant and hoary,
So I said to him: “Mr. Highbrow, I’m
Surprised you should patronise pantomime!”
Said he: “They are mostly waste of time,
But —this is a high-toned story !
“High art’s in this tale of the slipper of glass,
That is put so deftly before us.”
(Swift to his eyes went the lenses here,
For there entered the “beauty-chorus.”)
“No one is of lowly parentage,
And in birth and rank I glory:
And a high-born Baron’s whole familee
We’re seldom permitted, m.v friend, to see,
And these limbs of the aristocracee
Give—well, it's a hig,.-^o.. iU si,ory!
This temple of song and eke of dance,
What person, could ever be sad in?
It’s different quite with those vulgar tales,
‘Crusoe,’ ‘Sinbad’ and ‘Aladdin’ ;
Though I dote of Sudermann, Ibsen, Shaw,
You will find me commendafory
Of the high note struck by this beauteous show—
Excuse me, the ballet’s on now, you know! ”
Here he focussed a fay in the very front row,
And was lost in the high-toned story 1

WINES
A strange story comes from England of
a man with two late wives, both of whom
washed themselves. His name is George
Smith, and he is charged in London with
making a false entry in a marriage regis-
ter. It is alleged that his first wife was
found dead in a bath a few weeks after
marriage, and that his second wife died in
the same place the day after her marriage.
Smith himself wasn’t found in the bath, so
far as the evidence shows, either dead or
alive, or before marriage or after marriage.
Smith, it is said, used to be a land agent,
which is a suspicious circumstance and calls
for much investigation, if land agents in
England are anything like what they used to
be in Australia.
The misguided patriots who lately pro-
vided eight travelling kitchens for the Vic.
Light Horse Brigade are now kicking them-
selves with both feet. These camp-cookers,
costing £lBO each, have been ticketed useless
for active service, and are now hiding in a
shed somewhere. Cabled reports of travel-
ling kitchens in the German lines are re-
sponsible for the fiasco. Nobody knew much
about the manufacture of such things,
apparently; nevertheless a blacksmith, or
farrier, or other unqualified person went
ahead with the job. If he had consulted his
wife it might have been better. Anyway,
as “Johanna’s” ribald remarks suggested
after the first parade of the things, woman s
eye at once detected flaws which turn out
to be as fatal as they looked.
* * *
A Ballarat P.M. recently laid down the
law as to goats and the destruction thereof.
The goat is notoriously the Prussian of the
animal kingdom, wherefore he is frequently
potted, shelled, bombed or otherwise de-
stroyed by outraged civilisation. The trouble
with the particular goat which caused the
Ballarat argument was that, having been
shot, he didn’t die promptly, but perversely
lingered for 10 minutes or so awaiting the
ambulance. Therefore the S.P.C.A. sum-
moned the artillery for cruelty to animals.
The P.M. opined that the question was:
“Did the defendant shoot the goat carefully
and with skill?” and finding that he did, the
Court acquitted him —with £2 2s. costs.
* * * *
Recently somebody grumbled that, if the
tales of Gerhog atrocities in Belgium were
true, the medical papers would have said
something. Probably the reason they haven’t
said much is that the men who could write
have been too busy attending to the Butcher’s
victims. However, here is part of the
Lancet’s report of an address delivered to
the Edinburgh Obstetrical Society by Pro-
fessor Jacobs, of Brussels, the head of the
Belgian Red Cross :
I met a doctor who had not eaten bread for a fort-
night, living only on potatoes. Some had to lie on
straw for beds. One had his only pair of boots on
his feet, and these falling to pieces; others had only
torn garments to clothe themselves with. One had to
live for three days on wayside herbs, and his wife
shared his fate. Another was searching in vain for
six or seven days for his wife and family in the
country around. Some doctors had been imprisoned
as hostages; some had been shot. One, the burgo-
master of a place near Liege, was seized, had his ears
cut off, his eyes gouged out, and was then shot;
afterwards his head was carried on the end of the
lance of an Uhlan through the town. There were
widows and orphans of doctors deprived of every-
thing. A doctor and his son were shot before the
eyes of the wife. As head of the Belgian Red Cross
I visited a chateau, but found the Red Cross had
not been respected; it had been completely destroyed
and the bodies of six girls, aged from 10 to 17, were
lying on the lawn. A convent containing 60 sisters
had been entered by the German soldiers and every
one had been violated ; on the evidence of the doctor
to the institution 25 were pregnant. I had operated
on the wife of a doctor living near Namur. Three
weeks after the operation, when convalescing and
still in bed, their house was entered by German sol-
diers ; she was raped by seven of them and died two
days afterwards.
Is that enough?

The impressions thus far of a Maoriland
surgeon, conveyed' to his recent fellow-
traveller, “Hop” :
When only three weeks in England war was
declared. A r olunteering for the front, I was sent
out with the pioneer British Expeditionary Force,
and was among the first to land at Havre. Follow-
ing that, I had my share, of course, of the retreat
and all the bitterness that it meant—back, back
before the strong German advance. At last! A
long breather, and, like a thunderbolt, we were
hurled forward, and with a bloody rush won the
Battle ..of the Marne. We crossed the bridge at 7
p.m., still under a pitiless fire. All that night I
was operating on the scene of the morning’s battle.
Rapidly sending our wounded to the rear we were
on the march again by four o’clock next morning,
relentlessly following the rapidly-retreating Ger-
man. During those inspiriting days what captures
there were of prisoners, arms, batteries, equipment!
The march was across one of the fairest lands in the
world, but dotted now with the abandoned corpses
of Germans. Every hamlet breathed romance; but
it was not for us in this grim walk.
Then rain fell pitilessly and the roads were lines
of mud. The fair land became a sodden landscape,
with a pall of driving, lowering clouds. All sombre
and dark and threatening and very mournful. That
was the stage setting ; and the rattle of the rifle-fire,
the typewriter note of the Maxim, the whistle of the
shrapnel, the smashing bursts of the high explosives
made a devil’s orchestra. This went on for days, of
which we lost count —we pursuing ; marching night
and day; very exhausted, but very cheery, because
we were advancing in the good old British way.
Then the River Aisne and a pause. You know about
the battle that followed. You know its tales of
hard fighting in the rain, of its desperate bayonet
attacks at night, of its artillery combats, of its long
list of wounded, dying and dead. It was my fate to
know much of the last, for I was in constant touch
with the 6th Division, who, though smashed badly,
so gloriously held the country on the far side of
the valley.
Next we suddenly appeared in the north-east of
France at La Bassee, and later at Ypres. And here
I am to-day on Belgian soil.
The fighting all along has been most sanguinary
and stubborn. Oh, we are proud men! British
history is punctuated with stories of the heroism
and the bulldog fighting of British soldiers. But in
our island story there is nothing to excel—no,
nothing to equal—the tenacity of our soldiers in the
field to-day, hanging on against foes outnumbering
them by four to one. When they have retired, they
have done it so doggedly, dealing death with a piti-
less sleet of lead and with bayonet attacks that set
you wondering whether they were men or devils.
How magnificently, they all have upheld the nima of
the Anglo-Saxon only we who have seen them can
quite realise.
We feel the cold very much, and are waiting for
the spring to advance across the Rhine. I am not
vindictive, but I hope to see, before my days are
ended, a German city in ruins. I wish Germans to
know what it is to have their homes destroyed. I
do not wish to carry the German method of killing
defenceless women and children. Far from that.
But I am barbarian enough to enjoy the sight of
wrecked German railways and warehouses and
chateaux. I have seen these in France and Belgium,
and I am not civilised enough yet to forego the
doctrine of a tooth for a tooth. Needless to say,
from the surgical point of view, I have been busy.
Our wounded are getting the best possible care at
the front. In the stress of violent military opera-
tions it is frequently impossible to do all that one
would like to do for them. But that is understood.
I think it was Macaulay who said that the “essence
of war is violence,” and if he said that he knew
what he was talking about. But to violence we are
adding things that even Macaulay knew nothing
about.

“Enid McGush”: The S. M. Herald
quotes the letter of a trooper in Egypt: — 1
Everything was very interesting to us at first, but
the novelty has worn off now, and we long to get
away and do our bit of fighting and return to dead
old Sydney.
Has it come to this? Is Sydney actually
“dead” by comparison with the land of the
Pharaohs and Ptolemies? Perhaps after the
war we may be able to make a living by
showing the ruins of the G.P.O. to smart
young Egyptian tourists, who come from a
land where railways have a uniform gauge
and ports are open to bring goods to their
nearest market.
Of all foolish customs there is none more
utterly goatish than that which makes it
an act of heroism for a captain to sink with
his ship. The other day a French sub-
marine got into trouble, but managed to
land its crew. Instead of getting away to
fight again and help his country out in her
struggle, the commander climbed up on the
periscope or pimple of his vessel, struck a
pose, and fed himself to the iEgean sharks.
A man like that deserves his sharks. Also
there sould be a monument of mud erected
to him somewhere with a wild ass tethered
to it.
WHEN THE GERMAN HOPE WENT DOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Seydlitz was a silent ship steaming desper-
ately for shelter.
Over the sea, the grey, unhurrying sea,
Brightening'beneath the January sun,
The grim, gigantic Dreadnoughts turn and flee
Before the vengeful foe. And as they run
With Parthian hate they answer gun with thunder-
ing gun !
Swift overhead the aircraft dive and swoop ;
Beneath the wave there glides the submarine,
Like some enormous porpoise, and a troop
Of lean destroyers dull the morning sheen
With webs of smoke that form the shifting battle-
screen.
Giant is met by giant in this hour 1
The race is to the swift and to the strong.
Flung into fight, regardless, is the power —
Yea, unto strength the battle doth belong !
Hark! Where the Titan-guns roar forth their
triumph-song!
Britain, the ancient Mistress of the Seas,
In her fond-guarded waters hears anew
The challenge of a foe who fights and flees,
And, fleeing, is foredone with ship and crew —
Who, threatening all things, fails, and knows not
what to do!
N.S.W.
K. McGEDDON.

“The Old Soldier”: Touching those re-
ported cases of desertion from the contin-
gents, let us differentiate. The crime is a
trivial one as it is perpetrated nowadays.
As practised by the “bounty-jumpers” of the
British and United States armies of 50 or 60
or more years ago, it was not only disloyalty
but fraud. In the Crimean War cash boun-
ties of £5 and upwards, with a free kit, were
given to recruits to tempt them to enlist.
Numbers of them deserted at the first chance,
and annexed another bounty and free-kit
from the next recruiting party they met.
The free-kit did not amount to much, but
the money was a consideration. The. few
who have deserted here have probably done
so, not for such sordid gains, but because
they found the conditions of life in the con-
tingent were intolerable to them for some
reason. _ In brief, they discovered that they
were misfits; and they were then uncon-
sciously doing the country a good turn by
getting out, for a misfit is a calamity in
war-time.

“Chudah”: There are 6000 pros-
perous Jewish farmers in the United States,
and they have just been holding a conference
in New York, which has 1000 of them.
The burning question
was whether they
ought to raise hogs.
The conference de-
cided, on Rabbinical
advice, that pigs,
though not desirable,
were allowable. There
is nothing in Moses to
proscribe breeding for
the Gentile. Indeed,
I think the evidence
of Leviticus is that
the Jews had pigs,
under the care of that
individual so often re-
ferred to as the stran-
ger. That estimable
Melbourne citizen, the
late Israel Jacobs,
was ardent for young
Jews to go on the
land, and mainly sup-
ported a colony of
them at Shepparton.
Said he: “Our ances-
tors, you know, were
shepherds. We learnt
our present ways
among the Babylo-
nians.” Hereby hangs
a tale. An Australian
city has three suburbs,
straggling one after
the other, nicknamed
Bricktown, Porktown
and Torntown. A
lady used to hold the
nig business of Pork-
town in her masterly
fingers. She was the
main support of a fine
local church known
as the Cathedral. One
Sunday the preacher,
who knew nothing of
the local industry,
incidentally touched
on the 2000 demons
entering the swine.
Finding the reference
to pigs caught on, he
proceeded to expound the fearful diseases
engendered by pork-eating—trinchinopolis,
elephantiasis, quadratics and goodness knows
what. The Queen took him in hand after-
wards.
* The Adelaide City Council has just
“scrapped” the first building ever erected on
“the Corporation acre” —a single-storey
stone structure which has lately served as
the messenger’s quarters. The first Council
(1840) had to hire a place to meet in, and
this modest building was the first Council-
room and offices of its own. Now it is to
be smothered by additions to the present
Town Hall block to cost £ll,OOO.
“Coot” : The Maternity Bonus Department
has to investigate some remarkable cases,
but this one is reckoned the most bewilder-
ing yet looked into. A claim was received
from a mother for the five pounds in respect
of an infant, and, the papers being all in
order, the sum was paid. A few weeks later
she made a claim for another fiver for a
child born one month after the first one, and
submitted the usual proofs. The astounded
officials made inquiries, and the doctor
attending the mother assured them that two
babies had been born, and that a month had
elapsed between the births. The Depart-
ment got over the difficulty by pronouncing
the infants to be twins, and so declined to
pay the second five-pound note —which seems
rather hard on the mother. Moral: Don’t
have twins, except singly, and then arrange a
reasonable interval between their birthdays.
* * * *
The “Song of Hate” is not the first fam-
ous German love-lyric of the kind. Indeed,
it Avas probably suggested by the “Song of
Hatred” of the revolutionist Georg Herwegh,
whose “Poems of a Living Man” were once
well known. Herwegh’s “Song of Hatred”
was of a tyrannicidal character —that is to
say, it eulogised the man who gave “one last
kiss to the wife and child” and took a “firm,
tight grip of the poniard.” It was greatly
admired by the Young Ireland Party, and
was translated by J. Clarence Mangan, the
revolutionary poet. The refrain of that
translation was:—
For we’ve had more than enough of love;
So now for a spell of hatred.
Years after the original publication of
Clarence Mangan’s version, when Johann
Most had settled in London and was prose-
cuted for eulogising the assassination of
the Tsar Alexander, he republished “The
Song of Hatred,” together with Beacons-
field’s “Revolutionary Epick,” and other
poems that were thought to justify tyranni-
cide. Herwegh was a pretty thorough-going
revolutionist. In 1848 he raised a military
force which invaded Baden; but the enter-
prise failed, and Herwegh had to flee to
Switzerland. It was in those days and out
of that and similar movements that S. Aus-
tralia got the German refugees who laid the
foundations of the present big settlements
with the Teuton names.

“Because the longer we're engaged the less

Early in December a Berlin newspaper
was advertising special five-day trips through
devastated Belgium to enable sightseers to
enjoy the devil-work of their soldiers in
Liege, Dinant and Namur. Evidently Bel-
gium was butchered to make a German
holiday.
From the Ardrossan (Scotland) Herald :
The Rev. W. R. Thompson, of Bellshill, took as
his text on Sunday evening: “It’s a long, long way to
Tipperary,” and the tune was played as a closing
voluntary.
The ghost of John Knox has no terrors for
that cleric!
WHILE PLUMES
ARE ALL THE GO.
M AY: “No, George, it cannot

It is interesting to watch that habitual
striker, the Northern (N.S.W.) miner, when
he is struck against. The Bungs of Kurri
Kurri declined to go on working their beer-
pumps at the old price. The miners retali-
ated by locking the publicans out. They
wouldn’t have the beer at the increased
money, and they hung on to that till the
strikers got very tired and offered the poison
at the old price. Then the capitalist miners
spoke in the cld, familiar way: the whole
business having been opened up, it was now a
question not of restoring the old rate, but of
fixing a fair rate; and they reckoned this
fair rate was three ha’pence a half-pint.
Human nature is pretty much the same no
matter whether it calls its meeting-place a
club or a trades hall.
Grand Master Snowball,
M.L.A., Victoria’s Orange
lodger, has discovered the
secret of German militarism,
the cause of the German
navy, the meaning of the
Kaiser’s ultimatum to Holy
Russia, and the origin of the
late Paul Kruger’s objec-
tions to the British flag. “It
is proved beyond doubt,”
said the astounding Snow-
ball to an Orange picnic,
“that the war in South
Africa was owing to a desire
on the part of the Jesuits to
break up the British Em-
pire, whilst the present war
is owing to a campaign by
the Jesuits, who have suc-
ceeded in embittering the
German nation against the
English.” These are the
oddest clods that Snowball
ever ploughed up from his
field of political research.
What the Jesuits had to do
in S’Africa with the Zulu
rising in The ’7o’s, the dis-
covery of diamonds and gold,
the subsequent Jameson raid
and the greed of Jewhannes-
burg, is just as
evident as their
connection with
the Prussian at-
tack on Rom a n
Catholic Belgium
and France, and
the subseouent
world-conquest pre-
parations of the
Protestant of Pots-
dam. A Snowball
at an L.O.L. boan
feast is in the same
happy position as a
parson in a pulpit.
Nobody expects
him to draw a line
of reasoning through the centre of his alle-
gations. He just says things, and the
congregation disperses with a sense of hav-
ing been further confirmed in its belief.
There are some quaint streaks of sim-
plicity or credulity mixed with the cunning
of Hans Bludwurst. As a case in point,
an obscure Gerhog paper called Father-
land recently came out with an article
urging Canada, Ireland, Australia and
Maoriland to rise against Britain and
“throw off the yoke.” The idea of over
6,000,000 square miles of country throwing
off the yoke on the invitation of a very-
little-known organ printed in a foreign lan-
guage has an element of humor. Also it
seems a waste of ink.

“Melb” : The astute Archdeacon Bindley,
the real power behind the throne in this
archdiocese, is just back from England. He
has engaged some great unknown genius, of
the Ditchfield order, who is to take charge of
the St. James’s, St. John’s and St. Peter’s
missions. No, no—Australia cannot furnish
such ! Only the camel jobs for Australians!
Yet Ilindley himself gives that theory the
lie. He, the most commanding figure in the
diocese, has had all his experience in it.
Here it was he became a deacon in 1880, and
was admitted to orders two years later.
Since he came, to his days of power he has
invented three dioceses —Bendigo, Gippsland
and Wangaratta—and yet has refused to
take any. He is author of Melbourne’s
anomaly that the Archdeacon is above the
Dean. Stephen was, and Godby is, putty.
Dean Vance, with £llOO a year out of St.
James’s £9OOO or so, really was a Dean;
but he had the chagrin of knowing that, to
all intents and purposes, the billet was to
perish with him. However, the two arches,
Clarke and Hindley, want to remove the
scandal that St. James’s and St. John’s are
only loafing rent-collectors, like the mori-
bund Collins-street Independent Church;
so these parishes are to be centres of what
the church . terms work. But the funds,
brethren, are none too secure. A lot more
hopeless-looking shops have just been put up
at St. Jack’s.

LITTLE LADY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Little Lady! Little Lady! you’ve a very winsome
face,
And your white-winged hat is dainty—and it’s quiet;
You’ve a sweet, reposeful manner and you move and
talk with grace,
And your dress, although it’s smart, is not a riot.
AVhile you chat across the table I am stealing looks
at you—
Your expressions are distracting, like your eyes of
tender blue.
Yours is not a strident beauty—l discovered you by
chance;
Now the women all about you do not matter.
There is something vivid in you—some suggestion of
far France,
But —you’ve got your checks! You’re going? (Oh,
the chatter!)
Life is full of these small partings, tiny breaks that
leave a scar.
Little Lady! Little Lady! Now, I wonder who you
are?
N.S.W.
FRUIT SALAD.
AN EARLY VICTORIAN LADY TO HER TOOTH-
LESS SWAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ask me no more without your teeth!
Cease, sir, your toothless wooing!
’Midst battle-flames seek Glory’s wreath
And teeth to aid your chewing.
I can’t abide your British gums
Through which each mangled fond vow hums.
Ask me no more to give my hand
To one whose fangs are missing,
For toothless suitors I have banned
Because I hate their hissing.
Then, too, it makes me ill and weak
To see a gallant’s sunken cheek.
I beg that you will dry your tears!
Speak not of self-destruction,
Mumbling like one of ninety years
And killing sighs with suction.
Come! if you love me, smile with glee,
For toothless weeping vexes me!
The swain condemned to bread and slops,
Though he grow thin and lanky,
May go and grow tobacco crops
And come back as a Yankee,
Who needs no teeth. (They tell me those
Americans talk through the nose.)
So forth, Sir Knight, and learn this art,
Then you’ll no more offend me.
AYeep not, but play the hero’s part,
And Indian relics send me.
Farewell! No, sirrah, not my lips
AVith toothless gums. My fingertips!
Alas, poor creature! he is off
To brave his perils blindly!
If savages his scalp should doff
And deal with him unkindly,
I can’t be blamed, because, you see,
His bare gums shock my modesty.
N.S.W.

A MAD WORLD, MY MASTERS!


The Australians under fire have shown,
as was expected, that indomitable Imperial
spirit which rises superior to every ill, and
which is always encouraged by that wise
spirit, King George IV. topnotch Scotch.*
Despite the war and the drought, Sydney’s Royal
Agricultural Show, due at Easter, threatens to break
records in several departments. The animal that
provides the high-priced roast, in particular, promises
to parade in unusual o.uantity and quality. Entries
for tile various sections close within a month, and
the backbone is exhorted to hurry in its nomina-
tions.*

WARNING TO DYSPEPTICS.
Many interesting paragraphs have recently
appeared in the press concerning the remark-
able antacid properties possessed by Bisu-
rated Magnesia, and thousands of people
now regularly take half a teaspoonful of the
preparation in a little water after their
meals as a safeguard against indigestion,
dyspepsia, heartburn, flatulence, lt
appears, however, that in several instances
other forms of magnesia, bearing somewhat
similar names, have been used, often wita
very unpleasant results. To guard against
dangerous substitution and to ensure getting
the genuine produce as recommended hi
physicians, readers should ask their chemist
distinctly for pure Bisurated Magnesia, and
insist on being supplied in bottles having the
full name —Bisurated Magnesia— blown in
the glass.*
PERSONAL
ITEMS
Dr. W. L. Cleland left the Adelaide
mental hospital” (formerly lunatic asylum)
: n February 1. after presiding over it for
36 years. He is only just 68, but feels he
; as earned a rest from the depressing pro-
fession. The K.C. and the winemaker (’le-
nds are brothers of the doctor, who is
onnected also with the Davenports, the
oioneers of olive-growing in Australia. Dr.
Downey, his deputy, becomes Acting-Super-
intendent at Parkside.
JENSEN , Assistant Minister for Defence.
“Moana”: A recent par. (B. 14/1/T5)
accused Kitchener of having only one hobby
—flower gardening. Please charge golf
against him as well. He has taken to the
game or business recently, and is suspected
of having the makings of a first-class player
concealed about him.
There are cases in which even the doctor
is out of place in a sick-room. A Queens-
town (Tas.) medico was sitting on a
patient's bed the other day. when he sud-
denly fell back dead. Death can lie a ghastly
humorist at times without going to the war
to play jokes. By the way. the deceased,
Dr. Massey, had two sons with the Aus-
tralian force in Egypt.
“Jeames” about a duke who needs the
money:—•
The cable mentions that Zimmerman, the Duke of
Manchester's Yankee father-in-law, has just died,
leaving the Duchess £2,000,000. The Manchesters
need the money badly. He is the only British duke
who has ever earned his living in the “picture” busi-
ness, and-one of the few who have worked. Dukes
loathe work. Manchester is also about the only non-
royal duke who has had no hand in the war. He has
been living near New York for years, following his
honest but humble calling. The U.S. newspapers have
been asking, somewhat peevishly, of late why he
doesn’t go away and fight. His invariable answers
are (1) that he doesn’t know how to fight, having
had no military training since he left the militia
years ago ; and (2) that he can’t afford to leave his
job. The passing of Zimmerman removes one ob-
stacle. Kitchener will probably be willing to deal
with the other. The first time I ever saw Ins Grace
was at Dinard, in the north of France. A local host-
ess gave a fancy-dress dance, and he transpired in a
bath-towel tout seal. 1 don’t know what he was
supposed to represent, but 1 know he was urged to
go away. *
THEY WHO COME AND HE WHO
WAITS FOR THE COMING.
Alex. Horniman, 8.A., whose death hap-
pened the other day in Sydney, was. a
barrister who put in most of his life clerking
in the District Court. He had a fine lot
of yarns of the early days. His father had
the Australian College in Surry Hills in the
’4o’s, and was associated with Dr. Lang
subsequently. Among his scholars were the
brothers John, Tom and .George Dibbs, who
years later presented their old teacher with
a gold watch on his birthday. The father
passed this on to the son, and it was among
the latter’s treasures when he died.
On the word of a friend of the family:—
The Kaiser can never turn pale. Even if lie
were tried for murder, and sentenced to something
lingering with boiling oil in it, he could not
change color. His bronzed appearance is
rubbed in like a floor stain by his valet.
It has been the fashion among Gerhog military
swankeys to ape the facial aspect of peastly
Britishers just returned from foreign service, whilst
swanking in the martial duds which no British
officers wear when off duty. Hence they have a
characteristic in common with rouged lydies of the
town—nobody can tell how sick they ought to be
looking.
Victoria now learns, a lot of it for the
first time, that Gisborne was one of the first
settlements south of the Murray. It was
named after a Sydney police magistrate —
one of the earliest —the third. He was son
of Thomas Gisborne, who was member of the
House of Commons for Carlow in the Parlia-
ment of 1837. The son was sent to Port
Phillip as Commissioner of Crown Lands. He
was fond of the turf, was one of those who
selected the Flemington racecourse, and was
on the committee at the first meeting held
there, and ran a horse. The early press, of
Australia has many proofs of his art with
the pen. He sailed for England in the first
’forties, but died and was buried at sea.
The father of the Kaiser’s unfortunate wife
visited Australia in ISSO. Accompanied by
a guardian, “Lieutenant Groenwald” arrived
in Melbourne in one of the Godeffroy’s ships
from Hamburg, having been swayed to some
extent in the selection of a travel route by
the fact that German emigration to Port
Phillip had commenced the year before. The
Prince of Schleswig-Holstein was then 19
years of age. William Westgarth, who men-
tions the incident in his “Personal Recol-
lections,” represented the Godeffroys in Mel-
bourne. He says that Captain Carr, the
Prince’s guardian, came to him one morning
in great distress. At a public meeting on
the day previous the Captain had made some
remarks, and The Argus had reported that
“Captain Stanley Carr told the meeting that
the King of Prussia had told him” so and so.
Carr complained that the expression should
have been that “an exalted personage in
Prussia had led him to understand” so and
so. The Prince and his mentor spent some
days at Westgarth’s residence, Maryfield, on
Merri Merri Creek.
Michael Adler, who has joined John
Bull’s Expeditionary Force, is the first
Jewish chaplain to be attached to a British
army. Privates Sheni, Ham and Japheth
reckoned they had earned a rabbi by reason
of their numerousness, there being over 5000
of the chosen people in pursuit of the un-
clean hog. If only 3200 had enlisted, the
Hebrew, in proportion to his. numbers,
would have equalled other races in his re-
sponse. so far as the United Kingdom is
concerned.
The dead Pope’s Apostolic Delegate has
been so long on the road to Australia that
a story had started round to the effect that,
the Australian bishops having protested, the
Pope had hung up the whole business.
Apparently the whole yarn tied on to
Bishop Phelan’s swift dart for Rome is
rubbish. Anyway, a “wireless” casually
dropped in at what used to be the Kyar-
dinal’s palace in Sydney the other evening
announcing that the Delegate, Cerretti, was
on the way from Rome via the United
States, and might be expected in for lunch
any day. His job in Australia is to act as
a sort of High Court, with the Vatican as a
Privy Council, to which he can allow appeals
if he likes, and needn’t if he doesn’t like.

left Collins-street for Regent’s Park, Lon-
don. is letting himself down lightly. In

when you want to stand a hair on end and

A cairn has just been put up at Gisborne
(Vic.), to the memory of its pioneer, Henry
Ilowey. The date of his landing in Aus-
tralia is unknown, but he was in Sydnev in
1526, when he was married to the only
daughter of Capt. Minchin, of the 102nd
Regiment. This Minchin, who was a wit-
ness against Bligh, started as a land-owner
with 1000 acres near Parramatta. Ilowey
managed this estate, even then known as
Minchinbury. The doings of Batman and
the Port Phillip Association directed his at-
tention south, and in February, 1837. he
sent 4000 sheep and 800 cattle from X. S.
Wales in charge of one Hamilton. It was
reported that the party had been massacred,
but it got safely to Macedon in July. The
long familiar “Ilowey huts” were soon after-
wards erected at Gisborne Creek by Hamil-
ton. the real pioneer, though the boss, as
usual, nobbles the glory.
BRIBING THE MISSUS.
The health committee of the Sydney City Council
has recommended that a premium of £300 be
offered for the best method of disposing of city
refuse.
MARY ANN: “ There's the broom, Mum! If
you do me worrk I'll give yez a penny."
Thomas Joshua Dibdin, just dead at Cres-
wick (Vic.), was one of the pioneers of the
district that bred Premier Peacock’s cackle.
He had big interests in the Madame Berry
and other famous local companies, and also
founded a village named Dibdinville, where
he had over 200 miners’ cottages. But he
built upon sand, albeit there was gold in it,
wherefore Dibdinville crumbled long before
Dibdin.
Gregory Board, who died a couple of
months ago at Ashfield (N.S.W.), has ieft
20 pictures from his collection to the Art
Gallery and two to the Lewisham Hospital.
A Sydney native, this Gregory Board was
the son of another Gregory Board, who in
the "30’s kept the Blue Lion Inn in Ken’t-
street. During a long life the son cultivated
a judgment of real value in art matters, and
he had an income ample enough to allow
him to indulge his hobby.
THE ONE THING MISSING.
SETTE: “Yes, I've been most everywhere in

George Cochrane —better known by his
pen name of “Grant Hervey’ —a huge, vain,
muscular man from Casterton, in Western
Victoria, was sentenced the other day by
a Sydney judge to a pair of years’ hard
labor. Hard labor isn’t likely to trouble
him much, for he has a constitution which
could shift pyramids and tear up the Sphinx
by the roots. In his earlier and better days,
Cochrane-Hervey was one of the best writers
of frivolous verse in Australia. He could
make a musical rhyme out of a tree or a
caterpillar or any old thing, and the tree
was always readable and the _ caterpillar
worth paying for. At the same time he was
an unballasted sort of person with a lament-
able tendency to fly away after new ideas.
A day came when somebody —probably Jen-
kins or Brown —persuaded him that he
should rush into politics and proceed to
regenerate Australia. He wrote a very long
letter to the editor of this paper announcing
his new mission. It read like the sort of
address which Bernadotte used to deliver to
the French army, yet there was in it a
convincing streak of sincerity. After that
the pleasant musical verse-writer became a
laborious pounder-out of patriotic screeds.
He stood for various constituencies without
success, and he got mixed up with politicians,
Labor leagues, Liberal leagues, wire-pullers
and all manner of sundries. His size and
his lack of ballast blighted his political
career. Besides, he never had a political
career. Finally he seems to have resorted
to devious ways of raising the wind. It is
a pity that the man who wrote “Blacksmith
Once” should be doing two years’ “hard,”
but when a person rushes into the fringe of
public life without the necessary equipment
anything may happen.
That reminds “The Oldest Inhabitant”:
Died on February 4, 1915, William Byrne Hurley,
manager of the Bank of N. S. Wales at Campbell-
town, of which he was a native. His father, John
Hurley, came to Australia as a lad and worked for Cap-
tain Murray, grandfather of the present Lieutenant-
Governor of Papua, at Lake George. In the first
’3o’s John settled at Campbelltown and kept a pub;
then he took up the country on which now stands
the important railway junction town of Cootamundra.
(His station property was named Cootamundry.)
John Hurley was in the third, fifth, seventh, eighth
and ninth Parliaments (1859-1880). There was
another and a iater John Hurley, but he was of, a
different “color.” The son was manager of the
Bank of N. S. Wales from 1880. The business was
conducted in the house in which he was born and
in which John Hurley had kept the pub. And that
recalls a story of Henry Stuart Russell’s. He
accepted an invitation to visit his friend, Captain
Allman, at Campbelltown. Said he: “Towards the
break o’ day I retired to my quarters, at the only
inn in the place, John Hurley’s. From beneath the
windows of the sleeping apartments, three in number,
in one of which was my bed, the shingled roof of
an outhouse sloped to within a few feet of the ground.
Unwilling to disturb the inmates at such an hour, I
crawled up this roof. As I gently pushed up the
window a terrible voice assailed me: “Stand, or I
fire!” I fell back, for the only object visible was
a white night-cap. I rushed to the next and thence
fled, repulsed by a female shriek. There had been no
travellers at the inn when I had left it; but in the
morning I got a message: “Sir Thomas Mitchell did
not know that it was you last night. W ill you come
down and breakfast with him?” Sir Thomas Mitchell?
I had last seen him in London !
BIRTHDAY GREETINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The President of the U.S., Mr. Woodrow Wilson,
sent congratulations to the Kaiser on his 56th birth-
day.
Greet we Royal William now,
Brand of Cain upon his brow !
Lo! his hands are bloody red,
Curse of nations on his head !
Homeless children sobbing sadly,
Childless mothers, wailing madly,
Lift his glory on the highways,
Tell his story in the byways ;
Tell of Belgian towns a-flame,
Havoc, deeds without a name ;
Fire and slaughter, death and pillage,
Blackened fields and ruined village ;
Gaul ensanguined, riven Poland,
Bloody Yser, flooded lowland —•
All the work of him, I trow,
With the brand upon his brow !
Fifty-six his royal years!
Oceans of salt, bitter tears,
Trenches thick with dead arraign him,
All the wounds of millions stain him.
Pity her, his hapless mother,
Her whose son hath Cain for brother ;
Her whose pangs and bitter labor
Only wrought a Human Sabre!
Pity all who live a-near him.
All the bleeding lands that fear him ;
And when pitying seems to pall
Pity him the most of all
Who by Fate and Fury driven
Fares insensate, rent and riven,
Till his haughty throne and crown fall
In the crash of Death and Downfall.
Yet e’en Pity turns away
From him on his natal day.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

If you have tears prepare to shed them now.
The Sydney Ferries’ dividend has been
reduced to 7 p.c. “Owing to the heavy
falling-off in the revenue due to the war,’
the directors have reduced both it and the
steamer services. “When business becomes
normal again the Board hopes to revert to
the former dividend,” it adds. The rever-
sion to the former dividend is much more
important, you see, than the reversion to
the old time-table.
The co. still pays 7 p.c. Since it is
suffered to carry on a public service, should
it be permitted to pay any more? Of
course 7 p.c. is not a big dividend. But the
7 p.c. in this case is a fake. Of the present
capital of £46(5,666 only £220.066 has been
actually paid up by the shareholders. The
other £246,666 consists of profit capitalised.
Thus, on the money actually put up by
shareholders the present return is 144 p.c.
The investor in a co. certainly expects to
get more than 6 p.c.—more even than 7 p.c.
—as it goes along. He knows that in the
ordinary course it will make money which
it will not divide, and this money, being
invested in the business,_ will make more
money; and so the dividend will slowly
creep up to 7,8, 10, perhaps 15 p.c. That
was the old idea. Then a new one came
along. Why not, it was asked, capitalise
some of the'profits, so that the shareholders
might increase their dividend without
increasing the rate of dividend? In view
ot the fact that you don’t always want your
customer to know that you are making a
15 p.c. div. out of him, this seemed an excel-
lent scheme. Besides, it was good because,
if you felt that way, you could sell the extra
share-interest. But the Sydney Ferries and
some other mueh-too-greedy people want to
have things both ways. They want not
only to water the capital but also to increase
the rate of dividend. There is no reason or
justice in this, and the thing would not
have been tolerated if the recent Govern-
ments of N. S. Wales had not been so poor
as to fall almost beneath contempt. The
Governments being what they have been,
this and similar cos. have been encouraged
to believe that they have a right to eat
their cake and have it too. The result is
that investors have been induced to nay
ridiculous prices for these shares. The
time undoubtedly will come when they will
get a shock, for a Government will say to
them: “Increase, if you like, your dividends
on your original capital as undivided profits
are accumulated. But if you capitalise
your reserves, you mustn’t expect your rate
of dividends to ascend too.”
Assuming that public ownership is out
of the question for the present, the proper
thing is to apply to such services as this
the principles that Britain and other civilised
places have applied to gas. N. 8. Wales
did something of the sort recently. If the
co. has £500,000 of assets, whether pur-
chased with capital or undivided profit, it is
entitled to a fair return on the whole lot.
That is to say, it is entitled to make its
capital equal to its net assets, and then get
a fair dividend on the total. If the com-
munity controlled this public utility the
expected net return would not exceed as an
average 44 p.c. The N.S.W. trams reached
high-water mark in 1912, when they paid
£4 12s. Bd. p.c. Seeing, then, that of the
Sydney Ferries’ assets £182,756 is landed
property, which is to a great extent unpro-
ductive, 7 p.c. would be a fair maximum
return on that co.’s assets. This would he
50 p.c. more than the public would be
expected to provide if the State or muni-
cipalities owned the business; and 50 p.c.
is surely enough to pay for the blessings of
Private Enterprise. If the State is called
on to regulate the dividends, charges, etc.,
of gas cos., it is equally called on to regulate
ferry cos. ; and the investor who buys Sydney
Ferries on the assumption that the old.
cheerful watering and re-watering will be
continued to the end of things, will pre-
sently find he has been mistaken. The only
thing really in doubt is as to the maximum
rate of dividend that will be fixed. There
will no doubt be a struggle to make it
10 p.c. on old capital; but. in view of the
nature of the assets, it is very doubtful
whether it will be fixed beyond 8 p.c. even
if it reaches that amount.

-By successive waterings the capital hits been
made lip to £250,000; so the co. doesn’t con-
fess to anything better than a poor 40 p.c.
dividend last year. That it did so well as
that is almost miraculous, seeing that there
are few places outside France and Germany
which have been more disturbed by the war
than Broken Hill. Silverton itself, from which
the co. takes its name, has long been
quite dead; and the co. would have been
dead too, if Broken llill, 16 miles or so further
on, hadn’t been found to supplement the trivial
but rich surface deposits of which Silverton
was the drinking centre. Seeing, then, that
the co. took big risks and was saved only by
a fluke, Tiie Bulletin is prepared to admit
that it is entitled to something more than
a 5 p.c. dividend. But 2674 p.c. seems a
bit excessive. Some captious phople might
complain about even 214 p.c.
Naturally you would say that a co. which
pays a dividend of that size must be one of
the worst sharks in the world. As a matter
of fact, it is such a gentle shark that, you
don’t feel it biting you. You complain that
the freights must be excessive. It pro-
duces its books and shows you that for its
30-odd miles it really charges you less than
the S. Aus. Government would if it carried
the same goods for 30-odd miles. The secret
of the big figures lies in this: it makes up a
full train at one terminus and runs it right
through to the other. There is no running
half-empty trains, to possibly pick up pas-
sengers or freight 100 miles along the line;
no 10s. worth of shunting to drop or take
up ss. worth of freight en route. It charges
as for 30-odd miles, whereas that journey is
really an integral part of a 300-mile journey.
The S. Aus. Government might run the
freight the extra 30 miles for Is. The co.,
treating the stuff as though it were com-
mencing a new journey, charges perhaps
2s. 6d. Hence the majestic dividends.
The Broken Hill cos. are gradually finding
markets for their lead, but the prospects for
the zinc business are nearly as gloomy as they
were a week after the war started. Indeed,
there were hopes then that the smelting busi-
ness might be lifted bodily out of German
hands and put into English and Australian.
Apparently England has done nothing, and,
besides opening up negotiations with Jhe
United States, all that Australia has done
has been to add a few tons weekly to the
output of the B. H. Prop.’s baby works at
Port Pirie. If we must rely on Continental
works it may be years before Broken llill
finds a market again for its zinc concen-
trates. Meantime Broken Hill and this rail-
way co. will go short of that product which
during recent years has put the gilt on their
gingerbread. On the other hand, if the
tailings can’t be turned into money, they
won’t eat anything while they are waiting
for a market. The first column in the
table shows how greatly profits have in-
creased during the past few years, and that
boom has been largely due to the treatment
of accumulations of zinc tailings. The
accumulation now going on provides the
material for lengthening the boom when it
re-starts.
The co.’s liabilities are not worth men-
tioning —under £20,000. On the other side,
apart from fixed assets (line, rolling stock,
etc.), there are items, mostly liquid, totalling
£261,737. The capital is thus intact, exclu-
sive altogether of the value of the stock,
works and general undertaking. The only fly
in the ointment, apart from the uncer-
tainties inseparable from a mining field,
is the possibility of State competition. The
N.S.W. Government has at last started the
line to connect the present railway system
with Broken Hill. If a connecting link is
made with the S. Australian system—and it
seems certain that this link in a strategic
railway won’t be left in private hands —it
may be very awkward for this co. In any
case, some traffic will be diverted eastward.

The Vic. Government promises to pass a
Gas Bill through the new Parliament; but
this co. doesn’t seem scared. When a co. is
overburdened with profits and doesn’t want
to increase the rate of dividend, the handiest
plan is to issue more shares —free, if pos-
sible. but if not free at least at par. This
co., however, apparently doesn’t mind any-
one knowing that it pays a 13 p.c. dividend.
Hence when it wanted money lately, instead
of issuing 20,000 shares at par to bring in
£lOO,OOO, it sold only 10.000. but got a
premium of very nearly £ for £. The result,
of course, will be that, though the div. on
(he new shares will be nominally 13 p.c., in
reality the return on the new capital will
be only 0 2-3 p.c.
In fact, the 13 p.c. on old and new capi-
tal alike is misleading. The present share
capital is £907,210 and £513.745 has been
paid in premiums; hence the 13 p.c. is really
an 8i p.c. div. on the money actually put
into the business by shareholders. No doubt
when the new Gas Act arrives and limits
the rate of dividend, the co. will be allowed
to first capitalise its reserves. Then the
capital will consist of old share capital plus
premium capital plus additions to plant,
etc., made out of profits, after providing for
depreciation. In other words, presumably
the capital on which the co. will be entitled
to pay dividends will be the net value of its
assets. We have no idea what that will be.
The reserves shown in the table are not much
to go by, because hundreds of thousands of
pounds'of profit has been put into hidden
reserve, having been spent on new works.
Over £350,000 has been thus stuffed away in
the past, seven years. Now, some of this no
doubt is a set-off against depreciation. But
repairs and renewals are also charged direct
against revenue; so that the £50,000 or a
trifle more a year hasn’t to cover the whole
cost of replacement. As a matter of fact,
in the past 10 years the extensions charged
to capital have amounted to only £154,105
net; the total has gone up from £2,171,745
to £2,325,850. All the rest of the work
done in the past 10 years has been paid for
out of profits. And how extensive those net
additions have been may be more or less
judged by the fact that in that period the
receipts for gas consumed by meter have
gone from £138,091 to £290,493 —a half-
year’s figures in each case. If is just as
certain as anything can be that the co.’s
fair net profits at present are much in excess
of the 13 p.c, dividend. Under the N.S.W.
Act a co. (at the outset, at any rate) may
stuff away 2 p.c. of the capital outlay for
“general purposes.” The co. values its
assets at £2,325,850. Two p.c. of that =
£46,517. The year’s profit was £209.501.
Deducting the £46,517 there would thus,
under the new law in N. S. Wales, be
£162,984 to divide. This would allow of a
larger division than the co. now makes. It
is to be observed that the Act in N. S. Wales,
though it will no doubt prevent the cos. from
growing any more dangerous claws, had the
immediate effect of putting up the total
amount distributed. The ehances are that
the same thing will happen in Melbourne.
At any rate, there seems to be the money
there to do it with, though the whole pretty
scheme would be spoiled if Parliament made
a savage cut at the standard price of gas.

But perhaps this gives a better idea of the
co.’s recent history :
Dec.,’o4. Dec.,’o9. Dec.,’l4.
Liquid assets .. .. £2,718 £644 £3954

In 10 years the co. has shaken off the debt
which was incurred in 1904-5 in the recon-
struction of the theatre, and has put by
£3757. In the last five years it has paid the
24 p.c. div. and laid up £4854. besides spend-
ing a bit on re-decoration; and £4-54 is
equal to 2 p.c. p.a. However, it mustn’t be
reckoned that the co. is making 2 p.c. net
profit in addition to the dividend. For one
thing there is the return of capital to pro-
vide for, because the co.’s building is on a
lease which expires somewhere about the
middle of the century. Hitherto no proper
provision has been made for sinking fund.
But things should be better from this
onward. The J.C.W. people have a lease
that carries the co. on to 1928, and they pay
a higher rent for the last 10 years. Also
they are to pay for all repairs. There is
thus every reason to expect that, in addition
to providing the 24- p.c. dividend, lost time
will be made up in connection with the
sinking fund. Without being imprudent it
may even be possible to also give the share-
holders a trifle more to go on with.

In the first half of 1913 shareholders were
asked whether they would take a 334 p.c.
bonus in hard cash or in shares at par. As
the shares were then standing much above
par they naturally said they would take the
insult in that shape, lienee the increase of
capital from £45.000 to £OO,OOO. This was
managed without any reduction of visible
reserves. Up to that time the land at
Lithgow, freehold and leasehold, had been
written down to £20,584, and it was valued
low to start with. The co. wrote this asset
up, and in the next accounts it stood at
£40,504. That provided the extra assets to
stand against the extra capital. But appar-
ently the writing up was done very modestly ;
for now that £l5OO has been got for a piece
of the freehold, the co. treats the whole of
it as divisible profit, and pays out a bonus
of 124 p.c. in addition to the usual div. at
the rate of 10 p.c. p.a. Shareholders can’t
therefore complain of being starved lately.
Certainly there was a time when western
coal and poverty meant much the same
thing. At last, the owners formed the Lith-
gow Coal Association to handle their output
and stop the old undercutting: and on top
of that came the development _of Lithgow
as a manufacturing centre. Besides getting
indirect benefit from the manufactures the
co. two or three years ago decided to be in
that swim itself. Brickworks were the
result; and something over £12,500 has been
put into that business. For last year the
brick factory is credited in Profit and Loss
with £882; for the previous half-year with
£215. But whether interest is first charged
on the outlay isn’t plain. Doubtless it isn't
intended to be plain, In either case the
position is charming. This is how things
have moved in 12 months: —

That accumulation of £BBB5 is the result of
12 months’ work after paying the dividends
and bonus and providing for the year’s addi-
tions and replacements.

An absolutely “clean” balance-sheet at last!
Starting 254 years ago with £57,500 of
water in a capital of £60,000- —that is to
say, with only £2500 of tangible assets —the
co. has gradually put by surplus profits until
to-day the last of the goodwill has
been replaced by hard, solid wealth.
Before the payment of the present dividend
the co. owned £65,000, represented as fol-
lows :
Cash and deposits .. .. £20,352
Advances on properties. . . . 48,300
Book debts and oddments . . 7,998
£76,650
Less liabilities .. . . .. 11,447
Net assets . . . . . . . . £65,203
If the advances on properties are revenue-
producing, as presumably they are, the co.
should make more than half its dividend out
of interest earnings. This would leave only
£lO5O net to be earned from commissions in
a half-year; and things would have to be
bad indeed if the co. couldn’t do as much as
that. No doubt they are going to be pretty
bad. Some people tried hard for two or three
months to get the public to believe that
the Sydney land boom had merely taken a
breather after Willie had landed his punch :
but Sydney knew better. Still R. and W.
are not so much interested in the subdivi-
sion business. They go in rather for big-
property transactions; and though there will
he a slump in that line too, it won’t be so
bad a slump as in the business of selling
very desirable suburban allotments as far
away from Sydney as a well-trained crow
could fly in a couple of hours. In 1903,
which was a shockingly bad year in Sydney,
11. and W.’s profits reached £3801; and the
presept extra earnings from interest should
add £BOO to that, even if things get as bad
as thev were then.
