ROYDS, LTD., Investors’ Advisers, Challis House, Sydney
so a shade under 15 p.c. in five years, or 3
p.c. p.a., has been earned for the ordinaries.
Very wisely this hasn’t been distributed ;
for in a business of this sort and of this size
£7OO a year isn’t too much to put aside for
contingencies. However, the thing really-
ought to do better. Originally too much
dependence was put on ice-skating. The
Glaciariuin had the usual short boom; then
it got down to mere bread-and-butter —and
the war scraped the butter off at the end
of last season. Seeing that the Glaciarium
could be nothing better than a side-line, the
directors tried to push the ice business ; but
there isn’t much profit in that in Melbourne.
There ought to be more in cold-storage ; and
additional facilities for welcoming the frozen
lamb and the frost-bitten cow are no doubt
responsible for the better showing made
last year. Mainly because the early calcu-
lations of the co. were all amiss its finances
have never been very healthy. At the end
of the year it owed £12,656 on a mortgage
and about £3OOO otherwise. Its assets con-
sist of a freehold, which cost it £7359;
plant, etc., which have eaten up £50,006,
but have been written down by £9600; and
£3548 of sundries.
* * * *
Concerning another man who has had an
anniversary
A couple of weeks ago solicitor Barry, one of the
directors of the City Mutual Life Society, moved at
the Board meeting - that felicitations be extended to
Mr. Crowley on the 25th anniversary of his manage-
ment, and that his salary be raised by £750 a year.
Another director, Dr. Beattie, seconded ; and the
chairman of the co., the veteran Pat Hogan, fer-
vently said amen. Crowley thanked the Board,
declined the offer, and suggested that the staff should
have the £750. This recalls another occasion. A
disgruntled ex-employee accused Crowley and his
Board of corruption. The late Jack Want appeared
for Crowley. He produced a minute book showing
that the late John Hardie, a one-time city Mayor
and then a director, had moved a resolution, seconded
by “Toby” Barton, at that time another director,
increasing secretary’s Crowley’s salary by £l5O, and
showing, further, that Mr. Crowley had gratefully
declined on the ground that he was young and could
wait. The case had got to the High Court. Pidding--
ton was the counsel for the other side. One of the
“corrupt” acts —the main one, it appeared—was
that Crowley had allowed as the surrender value of
a friend’s policy £2O more than the sum set out in
the book of surrender values in use by the society.
Said Griffith, looking at Bidding-ton: “The inference
is that Crowley and his friend shared this £20?”
“Yes, your Honor.” “You heard the evidence given
in the Court below respecting this offer of the
Board to increase Crowley’s salary by £l5O per
annum, and you still wish the Court to believe that
Crowley corruptly allowed the extra £2O in order to
share it with his friend?” Piddington said they
were his instructions. “Well, all I can say,” said
Griffith, decisively, “is that it is an insult to any
mean intelligence to be asked to believe such a
thing.” And Crowley was vindicated.
Just before the war started a conference
was held in London to arrange matters in
connection with the mining and marketing
or diamonds. Present: L. Oppenheimer,
Gustav Imrotli, Stauch, Adolf Wagner,
Meyer Gerhard, Pasle, L. Breitmeyer, L.
Reyersbach, 11. Mosentlial, Carl Meyer,
Hirschorn, M. A. Viallate, P. Ross Frames,
J. C. Sheridan. All of them Briddish, of
course—except perhaps Frames and Sheri-
dan. And, in the natural and proper order
of things, those two are pushed down to the
bottom.
* * * *
Spelter, £3B 15s. It makes the mouth of
every Broken Ilill director water. But it
is no good to him. Except for a little made
by the B. LI. Prop, no spelter is produced
in Australia, and there are no facilities for
producing it. We have been content, in-
stead. to export our ore —the best available
in large quantities in the world—to Ger-
many, to build up that nation’s metallurgical
supremacy, and provide it with the means
whereby our sons are killed.
* * * *
The war had no sooner started than
general-manager Del-prat, of the B. 11. Prop.,
tore away to Europe to look for new markets
for the co.’s products. An agent was also
sent to the U.S. Finding he could do noth-
ing in Europe so far as “zincs” are con-
cerned, the American business was pushed
hard; and the soaring market for spelter
helped the negotiations, wifh the result that
bottoms have been secured for 45,000 tons
; —not much, but sufficient to justify the co.
in re-starting its flotation plant at Broken
Hill.
Elsmore (N.S.W.) is one of the Clark-
Mackay Melbourne group of Tingha tin-
sluicing cos. that had to shut down when
the war broke out. Since then a shaft
on the course of the deep lead where
tributors did their little best some time
ago represents work done. The fall
in the gutter is the excuse given for
the dirt not being payable, but another
shaft lower down ought to tell whether the
tin was washed on to a more level bottom.
Of the co.’s two dredges No. 1 started
early the year, but No. 2 continues to
rest. With tin on the soar again there is
no excuse for idleness when the new pad-
dock ahead will provide a continuous run
for 18 months to come.
The most important thing said at New
Buiragorang (N.S.W.) meeting was that
the vendors had agreed to accept 25 p.c.
instead of 50 p.c. of the profits set aside
for dividends. The liability is £l5OO, so
£6OOO will have to be available for division
before the debt is off the slate. The little
mine has produced 561 tons, valued at
£10,659, and more than half of that has come
from the high silver values. No one knows
if the rich ore met with between No. 5 and
No. 6 levels will continue at deeper levels ;
but it is most dangerous to expect uni-
formity in these rich streaks.
•* * > * *
Occidental (N.S.W.) sent 1197 tons of
Bdwt. stuff to the battery in 14 days. The
battery produced 17 tons of concentrates,
estimated to contain 140 oz. ; and 999 tons of
slimes, originally containing 3070 z„ were
cyanided for 2340 z. At 80s. per oz. that
amounts to £936, which added to the value
in concentrates makes a total return of
£1496. But costs are stiff, and the directors
don’t appear in any hurry to open deeper
levels, so the market is sick at about 3s.
Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) intends to
block out wash dirt again where the lead
has been developed and prepared for the
extraction of the wash. The purchase price
for Gordon’s property was £25.000, of which
£14,245 had been paid off to July, 1914. The
royalty is 25 p.c. of the tin value, so if no
other arrangement is made £40,000 worth of
tin will have to he raised to wipe off the
debt.
* * * *
As a counterblast to “bear” statements
anent the Edna May, a mining engineer who
frankly likes the show sends the following:
The reef is in granite country. In fact, it
is surrounded by granite, except the bent
portion at the south-west end, which comes
in contact with the greenstone. The granite
bars, of which so much has been heard, are
of later origin than the reef, and in no way
affect the deposition of gold. Neither do
they physically affect the reef to any extent.
The only thing they have done so far is to
replace the reef at the point of their inter-
section with it, wherefore so much less ore is
available for breaking and treatment.
Further than this, the bars have not done
any damage, and are not likely to do any
in the future. These bars range from 3ft.
to 10ft. in thickness, have a south-easterly
dip, and appear to decrease in width with
depth, or, in other words, as they approach
the reef. Why granite should be taken so
seriously by some holders of the stock is
hard to understand, as several successful
gold mines have been associated with this
class of rock in Australia. As to permanency
in deeper ground, the prospects of the Edna
reef are distinctly good.
Fremantle Trading Co., which owns some
metal mines at Northampton (W.A.), and
does a bit of smelting for the public as a
side-line, has issued its report for the year
ended July 31, 1914. In view of the ad-
verse influence which the war has had on
the co.’s mining operations, no less than
£22,538 has been written off plant and de-
velopment, leaving £ll5 at credit of profit
and loss. Of the co.’s own ore 22,968 tons
were treated during the year for 2707 tons
metallic lead, 83« z. gold and 30460 z. silver,
the total value being £51,200. The co. is
now raising ore and getting advances on it
from the State.
Chaffers (W.A.) tells a tale of woe. The
officials, it appears, considerably under-
estimated the cost of the new plant, and two
disappointing returns coming on top of this
expensive blunder landed the co. in diffi-
culties. Some of the directors and their
friends then st'epped in, and, “to protect
the interests of shareholders,” advanced
£3650 to pay pressing liabilities and took
first mortgage debentures as security. Not-
withstanding this, Perth creditors are ask-
ing the Supreme Court to wind up the co.
and let them in ahead. How much of the
75,000 tons of 30s. sd. ore estimated to be
in sight 24 years ago really exists, and what
has become of the dozen and one rich shoots
of ore which were subsequently alleged to
have been uncovered are matters which are
bound to be inquired into shortly.
Star Exploration (W.A.) cl nuns to have
earned £lB6B during year ended September
30. Expenditure absorbed £1431. The
surplus (£436) and the balance brought in
(£79) has been written off plant and
machinery. Share interests held by the co.
are practically identical with those in the
safe 12 months ago. for although a number
of ventures were dangled before the Board
during the past year it did not bite at any
of them.
Oroya Links (W.A.) intimates to share-
holders in a belated circular that on the
outbreak of the war and consequent uncer-
tainty of getting supplies, it curtailed de-
velopment and devoted most of its energies
to stripping the 900 ft. level north and carry-
ing a leading stope at 750 ft. in the Eclipse
section of the property. As a result it
claims that the shoot at 900 ft. has been
proved for 100 ft. in length to average 325.
over a width of 16ft., and the values in the
750 ft. workings to extend much farther than
indicated in the drive and to be much above
the average grade of the mine. The treat-
ment of a larger percentage of sulpho-
telluride ore has interfered with the ex-
traction, and on the date of the circular-
about five weeks ago-—efforts were being
made to remedy this defect.
Another 3s. div. is announced by the
Edna May (W.A.). Stone worth 2oz. is
still reported, but yields don’t get up to
that average. Still they work out very
nicely, as 3030 tons last crushing gave
27110 z., and 2700 tons residues cyanided
gave bullion worth £2087. The whole of
the month’s output was valued at £12,931,
and the profit was £BBB6. Results were
thus better than for Dec., though not much.
The co. is now punching into the gneissie
schist to see if it keeps its place to the
next leveh

People interested in oil-well drilling in
South Aus. are asking why geologist Ward’s
report on the South-East is held back.
There is not a geological section of
the oil country in existence, and the
people who are drilling to discover what the
Government should have learned before
this are irritated to find that Mr. Ward.’s
name is spelled something like “oyster.”
Boring has been started by T. J.
Whaley’s Ocean Oil Co. at Kingston, and
by the South Aus. Oil Wells Co., eight miles
from Robe. Von Doussa, M.L.C., a director
of the latter, has been showing in Melbourne
a pure specimen of paraffin wax alleged to
have been found in situ in its proper matrix
inland from the sea, between Port Mac-
Donnell and Beachport. Australian Oil
Concessions has been underwritten in Syd-
ney, and the Standard Oil Wells Co. is being
formed to secure 640 acres next Whaley’s
bore at Kingston.
The Powlett North colliery is the only pri-
vate show that Ex-Minister Peter Mcßride
would allow at Wonthaggi when he started
the State Coal mine; but though he allowed
it to live The Bulletin cannot imagine
him forking out for it £5OOO as a State
grant. Yet that help is actually in the
wind. McLeod, M.L.A., says that what
weighed with the Minister was the number
of men that would be employed if the State
forked out the money. Ex-Minister Mcßride
would have rejoined that if the State cash
was to do so much the State had better take
the ground. But Peacock’s crowd isn’t built
that way.
* * * *
A' fresh tint suffuses Golden Pyke, since
if has been realised that the slump which
followed the boom hadn’t shaken all the ore
out of the old Pyke shoot of stone. There
never was 355., let alone 495., in sight in all
the mine actually proved or probable, and
the public got shot as usual. The grade of
the stone ought to have told the tale: but
a 7dwt. average was bolstered by the old
record of £93,000 dividends paid. The
present “highly remunerative” patch in the
mine is in the shallow stopes at 367fc. But
lOin. or Ift. won’t make big profits. As
those in the show usually slide out on a
firm market, the tip to quit is when the
buying fever is at its height. That may be
reached on a flutter over Chadwicks or some
little development in the deeper levels on
the Pyke reef.

John Simpson is doing cheaper work for
the Oaks than any other way-back Queens-
land field can swap lies about. For four
weeks to January 23 he put through 1040
tons for ss. lid. per ton —mining (including
fuel and stores), 2s. lOd. ; milling, 3s. Id.
As the 1040 tons gave only 1270 z., and as
Oaks gold isn’t worth more than 705., costs
have to be fine. Still there was close up
£l5O on the right side for the month.
* * * *
“Glassy Glance”: For the sake of the
whisky I have listened to a good deal of
skite by tin-dredge managers about the mil-
lions of yards of dirt they have sluiced
in so many hours, as if yardage was the
only essential. With very few exceptions
in my experience have they paid the least
attention to the much-more important factor,
vis., a process of concentration that will
ensure a maximum recovery of mineral con-
tents. Calculations based on the yardage
treated and the tin won are not an infallible
guide to the quality of the ground, nor the
capacity of the manager. With proper
facilities (and they exist on most dredging
propositions), and with an intelligent system
of sluicing, a pump can be worked with
much better results than ordinarily obtain.
To elaborate slightly, the process of con-
centration must begin almost from the
moment the jet strikes the face; and it is
safer not to rely too much on the saving
power of 100 ft. or so of iron sluices,
assisted by the ridiculously futile efforts of
a boxman. Another hoary superstition is
that the manager who procures a 75 p.c.
assay for his tin is the dizzy limit of mana-
gerial capacity. As a matter of fact, the
insane desire for perfection in this resp'ect
very often leads to the loss of a lot of good
60 to 70 p.c. ore, and not infrequently not a
little of the 75 p.c. ore also. This, by the
way, has been a godsend to the fossiekers.
who do not turn uo their noses at the
poorer-grade ore. Neither do the ore buyers.
* * * *
The Philippine rake of dredges, in which
John McWliae and other Melbourne people
are interested, got on to poor stuff last
month, the yield from all the plants being
only 5380 z. against llTGoz. for December.
John didn’t blench, even when No. 4 showed,
that the bottom was as barren of wash as
a vulture’s neck is of feathers. Nearly all
the plants ran short time, but everything
is to improve from now on.
Walla roo and Moonta, which made a 20
p.c. cut in wages when the Madman knocked
the bottom out, now restores half of it. the
copper market having improved so much.

The Freeman and the Pratten interests
in Malaysia have been severed. 11. E.
Pratten originally went to the Malay States
for what is now Austral-Malay Tin. Ltd.,
and picked up two or three properties which
look good. A. W. Freeman was the mining-
man attached to the co. Lately it has been
made clear that the two interests would be
happier apart. Pratten and his friends
therefore sold their shares in Austral-
Malay ..to the others, and have retired also
from the Kampong Ivamunting Board, while
the two Freemans and Frank Leverrier,
Iv.C., have left the Larut Board. J. M.
Newman replaces A. W. Freeman as mining-
engineer on the Larut directorate, and will
make himself familiar with the co.’s problems
by an early inspection.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, February 8, 1915.
My Dear Arini, —
The British pressman has reason to thank
his stars, for the new assistant censor, Sir
Edward Cook, is a journalist. He was a
correspondent during the South African
trouble years ago. Since then he lias com-
bined more serious literary efforts with the
usual Fieet-street grind, which included a
spell as editor of the Dairy News. He is
married to Emily, one of the numerous
Baird sisters, of whom the lanky Dorothea,
11. B. Irving’s wife, is one, and Mrs. B.
R. Wise another.
The Naval College at Jervis Bay is to be
officially opened this month to young gentle-
men in white ducks and saucy cadet coats.
Ex-Flag-Lieutenant Seton, who used to
shadow Admiral King-Hall, has left (tem-
porarily or otherwise) the training craft
Tingira, and is now at the Jervis Bay
school. The academy is approached via a
bog or bush moat (without the Grange that
Tennyson supplied to Mariana) in bad
weather. Still, the official ceremony is not
likely to be attended by any casualty except
to the marines.
Senator Pearce has hinted (not too soon)
that the wives, sisters, cousins, aunts and
sweethearts of Australian officers in Egypt
are not going to be accommodated with a
dress-circle seat at the Suez fray. In fact,
local Society, which has very crude ideas
under its chiffons, is fairly certain to be
coldly returned to its Exclusive Suburbs if
if gets within coo-ee of the sandy Egyptian
stadium. Side winds must have blown the
wide-awake Minister for Defence a hint of
a much-talked-of “Cairo season.” So a few
tons of feminine finery are about to be —
unpacked for home consumption.
An exodus to Tasmania is among the
social events of this flabby month. Aus-
tralia’s great lawyer, Samuel Griffith, is
cooling his learned brain in the jam-tin
ir.liuivi. So is our Chief Justice Cullen, who
usually camps half-way up Mount Welling-
ton during the long Christmas vacation.
Actors’ Day at the White City cooled off
under the influence of a southerly, and the
crowd went joyfully off to bump itself over
a scenic railway, or buy things from the
profesh. Frances Ross, in an airy-looking
garment of black lace and white shimmer,
cold flowers in the Allies’ stall. Next door
A theatrical gent, in a crushed-mulberry suit
bad a small side-show that was said to be
worth quite twice the entrance money.
Nellie Stewart, under the brim of a large
hat, also tied up posies and sold them at
fair market value. She had for company
Dorothy Brunton, who was herself tied up
in a floating gossamer. Manager Hugh
Ward strolled round, and George Titheradge,
president of the Actors’ Association, chap-
eroned Governor Strickland through the
mazy grounds. Beaumont Smith’s frolic-
some company gave one of the best side-
shows of the afternoon.
Fred Niblo beat a drum and addressed
the crowd in stirring language outside
another show pavilion. But there is such
wit in Niblo’s gags that the crowd enjoyed
about three hours of the comedian’s extem-
pore performance outside better than the
prospect of paying a shilling to go inside.
Jack Cannot, another strenuous toiler, must
be sore all over —particularly in his wind-
pipe—after his exertions.
Some Sydney folks who are supposed to
be partaking of rural life and bridge at
Bowral bobbed up. Mrs. George Earp, with
her young brood under her wing, sampled
various barn-storming events. Mrs. Spencer
Brunton and two daughters also stalked the
sights. Mrs. Prescott and a fair-haired Miss
Shaw, supported by a Polish eagle
outspread on Miss Csaykowska’s national
flag, sold anything they could lay their
hands on at the top of a flight of wooden
stairs.
There was a crisis in sugar in the tea-
room about 4 p.m., and those who habitually
took three lumps wore wintry smiles. The
two Brownlow girls, who were assisting,
tried to succor the sugarless in striped and
plain cotton frocks.
Actor Walter Bentley, who is the official
nib of the Actors’ Association, ambled peace-
fully round the innumerable foot tracks,
where the plain citizen’s brain hums like a
top, owing to the dizzy whirl of revolving
scenery. As an antidote it’s a fine thing to
watch the passionate desire of another sec-
tion of the public to fall down precipitous
canvas mountains in a long wooden slioof.
The recent route march of 2000 men in
khaki was a small affair compared with the
last big display of Australian tan. But the
men look very brown and fit, and I would
just as soon not be a Turk for the next
six months, if you really don't mind.
Eric Mugliston, son of the late Dr. Mug-
liston. barrister, joined the expeditionary
forces in England as a private. News
comes now that he has been promoted, to a
commission in the Royal Field Artillery.
11 is first training in arms was as a school
cadet in Sydney.
The local artists’ war contributions will
be chiefly in kind —pictures or art work in
some handy form, which the public will pay
for, incidentally, by subscription. Patriots
get something, if it’s only an impressionist
sketch of a back yard on washing day, for
their (‘ash, and the coin falls into the yawn-
ing war-bag. Artists Julian Ashton and
Lister Lister are pushing the scheme; and
hon. secretaries John Lane Mullins and
Oxnard Smith will receive your largest con-
tributions with their warmest welcome.
News of the engagement of another of
the nice Maddrell girls, of Braidwood,
trickles through from London. One of the
pretty sisters married young Downes, of
Brownlow Hill, a while ago. Now Coralie
is flashing an engagement ring, for which
soldier Kenneth Hinde, of the A.I.F. (I
haven’t the least idea which limb of the
British bulldog this indicates), is respon-
sible.
Governor Strickland and his Lady
Edeline are booked for a flying visit to
Kosciusko about February 17. They motor
first to Cooma. Thence they petrol up to
have a whiff of mountain air on the big
Monaro hilltop, and start for home a day
or two later. A limited number of the
vice-regal kindergarten will go aloft with
their parents.
11. Barnard, the man who talks Wedg-
wood china, is finishing his Australasian
tour with a run in Maoriland. He is travel-
ling right through that scenic country, and
is bound to give a discourse or two on the
particular brand of crockery he prefers on
his own dresser.
Outside the war zone the summer sale is
supplementing the heat wave as the most
stirring event of this month. Haunting the
bargain counter is a curious tradition: no
one has ever been able to state definitely why
Woman, at this time, insists on carrying
home all her parcels. Every tram is half-
filled with paper bags, and the übiquitous
suit-case also helps to keep one’s toes warm.
It is the season when women are filled with
a frenzied desire to purchase the things they
don’t want. The lure of llfd. must have
been invented by some imp below for the
destruction of domestic peace. Just now
the very vitals of feminine finance are
sapped by the fever to buy, and carry home,
something that costs 19 shillings and II
pennies and decimal 75 of another penny.
The local housewife is even beginning —
more power to her! —to make personally-
conducted tours among vegetables that are
sold at wholesale price. The result trickles
through the neat suit-case she bumps under
the tram seat in a fine nosegay of onions.
The three pictures recently added to the
National Gallery do not excite the average
spectator to wild enthusiasm, and the
trustees are not likely to be overwhelmed
with congratulations at giving house-room
to still another consignment of mediocre
stuff. Of the three, Shannon’s portrait of
a handsome pouting young woman is the
best. Campbell Taylor’s conventional pic-
ture. “Piquet,” represents the typical
British middle-class family having a very
dull evening at home. A portrait of 11. B.
Irving completes the trio. The subject is
depicted as a portly person with the general
appearance of a prosperous stockbroker.
The artist mercifully forebore painting
Irving as Hamlet —all knobs and acute
angles—and merely gives a frock-coat im-
pression of the gaunt frame.
Another wool man, W. Pitt Brown, of
Tasmania, recently of Durban, has offered
his services to the War Office. He has lat-
terly been looking after the S’African end of
the foolish business of exporting stud-sheep
from Tasmania. You’ve noticed, I sup-
pose, that there’s nobody in Australia to look
after the importation of ostriches from
S’Africa.
This week’s visitors included R. P. Vin-
cent, .an ex-Mayor of Perth, who has been
staying at the Australia with a sapling of a
son. When last glimpsed the pair were fly-
ing to the Mountains to look for a breeze.
Kenneth Stephen, a son of the late
barrister Cecil Stephen, was married the
other day to Dorothy Rennie, a member of
,the George Rennie family—descended from
that splendid old man who was for so long
Auditor-General of the State.
Hugh D. Mclntosh, of the Tivoli, supple-
menting the awards of the Royal Humane
Society, has struck a personal note of
appreciation for local bravery. Harold
Parle, who made an heroic effort to save
(lie life of his friend Tooze on January I.
was the object, and a gold medal the award.
The Tivoli stage was the scene of the little
ceremony, in which Frank Tudor, the visit-
ing Minister of Customs, played the decora-
tive part. It takes a special brand of courage
to swim out and literally drag a friend from
the snapping maw of a shark, as Parle did
in Little Sirius Cove, where he a'nd his
friend were bathing. Yet it is to such young
men who, for one reason and another,
haven’t gone to the war. that certain foolish
maidens propose to hand out white feathers!
Lena Bates, a Strathfield girl, got away
with the highest pass (S A’s) in the recent
Sydney intermediate public exam. This
distinction wasn’t earned by any boy, and
the Girls’ High School, of which Lena was
dux last year, is swelling with pride in con-
sequence.
A young colored potentate who rules in
East Africa is one of the most recent king-
lets to toss his goods into the European
melting-pot. Kabaka, of Buganda, was
taught by English tutors, and learned to
play golf and mingle soda-water with his
whisky in England. Now he asks to be
allowed to lead a native African regiment
against the common foe.
Mr. and Mrs. Freddy Shipman were at
latest at Chicago, the sty of the tinned hog.
The passing of Miss Braddon, the veteran
novelist, recalls the happy days of irrespon-
sible flapperhood. When “Lady Audley’s
Secret” was secretly gloated over in young
ladies’ boarding-schools the world was a
good deal younger, and the lurid imagination
of “Victoria Cross” and similar writers of
loud fiction were unknown quantities. Miss
Braddon, who was Mrs. Maxwell in private
life, was aunt to Mr. 11. Y. Braddon, general
manager of Dalgety’s in N. S. Wales. He
writes, too, but it is mostly on commercial
subjects for the benefit of the students in
economics at Sydney Uni.
Walter Massey Greene, who. represents
Richmond (N.S.W.) in the House of Reps.,
and can talk stock by the mile to his cow-
keeping constituents, was married in some
local splendor at St. James’s Church the
other evening. Miss Lulu Lomax, a
daughter of squatter Lomax, in the Tenter-
field district, was the bride.
In the slack season the Education Depart-
ment lightly turns to thoughts of omelettes.
Cookery demonstrations occur this week in
several big city State schools. Mothers
are urgently requested to step along, and
see innumerable digressions from the ever-
lasting frying-pan. Fathers ought to be
keenly interested in this show, for they put
away a good deal of cooked leather in the
course of a week. The high price of bullock
and the uppishness of the cutlet give an
extra value to these demonstrations, which
are aimed directly at butchers’ bills. The
Australian girl has yet to learn the uses of
macaroni and the French habit of making
soup out of the bone of a chicken and a
handful of herbs.
Dr. Russell Nolan, who lately went over
to the great majority at the age of 48, was
an Australian-born specialist of some emi-
nence. His “line” was the nose and throat.
An old Newington College boy, the son of a
Wesleyan parson, he married a daughter of
another Wesleyan parson, the conspicuous
Dr. Kelynack. In his profession he was
free of that commercial spirit which is
getting many of his specialist brethren so
cordiallv disliked. Yet he had a solid in-
stinct for values in landed property. A
good deal of the Poplar Grove estate at
Leura is or was in his keeping. He built
an ideal Australian bungalow with no
suburban frills or pepperpot towers in the
leafy glades thereabouts.
Though the Peace Society s.till holds
occasional meetings, and records blood-
thirsty resolutions against those who go to
war, it is trying to help, according to its
means. Naturally it looks for contributions
towards this object. But it doesn't circulate
its saucer with any great result. Up to
date, the Society’s Local Distress Fund has
only snared about £5O.
The Commonwealth Patriotic Association
is an infant society which pledges itself to
support British and Australian trade. Of
course it regards German goods as particu-
larly venomous; and any salesman who tries
the “made in Switzerland” dodge on the
members is liable to be spoken to in plain
figures. Some of the expert shoppers de-
clare that they would know goods made in
the enemy’s country with their eyes band-
aged. Perhaps. Still, wouldn’t it be better
to avoid the possibility of error by charging
so much on these goods at the Customs that
they couldn’t possibly come in?
The burly Scotchbyterian parson, John
Ferguson, has returned to his flock after a
few months’ holiday in troubled Britain. Big
John is the cleric who caused such a com-
motion amongst the more dour members of
the Scotch Assembly a few years ago. His
offence was an audience in Rome with Pope
Leo, and he stoutly told the theological hor-
nets that he’d do the same thing again if he
were lucky enough to get the chance. But
Leo has gone where even churchwardens
are at rest. Also, Europe being more or
less on its head, and armed to its teeth,
Parson Ferguson didn’t wait to leave his
card on Pope Benedict. He discourses on
war instead.„
The flounce which came back with such a
long prelude of discussion has scarcely made
its presence felt during the six months it
has been with us. It makes a mere slender
ghost of jts former outstanding fuss. And
the dreadful threats that the crinoline was
waiting round the corner have perished.
Paris is too busy to concern itself about
wire cages for ladies of leisure. Besides,
are there any leisurely ladies now? Even
duchesses are a-wing on the outer edge of
battle, to presently fly back and record their
emotions in print. ’Er Grace of Sutherland
has broken out already in a booklet called
“Six Weeks at the War.” —a title which
has a glorious ring of self-advertisement to
which poor human nature is prone. The
treasure hasn’t come this way, but: it must
be exhilarating reading—for those who have
not been nursed by the untrained duchesses
who haunt field hospitals.
A number of musical Australians now on
the other side lately cheered themselves up
by having their photos, taken together.
Donald Mcßeath, the young Sydney fiddler,
was among them ; also George Jennings, a
Melbourne tenor, whose beautiful moustache
has an Italian wave at the ends. This is
probably the result of recent residence in
Milan, where the Victorian was occupied in
acquiring polish.
A Maorilander named R. G. Blow has
been making a noise at the Royal Academy
in London. In 1018 he won the bronze
medal. Last year be took the silver medal
—the highest trophy the Academy pins on
the coat of aspiring youth. After three
years’ pursuit of harmony, the student has
gained his Licentiate. Young Blow, who is
a son of Maoriland’s Under-Secretary for
Public Works, left the Thames for his
native geysers on January 9.
The domestic problem appears to be _ a
fixture which even the greatest war in his-
tory cannot shift. In England the cry of the
Unemployed Woman seems to come from every
class except the domestic helper. She is as
comparatively hard to get in small house-
holds as she was 12 months ago. Also, she
is not taking kindly to the influx of foreign
refugees. News comes through private let-
ters of Mary Jane’s distinctly uppish con-
duct to those whom her employers welcome
as non-paying guests. Altogether, the
British matron is having a pretty rocky time
between the speeding of her male belongings
to the front and her efforts to smooth
Maria’s temper when it is ruffled by the
sound of foreign languages.
Harriet Newcomb, a former Sydney
school marm with a taste for politics (she
used to wave flags in suffragette proces-
sions), is finding a better outlet for her
restless energy. Amongst her war-time
occupations in London is some solid assist-
ance to the Babies Kit Society. This swarm
of busy bees has its hive in Sydney. Most
of the kits will go to London for Belgian in-
fants, and the almost-übiquitous Harriet is
waiting on the shore to hand them out. as
advised. Australian women are putting
into these tiny rags some of the very bes't
stitchery. When a careless soul falls short
of the standard, the hon. lady overseer looks
over the top of her spectacles for a patierit
Griselda who will unpick and re-make the
garment. Volunteers are always to be had
for this job. Still, it would be better for
Griselda’s temper, in this warm weather, if
ladies who make stitches as large as pot-
hooks would mercifully refrain from sewing
for defenceless Belgian orphans. Casli
would be handier, because it never has to b£
unpicked.
What freakish notions come to a head in
the way of societies for the propagation of
this and that. Sydney, for instance,
has a “Gold Brick Society.” The
members of this association, who have struck
the tremendously-novel idea of standing by
the Empire in the present trouble, will also
add to the temptations of the hard-worked
burglar. A small facsimile of a brick
glitters in gold on the watch chains of
the fraternity. Frequent patriotic out-
breaks will emanate from the wearers of
this badge—unless the rush of canvassers
for war subscriptions doesn’t hunt them to
an early grave.
Last week’s visitors at Medlow included
Dr. and Mrs. Macneill Simpson, Dr. and
Mrs. Gordon Marr, Dr. Roland Pope, and a
few more well-known professors of the
stethoscope, who are cooling down on the
Blue-hill top. Mrs. Fred Niblo is taking a
spell from the footlights at the same
breezy address. Enid Bennett fills the
place of clever Josephine Cohan Niblo, and
inserts an uncommonly pretty face into the
cast.
A threat to restore the boned neck-band
to the feminine dress comes from the other
side. But the Australian woman is scarcely
likely to put her throat into bondage for
some time to come. Certainly there -ireipe
summers when woman wore high, starched
collars and contrived to look pleasant in
the halter; nevertheless, one can’t regard
the dead-and-gone torment with anything
but a shudder.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

duct, just before each meal. For conveni-

you with the standard phormoid tablets, which
contain 20 grains each. For a few shillings
you can get enough to last you two weeks,
during which time your bust should develop
from one to two inches. Many ladies report
five inches development in four weeks, and at
the same time marked improvement in the
general health. Phormoid is pleasant to the
taste.*

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, February 8, 1915.
IVIY Dear Yarrie, —
With the return of Dame Hennessy from
her seaside vacation, the Patriotic I and
work is starting to buzz again. There was
a wake-up meeting in the Town Hall me
other day, when suggestions were made for
dividing up the extra work into smaller
parcels. Representatives of every suburb
and the near country towns will meet at
regular intervals and report what has been
dene. The idea apparently is to lift some
of the burden off the devoted few and har-
ness up the general public. The general
public is a willing enough horse when har-
nessed ; but when it sees the devoted few T
dragging the cart themselves, it just looks
over the fence at them.
The G.-G. couple have arranged to lend
Government House grounds to the Victorian
Poultry and Kennel Association for show
purposes early next month, so that all the
proceeds can go to expand her Ex.’s Red
Gross Fund. It is a horribly noisy way of
raising money for a good cause; but nowa-
days money is so scarce that if .money could
be raised by also raising Cain, the first
murderer wouldn’t have another quiet
moment.
The vice-regal Stanleys are fascinated
with Macedon and the lodge that sits
thereon. They are planning to entertain
there instead of coming down to town for
a garden party which they are simply bound
to give this month. The journey to Mace-
don will go a long way towards keeping the
company “select” and the food bill down.
The Hennessy dame is taking* an interest
in sericulture, and lately called a preliminary
meeting of friendly souls and arranged for
a deputation to the Minister of Agriculture
about the matter. Families in many parts
of the country could no doubt indulge in
silk culture as a side-line. The work
connected with the business only occu-
pies six weeks in the year, and could be
undertaken by the weaker members of a
hard-working family. The worms are never
angry when robbed —they don’t even turn
and look threateningly at the robber.
European silk culture is likely to have a
set-back,, and impressive figures are quoted
regarding the value of the industry to
France and Italy. Thus we make haste to
profit by other people’s misfortunes. Or
are we just being self-reliant? One gets so
confused these times!
Some rich Maorilanders are preparing to
send a. hospital to the war with Mrs. Bone-
fue in charge. This lady has already a
claim on the gratitude of Maorilanders for
good work done among Maori girls in the
North Island. Her son left a Maoriland
college to go to the war, and she has a dozen
other close relatives on the long and dread-
ful job.
Highbrows had a characteristic night-out
on Saturday, when naughty little modern
classics, translated from the German, were
played by semi-amateur, semi-professional
actor-folk at that haunt of dare-devils, the
Austral Salon. There were three plays,
“The Affairs of Anatol.” Advertisements,
programmes and all other proclamations
carefully suppressed the name of the author,
Schnitzler, presumably because of his un-
popular nationality, and gave all the credit
to translator Granville Barker. Schnitzler
is the Austrian who is famous for his snappy,
crisp little plays. The Repertory gave
Melbourne one big, strong production of
his “The Mate.”
The translator of “The Affairs of Ana-
tol” has cut out much of the friskiness of
the originals; but there is quite enough
left to interest. Nicholas Joy, who played
Anatol, is a local product who wears lovely
black hair and looks bored. Sometimes he
looks so bored that I doubt whether even
the placing of the world at his feet would
rouse him. It might be possible to stir him
by throwing the Crown Jewels at his hair —
but 1 don’t think so. J. 11. Cunninghame was
his second, and his women were Maud
Jolliffe (she first wore grease-paint at
the old Repertory Theatre) and Nellie
Bramley, a youngster with naturally curly
black hair who has toured in bellowdrama
for Rill Anderson. The orchestra con-
sisted of Ethel McKay and a piano.
The naughtiest of the plays was "The
Wedding Morn.” Anatol had been toasted
much in champagne at his future father-in-
law’s house the night before. The snow
encountered on his way home drives him
into an opera-dance. There he finds an old
flame with mask and dazzling eyes, and
wakes up on his wedding morning to find
the embarrassing young lady in his bache-
lor diggings. The puzzle of how to get rid of
her without disclosing the wedding-event
approaching made the audience sit up and
laugh. “The Episode” was a playlet in
which Anatol bur.vs his Past at the quarters
of a bachelor friend. “The Faro\ye 11 Supper,”
with Maud Jolliffe i_ pale green and pearls,
is an effort by Anatol to artistically finish
an affaire.
Gregan McMahon sat at the hack with
Marshall L.vle and said that he had “no
idea an Australian audience would stand it.
or he would have given the stuff long ago.
ago.
Beryl Reid, well remembered in Mel-
bourne as a pretty girl who made charming
sketches of children, suffered a distressful
time when the war broke out. She was liv-
ing in Nuremburg with her husband, and
had GO pictures on exhibition there. Hubby,
burdened with the name of W ilhelm von
Goldstein, is the son of a German natural-
ised in England and living there. Hence,
he was promptly arrested and hauled off to
prison, with about TOO French and English
residents. The German police are sus-
picious just where the British are most con-
liding. In Britain, or here, the English-
named son of an English father who gets
naturalised in Germany, is supposed to be
beyond suspicion. Well, Beryl s good man
was grabbed so suddenly and secretly that
she lost the run of him for some days. Then
the influence of German friends restored
him to her. but only on the condition that
the couide left Germany at once. So the von
Goldsteins made a hasty and tedious jour-
ney to Zurich, leaving their pictures, house-
hold goods, and Beryl’s collection of pet
animals behind them. Zurich proved to be
almost as German as Nuremburg. so they
moved to Lugano, where the artist’s mother,
coming from England, joined them. There
is still a small English community at Lu-
gano, and Beryl, ma and Von have a flat in
what used to be the Golf House. The young
artist has made something of a name for
herself in London since her marriage. She
was in demand for illustrating kiddie stories,
and did pictures for “The Jungle Books
published by Nelson’s.
The twin daughters of It. Kelly Maitland,
the V.A.T.C. liandicapper, faced the starter
for the Matrimonial Stakes at St. John’s,
East Malvern, the other night. Mirth, the
elder, gave herself into the keeping of a
Sydney man, Percy Raymond-Raymond;
while Sylvia married a hyphen too —Eric
Russell-flibbs. Both brides went gloveless
and long-sleeved to the altar, and wore pink
trains with their white bridal dresses. The
church was decorated in pink and white, and
so also was the Malvern Town Hall, where
champagne and oratory were dispensed.
Margery and Nancy Withers, daughters of
the late artist Walter Withers, are making
ready to carry on studio work in their
father’s place. They will set up their easels
at Robb’s Buildings, in Collins street, where
Withers’s pictures have lately been exhibited.
Margery has won her National Gallery Cer-
tificate. She passed in every section and
gained second place in the Scholarship com-
petition. Nancy. the younger girl, is
specialising in designing and commercial
work. In a few weeks this busy pair will
start their struggle.
The Withers girls had an exciting experi-
ence during a recent country holiday. They
had rented a cottage at Malmsbury. and one
evening they went out to a fancy-dress
party. A frantic neighbor suddenly burst
in and stopped the revelry with a rude yell
of “Fire!” Then the news was broken to
the Withers that it was their fire. The
rented cottage and everything in it was de-
stroyed, and the unhappy young people were
left with nothing but the very inadequate
fancy dresses in which they had been cavort-
ing. They had to return sadly to town in
borrowed garments. As for the cottage-
owner. I presume his tears were dried by a
fire insurance co.
St. Kilda is quite demented on the subject
of statues. The Foreshores Committee
recently planted Captain Cook on the
reclaimed beach, and there lie stands and
gazes at the sea. Now they want to set up
the late adventurous Flinders in stone, and
are imploring Gaud Mayor Ilennessy to give
them the chiselled mass. Allen, a com-
mitteeman possessed of antlike activity, has
offered a site and promised that an enthu-
siastic private citizen of St. Kilda will pro-
vide a pedestal. The Gaud Mayor, suitably
top-halted, is now prowling round the lawns
attended by a breathlessly eager committee.
Wouldn’t it be terrible if, after asking him
for a stone, he gave them bread?
Miss Audrey, daughter of the George
Chirnsides. gave up her sporting dogs when she
went off reluctantly for her “finishing” tour
with mother. Oscar Asche bought “Captain
"Wood.” the star greyhound of the kennel, and
the death of this elongated anti hurried ani-
mal (reported by cable) has probably thrown
the Chirnside girl into half-mourning. How-
ever, she has bought some more lean dogs
and is “doing” the coursing season in Eng-
land. I have no doubt that Audrey the
sport was secretly ; delighted when the out-
break of war put something of a stop on the
tiresome “finishing” process which. I under-
stand, entails much dressing up, manicuring
and small talk. I cannot be sure, because I
myself was never finished. Only the meek
and rich are finished. In an official sense
the rebellious rich girl and the poor literary
bud have to remain raw products to the end
of their days.
This is where I become mysterious. The
divorce who married a divorcee has brought
her back from their European jaunt, and in
her foreign clothes she is the smartest thing
iu sight. The pair have settled down quietly
in a seaside suburb with a definite and neatly-
timed arrangement about the two sets of
children. A motor is sent to convey one
pair of offspring to visit their father on liis
days, and another car fetches the other pair
of infants to see their mother on her days.
The consignments never meet nor mingle,
and the complex machinery of life works as
smoothly as can be expected on a mixed
diet of forbearance and petrol.
From my Adelaide correspondent: —
Douglas Mawson’s projected lecturing tour of
England and the Continent has been knocked on
the head, of course. He has substituted a season
in the United States, and has arranged for two
months’ travelling, with two lectures a day—
Sundays and all.
Dr. Bickle’s elder son, Neil, has secured a com-
mission on the Amphion ; and Gordon, a, son of
the- late A. W. Sandford, who joined King Edward’s
Horse in the beginning of the war, has been given
a commission in the Field Artillery.
The South Aus. division of Wattle Day League,
having decided to buy a motor ambulance, held a
meeting in the Public Library lecture hall on Wed-
nesday, to discuss ways and means. Mrs. ..Jeanne
Young, who has been the champion of Effective
Voting since the death of Miss Spence, has under-
taken to devise schemes, and the ball will be set
rolling with a fete at the Jubilee Oval on Feb-
ruary 27. The star speaker at the meeting was
.1. 11. Svmon, whose wife was one of the League’s
founders.
Miss Helen Murray and her sister are back from
England, and are again at Rhine Park.
On Foundation Day a blast from the bottomless
pit raged all day and all night. Holiday-making
slumped, partly because the hills are so burnt that
picnicking makes no appeal ; still, a good crowd
ventured to the A.R.C. Races at Victoria Park—
among them the Governor and Mr. Mulholland, most
of the racing men, and a few of the keenest
racing women. The totalisator took in £6OOO
less than at the same meeting last year. The
best thing was £ll 16s. paid by Miss Neckersgat,
in the last race.
Clara Serena (Kleinsehmidt), the South Aus.
singer who came back from Europe at the end of
the year, is spending a few days witlr Melba at
Lilydale. Madame heard her sing when she was in
Adelaide for her Red Cross concert, and was
sufficiently impressed to ask her to Lilydale there
and then.
The new premises for the Missions to Seamen, to
replace those which were burnt last year, were
opened at Port Adelaide on Wednesday by the
Governor, with Dean Young as one of the speakers,
and the Bishop in the chair. There is a debt of
£l6OO to be cleared, but the Mission has an ener-
getic committee, so the money will be raked in.
Dr. Helen Mayo left on Thursday for a trip to
Tasmania, in which peaceful spot she’ll presumably
forget germs and temperatures for a while.

Hilda, the younger of the Frank Fisher daughters,
to Lieutenant E. Britten Jones, of the Royal Army
Medical Corps. He is the second son of the Britten
Joneses of North Adelaide.
Lady Galway has received news of the death of
her eldest brother, Sir Arthur Blennerhassetfc, at
Nagpur, India. He was in the Political Depart-
ment of the India Civil Service, and was Assistant
Commissioner of the Central Provinces of India.
Miss Thomson, the new secretary of the Y.W.C.A.,
arrived by the Egypt on Saturday. The institution
has been thriving under the guidance of Miss Ander-
son, who combined good works with an attractive
personality arid sound business habits.
Peter Whitington, formerly Commissioner of
Audit, lias bad his seventieth birthday. He is a
son of the late William Smallpiece Whitington, who
came to the State in 1840 in his own ship, the
New Holland, and was one of the first to introduce
pedigree stock into S.A. Hobart's archdeacon is
another son.
Gordon Ayers, grandson of Henry Ayers, the old-
time South Aus. Premier, has met a tragic finish,
being somehow lost overboard during the night, on
the Egypt’s voyage, between Melbourne and Sydney.
He was not so well known as his brother Julian,
but he put in some good work as engineer of
“overhead” lines during the first years of the
electric trams in Adelaide. He was 40.
A unique banquet was given the other
evening by GO priests to the Very Ilev.
O’Connell, of Carlton, after his return from
having a word with the Pope. The cathedral
hall was the scene, and the sandwiches and
soda-water were sent in by Berges, of the
Savoy. The whole brilliant affair was
engineered by the Rev. Guy Gavan Duffy,
son of the High Court Judge. Dean
Ilegarty presided, and the guests included
bishops and deans and principals and good
fellows who could sing a song and tell a
story. Dean Carey was a sort of star; he
recited'an original complimentary ode, and
put in a capital song; and there were
others by Fathers O’Sullivan and Moynihan.
And Father Tom. of the Coll.veen Balm,
was to the fore, with the Rivirind Dolan of
the Shockrawn.
If the lovesick Nanki Poo meant to
reflect on the softgoods business when he
described himself in mournful song as a
thing of shreds and patches, his Chinese
sarcasm was quite harmless. Only the seller
of shreds and patches can afford to give his
wife a whole mansion for a flat, by way of
freeing her completely from housekeeping
worries. This great and good act has just
been performed by warehouseman Felstead,
who went forth like a true knight with his
cheque-book in rest and hired the skilled
services of the lady who steers an adjoining
flattery. Now if Mrs. Felstead will only
hire another warehouseman to look after
her husband’s business affairs the account
will be square.

have already cost one man his nice, snug

“Jimmie”. Lawson, of these parts, has an
Irish-Australian brother who has been
raised from the rank of Major-General to
that of Lieutenant-General, and now is a
sort of elbow to the Kitchener autocrat him-
self. The soldiering Lawson married the
widow of an English earl a year or two ago.
and was given the job of Governor of the
Channel Islands —which specks in the sea
are blessed by having no excise duty on
drink. The result is that champagne and
that sort of bubbling nectar can be bought
for tenpence or something a gallon, and a
square bottle of gin costs about the same.
But in these days of soda fountains what
use is all that information to you?
The J. A. Davidsons, who have plenty
of browsing space round their Oakleigh
castle, gave a real Australian party last
week. That is to say, they paid due defer-
ence to the summer climate of the land we
live in. The chief item was an outdoor pan-
tomime, with a grass terrace as stage. Mrs.
Davidson received her guests on a side lawn,
and passed them on to the seats of the out-
door theatre, where, later, Australian ama-
teurs played an Australian pantomime
written by some modest unknown. Hostess
Mrs. Davidson played the part of an Eng-
lish duchess, while her husband did his best
as the regulation Dame. (But let us draw
a tarpaulin over that part of the entertain-
ment!) Daughter Joan, who was also in-
volved, had enlisted some girls with pretty
Irish names, one of whom, Eileen O’Connor,
was the Witch. Mr. Percy Henry (hon.
stage manager) was a Green Demon of the
Bush, and made himself popular by singing
“Sister Susv’s Sewing” etceteras. Miss
Ivy Wil son held the orchestra down with
one hand. The performance began at eight,
and supper was a mere glorious memory at
10, so you will see nobody lost any beauty
sleep, especially as those present were far
too rich to go home slowly in decayed trams
and things. One small thunderstorm tried
to mar the proceedings ; but someone of the
first importance looked threateningly at the
sky and put an end to it. The clothes were
so' beautiful that the dialogue was prac-
tically painless, and everybody forgave Mr.
Davidson.
Mrs. Fred Fairbairn and dark-eyed
daughter Jess are expected back in April,
after their dash to England to see the sol-
dier son off to the front. Jess is said to
be very like Auntie Jess Fairbairn, a belle
of the early ’Bo's, who married an English-
man named Lumsden, and migrated to Eng-
land. since which the ex-beauty has been
widowed and remarried. (Is this matter
for the Historical Society?) She has
(Auntie still) developed into an ardent
disciple of Mrs. Eddy, and is now piling up
the dollars as a Christian Science healer.
That, I think, brings her up to date.

Miss Braddon, who wrote “Lady Audley’s
Secret” and then 65 other novels, died in
the middle of last week in London. Her
husband • was the late John Maxwell, the
publisher, and her talent for sensational
story-telling she passed on to their son,
W. B. Maxwell, the novelist and war-
correspondent. Miss “Braddon’s” brother
“Ned” was formerly Premier of Tasmania,
and afterwards a member of the first
Federal Parliament. He was a born
comedian, quite wasted in dull old politics.
When he was in the Assembly in Tasmania
he supplied the Hobart press-boys with the
only little entertainments some of them ever
had. But that was in the retiring rooms.
In the Chamber he was always the pink of
propriety.

I‘. and O. boat without her appendix. And
the company didn’t knock even an anna off
the fare.
A young man who is to marry one of
the Williamson panto, chorus girls has made
a terrible discovery —telegrams, even those
marked “Urgent,” are not delivered to the
ballet or chorus until the end of the show.
These missives share the fate of all the
billets-doux that are sent round to the stage
door. A girl’s grandmother may be dying, or
her fiance perishing of loneliness in a. cafe,
but she won’t be any the wiser until the
sacred show is over.
Disconsolate flappers who positively must
frivol are falling back upon morning bathing
parties at St. Ivilda. A young Englishman
who was asked to one of these little shows
described it as “most immodest.” He had
probably been used to Margate, where most
of the costumes are formally passed by a
council of wowsers before being donned. To
the British " mind that has been nurtured in
Margate or Blackpool, Australia is an abso-
lute devil of a place.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“Paddy Melon” : Who says an emu can’t
be broken in as a beast of burden? Let him go forth
and obtain knowledge. I saw a bagman. arrive at
the Speewa, himself on foot, but his swag and dilly
bags neatly packed on an emu. The feathered
horse’s halter consisted of a piece of green hide
attached to a stick that protruded from the nose
(the bagman must have been used to camels). Any-
way, the emu led well, and at night its coxswain
hobbled it out on a patch of prickly pear. The night
I was in his company he discoursed at length on the
many useful things an emu was suitable for. He
got it to sing “Come into the garden, Maud”; but
it was a rotten vocalist, I thought.
“The Reefer’ 1 : In the Sandwich Isles
when extra-large eels are caught (some are 30in. in
girth) the natives transfer them to shallow rock
holes some distance inland. Each “boy” has an Wei
pool of his own. In course of time a nigger's eels
learn to come at their boss’s summons. A missionary
told me he had watched them feeding out of a
young chief’s hand with perfect confidence. He was
a bishop, too.
“Outcast”: The Papuan is rather a boast-
ful person. For instance, he keeps a string tally
of all his adventures with the ladies, and when
under the influence of too much joy drink he delights
in telling over his exploits to boon companions.
IV hen a man dies and is laid out, the string tally
of his reprehensible relations with married women
and unmarried girls is suspended from a croton
plant placed at his head or feet. The bigger the
tally the greater the honor. One Papuan I know
has a list of his lady loves scarred off on his shoulder
muscle, but he was careful to explain to me that
this was only the list of his amours in his own
village, and did not record his casual adventures
throughout the territory. For that, there wasn’t
enough space available.
“R.B.II.”: In the interests of natural
history I want to shed some light upon the propa-
gative possibilities of the snake. A couple of days
ago a friend of mine at Osborne Park (W.A.) had
an encounter with a specimen of the tiger brand.
A post-mortem revealed the presence in the corpse
of 23 partially-developed reptiles, from five to six
inches long, a circumstance which plainly shows that
snakes are subject to the ordinary process of gesta-
tion, despite the common fallacy prevailing that the
young are hatched from eggs. The specimen has
been identified by the Director of the Museum at
Perth as of the notechis scutatus variety, which is
considered the most venomous in Australia. The
female may produce as many as 50 in a single
litter.
* * * *
Paddy Melon”: On the claypan and
sandy country of the Lower Diamantina and Cooper
livers there is a weed which plays aged Henry with
horses that happen to eat it. It. is the eyes that
suffer. At times mobs of horses are rendered tem-
porarily stone-blind. A thick scum covers the eye,
and, unless attended to promptly, a horse is liable
to lose the sight. Powdered sugar and salt, alum,
powdered bluestone and vinegar have all been suc-
cessfully used to clear the scum, but in some cases
nothing will restore the vision. 1 have seen
drovers with large mobs of cattle stuck up for a
week and more on account of blind horseST. One
unfoi innate cowpuncher last year, going down the
Cooper with 1200 fats, was stuck on one camp for
17 days. During that period he used all the sugar,
salt and vinegar in the camp. Then, to cap the
lot, as soon as he had fixed his horses up and was
ready to move on, all his men left, because sugar
was off. I don’t know how he got on after that;
vvhen I passed him he was cursing the dear old
Cooper with uncovered head and a holv light in hif
eye.
“Paddy Melon”: A‘ c “A.V.L.” (B
S/12/-14) and “Sarto” (11. li/l/’U) on the friendly
nature of the fantail and the blue-wren. I agree with
the former that the fantail wins the stakes. Last
year, while overlanding a mob of rams, a fantail
accompanied us the whole way—and the whole way
was as far out as Winton. It was the day after I
took delivery that I noticed the bird having a ride
on one of the leaders’ backs, and from that out I
saw the visitor every day till I delivered. It used
to fly from back to back and got as fat as butter
before the trip finished. At night it used to go to
its nap on the calico break, and, after being a week
on the road it used to come every morning into the
fly under which I camped and give me the office it
was time to get out.

ilie Ifiinu ’: Is there any reason why
Australians shouldn’t have local agricultural notes,
instead of goods imported from the Chewing Gum
country? Ever since the N.S.W. Agricultural De-
partment announced, with a whirl of trumpets, that
it would publish, every week, results of experiments,
note S a n C comments calculated to help the farmer and
settler, I have watched the effort with interest. Before
me lies a copy of the notes for January 9. There are
five articles, and three of them start off like this:
An interesting experiment was conducted at
Nebraska Experiment Station, U.S.A., during last
winter.” Now, what in the name of the Great
Snake is the use of such stuff? We never have a
vvinter in this State to correspond to Nebraskan con-
ditions ; and, even if we did, local experiments are
much more interesting. Also, they are much more
useful. Perhaps the Department never thought of
that.
* * * *
B.N. . Above all things else a Papuan
pig prefers sweet potatoes. The natives protect
their vegetable patches with regular stockades, and
still the wild swine bunt their way in. The Sere°-em
villagers, ’way up in the main range, to prevent their
domesticated pigs pushing through into the gardens,
cut a hole an inch in diameter in the ears of each
pig. A 3ft. length of strong wood is put through
these holes, and secured. The squeals and grunts of
the animals when this stick is all that stands be-
tween them and a spud patch is enough to cause the
inventor of file scheme to squirm in his mausoleum.
One night a few jears ago the missionary at Ivevori
thought lie heard pigs at his garden ; so lie grabbed
ns gun and sneaked along through the reeds outside
his fence. Presently the long back of his quarry
moved in the dimness beyond, and the excited pastor
gave it both barrels. The target, instead of squeal-
ing, set up a blood-curdling screaming. It was a
native girl who had been on her hands and knees
catching swamp-crabs when the hail of lead over-
took her. That little lot cost the parson more than
a sleepless night.

Talk about lonelv deaths Outback ! Reid
Park, Mosman, the shadowy, tree-lined ravine
whereto the speechless evening lovers of Sydney go
with arms wound carefully round each other’s waists,
has provided the tattered remnants of an unknown
woman who died or was murdered, and whose body
lay undiscovered for the best part of a year, within
a stone s-throw of dozens of red-tiled suburban houses.
Lord only knows how many love-murmurs the un-
known overheard as she lay out in the dark wait-
ing' for her overdue coffin, but she must have listened
to more than is good for any woman.

“Suds”: What shearer or bushwhacker
was it said the city bloque can’t drink ? I produce
Exhibit A. He was a witness in a suburban court
case last week, and under examination admitted he
was no bigoted teetotaller. On Christmas Day, ht-
said, he did not begin drinking, beyond a pipe opener
or two, till 11.30 a.m. Then he had two beers and
a drop of gin. At 12 o'clock he had a couple of
glasses of wine, and between then and 12.30 two
more beers. He took a bottle away with him for
lunch. At two o’clock he visited a friend, and had a
couple of tastes and a bottle of wine. During the
afternoon he visited another friend’s, where a “jimrny-
john” of beer was broached. Subsequently he was
the victim of insulting language in a public place,
being perfectly sober at the time. '
“Lagani-Namo”: Male natives belonging
to secret societies in the Banks group wear certain
croton leaves in a particular way as lodge emblems.
Though any native may wear croton foliage, it is
strictly taboo for the uninitiated to wear the leaves
in the way any of the societies have made their own.
Some of the lodges cultivate croton plants around
their club houses; and to preclude outsiders from
decorating themselves with the select variety the
plants are grafted so as to produce a singular kind.
To do this two. different slips of equal size are taken,
an inch of the bark is stripped from one and a like
depth of the pith is removed from the other, so
that the former can be inserted into the latter and
the barks meet exactly. Some of the most experi-
enced native horticulturists succeed in producing an
identical variety, year after year, by this grafting
process.

An’ he yourseIf—YOURSELF, or I’m
Done once for all with you !
“Those nights at Piker’s pub, fcegod !
What rorty nights were those!
The pinchgut tracks we often trod,
Bare-heeled an’ out at toes —■
An’ yet you'd make the bluebells nod
Round those things, I suppose!
“The scarlet girls that Piker kept
Behind the Sunset bar
(I grant you now that once one wept
For her young Lochinvar,
What time you didn’t know, but slept
An’ dreamed of faces far).
“You thought them hard —an’ so they were,
Yet more than once I saw
A tragic teardrop glisten there
That filled my heart with awe
(The paradox of sweetness rare,
An’ fang, an’ tooth, an’ claw!).
“An’ once you thought Her wondrous kind,
’Twas then for me to show
llow blind you were, how dense an’ blind,
How fast—an’ yet how slow!
It pained me grievously to find
How much you didn’t know!
“We saw the camel teams, an’ we
Twain pictured Old Romance,
The spice an’ myrrh of Arabv,
The Desert daughters’ dance ;
But that was rot, it seems to me—
A damfool circumstance!
“But there the blood of life ran red,
Beyond all hint of doubt,
And there was Mirth to Madness wed
For you to write about ;
So now, old mate, you go ahead—
But cut the tinsel out!”
So if my lines are apt to miss
The good bull’s-eye of Fame ;
If some are blue with emphasis,
And more are red with shame,
You might, perhaps, remember this:
That Mulga Mica’s to blame!
N.S.W.
R J. CASSIDY.
‘‘Bill Bowyang” : I’m going into the patent
medicine business. I kr.ow several persons who have
been thoroughly cured of rheumatism by sleeping on
beds made entirely of bluegum leaves taken from
young plants, and stripped from the branches near-
est to the ground. It is, moreover, well known
among bushmen that a brew made from a dozen of
these leaves is good for inflamed eyes.

The “Sanderson” Hand Camera is the most useful and
valuable Camera for all-round work that it is possible to obtain.
The “Sanderson” is at once simple in construction, easy to
manipulate, and combines the manifest advantages of a Field
Camera with the portability and “instantly ready” character
of a Hand Camera. It is without doubt the ideal instrument
for a skilled worker.
The Universal Swing Front, a movement that has made
the “Sanderson” famous, is entirely governed by a single pair
of locking nuts. All the actions are controlled from the back,
and the lens swings on its axis, travelling upwards, forwards
or backwards on a pair of struts slotted in their full length.
These movements, however, are held in reserve, and need only
be brought into action when required.
Amongst the many improvements that have been added to
the “Sanderson” Camera recently are the New Back Focussing
Rack for wide-angle work, Rack and Pinion control to the
Rising Front, Improved Method of attaching the Reversing
Back.

“The Pariah” : Colac (Vic.) is at present
chiefly interested in goat raising. Some months ago
the Railway Commissioners announced their inten-
tion to spend £37,400 on improvements to the
Colac station. Some of the local Poohbahs bucked
into the matter and wrote explaining that the plans
were unsuitable, etc. After being informed that
their objections have been over-ruled by the engi-
neer, they forwarded a deputation to the Depart-
ment to instruct the engineer in his job. That did
it. After an interval the Department wrote saying
that, as desired, the intended work for Colac had
been modified, and it would now cost only £2OOO.
But about the goat race. On January 1 the three-
guinea . event was won by Sweet William, with
Boronia and Sweet Nell in that order, and Odorless
and Eau-de-Cologne fighting it out for fourth place.
“L.N.”: Everywhere around the South Sea
Islands the conch shell is used as a trumpet. A shell,
say, 20in. long and about 9in. in 'the biggest part, is
chosen, and a hole three-quarters of an inch in
diameter is made about three inches from its apex.
The trumpeter holds the shell in both hands, takes
a deep breath, and blows into the made hole. Over
water, the reverberating note can be heard a mile
and a half away. Instead of putting their lips to
the hole the Sandwich Islanders cement a three-foot
bamboo pipe to it, with gum from the breadfruit
tree. This enables them to prolong and control the
sound better.

“Uloola” : Some calculating fiend estimated
that in a few years’ time it will be possible to take
from the Burrinmck dam 11,000 tons of fish annu-
ally. This should solve the pro-
blem of the supply of fresh fish for
N.S.W. country towns. That is,
if the stock isn’t all dragged to
Sydney for the benefit of the
bloated metropolis. Judging by
the quantity of fish caught with
hand-lines during the past year,
the estimate is rather under than
over the probable yearly haul. A
few months ago it was almost im-
possible to dip a bucket of water
anywhere along the shores of the
lake without taking up small cod
and perch. The water was liter-
ally alive with fry. About 80ft.
above the original river-bed the
wall is pierced by two tunnels.
Each of these is open a foot or
so, and through them have come
trout up to 71b. weight, cod, perch
and “yellowbellies,” as much as
121 b. in weight. One morning
. over a hundred pounds’ weight of
fish was picked up. One energetic
fisher is already on the job—the
destructive shag. Before the
water was stored, only odd couples
of these voracious birds were to
be seen along the still reaches of
the ’Bidgee. Now they are in
hundreds, and last breeding sea-
son most of the old gum trees
left standing on the flats over
which the water spreads were
dotted with nests, every suitable
branch having from one to half a
dozen on it. Recently the Irriga-
tion Commission organised gun
parties and obliterated the enemy
in hundreds.
“Outcast”: I have frequently tried to
explain to our Papuan brother that when a wise
white man takes a wife he expects to receive some
payment with her if possible. This procedure seems
to be looked on with approval by the young bucks,
but respectable fathers of families with several
daughters to dispose of shake their heads. A daugh-
ter to them is a highly profitable piece of property,
and, as a general rule, a man has to pay his parents-
in-law pretty heavily for the privilege of marrying
into the family. Among the Kiwais of the Fly
River a wife is valued at a canoe or a dugong spear
or an armshell. About the year 1850 a wife could
be bought in the Torres Strait Islands for a knife
or a glass bottle, but prices have gone up in recent
years. In fact, the cost of wedding is becoming
outrageous. Old Maino, the well-known chief of Yam
Island, paid for his missus one camphor-wood box,
seven bolts of calico, one dozen shirts, one dozen
singlets, one fish spear, one pound of tobacco and two
pearlshells. Subsequently he considered she was
altogether too dear. In these mercenary days values
have risen so rapidly that a man often pays for his
wife on the instalment plan. The Government
blocked this on the Fly River, and fixed the price of
wives at £4 sterling, spot cash.
WHEN SHAKSPEARE CAME TO BILLABONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You can’t make Hamlets without breaking eggs.
—New caw.
To Murrumbidgee’s Billabong
The Strolling Hogans came.
A pupil wild and lean and long
They tried upon the same.
The patrons came from far that day,
And Shakspeare's ‘‘Hamlet” was the play.
The pupil’s name was Ernest Green,
A fearful bungler he ;
He roared right through the ghcstly scene —
The people yelled with glee.
But soon they wearied of the sport,
And threw tomatoes of a sort.
Then every time that Err.est came
On thin and quaking legs,
They harried him with little shame,
Aud hurled their scer.tcd eggs.
The Hogans egged him on in vain,
The patrons egged him off again.
Then Ernest struck. He scooped the voile
From out his dexter eye.
“I’ll act no more. This is no joke”—
He threw his garments by.
“The play is ‘Hamlet,’ but out there
They think it Omelette, I swear!”
victoria.

The Shyness of Tim Canty.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
‘fnilE shy, coy lad was Tim Canty,”
said Flynn reflectively. “A bit iv
a bye six fut in his socks, ’n’ wid
freckles to him ye’d be hangin’ a hat on, ’n’
sthrong too, cud take a thriflin’ horse on
his back ’n’ walk him off shtraight.
“Whin Tim come t’ Corcorain’s, Mary
Dwyer was turned aightee-i ’n’ a saucy fine
gir-rl livin’ wid her da ’tween the two cricks,
him ownin’ ez nate a far-rm ez yill meet wid
anny place, ’n’ a dairy herd ’twould comfort
yer soul t’ see.
“Tim Canty come frim the other end iv
Bungaree way, havin’ hot Ilooley’s land
intint on shtartin’ fer himself, wid his
father’s blessin’, ’n’ rare knowledge iv cows
’n’ crops. Tim was twinty-five, no more, ’n’
a distant an’ reluctant gallant wid the weemin.
would occupy a fortified position wan mile
off, ’n’ trow am’rous glances in the fir-rm
conviction he was the devil iv a felly, ’n’
makin’ great headway wid the sext, mark
you. ’Twas his delibrit intintion t’ marry
’n’ settle. Didn’t he tell me wid his own
lips?
“ ‘There’s a nate, tall wife a man’ll be
nadin’, Flynn,’ sez he. ‘D’yeh mind anny
such at all? Wid a gift iv milkin’ ’n’ a
disposition fer domestic juties?’ he sez. ‘l’ll
not be har-rd,’ sez he, ‘can she bake well,
’n’ wash, ’n’ hoe an acre iv shpuds wanst in
a way, ’r stand up a chain iv good fencin’.
I'll nade wan sound iv health,’ sez he, ‘stout
’n’ actiff, will be no dthrag to a man makin’
his fortehin. Is she pretty beside ’twill
be no dthrawback at all.’
“I cudn’t bethink me iv a lady would meet
thim requirements, but give him me promise
t’ kape a sharp look out. Manewhile, how-
ever, Tim cast his eye on Mary Dwyer frim
a rispictable distance, ’n’ shtarted a delibrit
’n’ judicious courtship frim the hill behint
his bar-rn, lookin’ at her a dale troo a micro-
scope give him be his great aunt Martha.
’Tis a shy man will make love to a
nate gir-rl troo a microscope, but I’m tellin’
yeh Tim Canty was wan afflict wid a painful
reserve in his dalin’s wid the weemin. Did
Mary come nigh him be the width iv a
paddock, Misther Tim brack into a cowld
shweat, ’n’ shied off a mile. But wid his
glass in a tree, ’n’ him well covered be the
boughs, ye’d find him a disprit characther,
gazin afther the gir-rl long V ar-rdent wid
an insolent eye ’n’ ez stout a hear-rt ez the
great Don June himself.
“Manny’s the time I come on Tim in the
rocks iv the hill over agin his cow paddock
crouched wid his distended microscope fixed
on to Mary Dwyer, watchin’ the gir-rl com-
m V goin’ about her juties. ‘ ’Tis a dacent,
ortherley woman, Flynn,’ sez he, ‘ ’n’ yill
not be seein’ a thrimmer figure in the width
iv Asia,’ sez he.
“ ‘Thrue,’ sez I, ‘but what progress would
yeh make wid a lovely gir-rl, rnakin’ eves at
her troo a glass, ’n’ you a mile off ’n’ aightv
yar-rds?’
“‘A man would be careful,’ sez Canty,
‘bein’ wan wid property ’n’ jewdicious be
nature ’n’ sthrict trainin’. Would yeh have
me rush the gir-rl,’ sez, he, ‘ ’n’ marry her
off-hand this avenin,’ sez he, ‘ ’n’ me un-
acquaint wid the habits iv her ’n’ her tin-
dincies ’n’ dishposition?’ he sez.
“ ‘Yer a moighty rushin’, tearin’ ’n’ dish-
turbin’ foorce, Timmy,’ sez I, ‘ ’n’ ’tis wise
’n’ well yill restrain yer proclivities, but
maybe wan day yill be seein’ a rare, illigant
weddin’ troo yer glass, John Hogan havin’
a passionit regar-rd for Mary, ’n’ though
John is bald ’n’ forty, long-distance love
makin’ wid an opery glass is not his idea iv
am’rous tactics ’n’ shtrategics.’
“ ‘That ould, good-fer-nothin’, limpin’
villain!’ sez Tim wid shpirit. ‘Lave him
cross me path once only ’n’ I’ll put a flamin’
sorrer on him will stay wid him the year.
Y’ll hint t’ Mary Dwyer, will yeh, Flynn,
I’m givin’ her case jue consideration? But
be careful, man, ’n’ tie me t’ nothin’. I’ll
not be tied till me mind is satisfied entirely.
’N’ wrong ye are, Flynn, in yer hear-rt
thinkin’ I’m shy, V diffident, ’n’ fearful
iv gir-rls. Don’t the glass bring Mary
Dwyer dost t’ me I cud touch her, ’n’ me
hand not shakin’ a jot, ’n’ never a blush t’
me cheek at all. ’Tis a brave man surely
will be able t’ say that, Flynn.’
“This sort iv progriss was continued a
mather iv a year ’n’ a ha’f, Tim circulatin’
in a rayjis iv siv’ral hundthred yar-rds, wid
his microscope will stretch two fut ’r more,
’n’ trowin’ a figure wanst in a way did Mary
look towar-rds him, then scuttlin’ fer the
rocks like a shtartled rabbit.
“ ’Twas meself tuck the news to him.
‘She’s t’ be wed, man ’ sez I. ‘ ’n’ well ’n’ good
yer served wid yer coortship be a system iv
signals.’ sez I, ‘ ’n’ yer love srlances wid a
mis’rable microscope. John Hogan is wan
holdin’ faith in d’rect communications, ’n’
the weddin’s no more’n a week away this
day.’
“He looks at me quiet ’n’ still. ‘ls that
so?’ he sez. ‘Then yill have me think I’ve
waste me time these long days? Tut-tut-
tut!’ sez he, ‘weemin is fickle ’n’ conthrary,’
he sez, ’n’ a tear would weigh wan ounce
flops to the cheek of him. bounces to his
chin, ’n’ falls on his boot, V at that I lave
the poor boy to his sorrer.
“The weddin’ day came shweet ’n’ shinin’,
wid the glory iv shoring flowers, ’n’ the
twitter iv bir-rds along the road t’ Ryan’s
Town, ’n’ Mary ’n’ her da is jauntin’ t’ the
church in the new trap, her in her weddin’
dhress, shweet as a pink saint in a picture
be the store. ’Tis at the fut iv Pete’s Hill
Tim Canty slips from the saplin’s. ’n’
snatches Dwyer’s horse be the head.
‘Where’ll ye be jauntin’ to in yer best, Morris
Dwyer?’ sez he.
“ ‘Lave me horse, Canty, will yeh,’ Dwyer
he sez, ‘fer we’re away t’ Ryan’s Town t’
the weddin’ iv me Mary, ’n’ no time fer
compliments,’ sez he.
“ <Yer goin’ t’ no weddin’ et Ryan’s Town
this dav, Morris Dwyer,’ sez Tim.
“ ‘ ’N’ fet why not?’ sez Dwyer.
“ ‘Haven’t I this mornin’ set fire t’ the
church?” sez Tim.
“ ‘Then the curse is on yeh, ’n’ yill go t’
gaol, ’n’ Misther John Hogan will find
annother place t’ marry in will do well
enough,’ sez Dwyer.
“ ’N’ all the time Mary Dwyer was sittin’
still ’n’ pale, savin’ nothin’, but lookin’ at
Tim Canty wid big eyes.
“ ‘Yill not be marryin’ wid ould, bald-
headed, limnin’ Hogan in church or anny-
this day.’ sez Tim.
“ ‘We’ll not?’ cries Dwyer.
“‘You’ll not; ’n’ why? Becauze I’m afthei
havin’ took John Hogan be foorce, ’n’ put
him where he’ll keep fer wan day.’
“Then Mary Dwyer shooke at last, ’n’
her vice was clear ’n’ high : ‘ ’N’ for why
have yeh done these things, Tim Canty?’
sez she.
“ ‘Becauze,’ sez he, ‘yill marry wid me,
Mary Dwyer, or with no man.’
“ ‘Then I’ll marry wid you, Tim Canty.’
sez she. ‘ ’n’ it’s a sore long while I’ve been
brinsin’ yeh t’ the point,’ she sez.
“The church was but a thrifle scorched,
’n’ John Hogan was locked secure in Canty’s
underground cellar small bit the worse fer
it, ’n’ injueements was made t’ save Tim
from gaol- him bein’ abroad to his honey-
moon be this. So ut all ended happily ’n’
wid satisfaction, people aereein’ generally
Tim Cantv done pretty well fer wan exces-
sively shy.”
EDDYSON.
