Business, Robbery, etc.
“Remah”: “Vic” (B. 24/12/’l4), writing
of the turning out of the first loco, from
Thompson and Co.’s foundry, Castlemaine,
says the codlin moth in the apple of the
industry is the fact that only 22 out of 125
apprentices will improve themselves by a
course of technical-school training, although
the firm gives every facility and offers large
inducements. I offer the suggestion that
perhaps one of the best inducements would
be the reasonable certainty of employment
when the term of apprenticeship having
ended, the finished workman marries and
settles down in the town which Thompson
and Co.’s enterprise has undoubtedly done
much to build up. ' I don’t know wbat it
may be at Castlemaine, but I do know how
it is at other places where similar facilities
are offered. The fact that single men are
often retained in slack times, while married
ones are put off, is easily explained by the
fact that the latter, being anchored by fam-
ily ties, is likely to remain in the district
and so be at hand when again wanted, while
the single workman, when slack times
occur, is liable to board the train and
light out for parts unknown. The system
may be all right for the employers, but it is
hard lines for the married workman and
for the one who wants to be married.
“Chloe”: The altitudinous price of fruit
in Sydney is an outrage on a too-complaisant
public. Huge quantities of fairly good fruit
are wasting in the gardens around Parra-
matta, simply because it does not pay the
grower to pick and pack any except the
very best. A great quantity of _ slightly
inferior quality just wastes, when it would
be a blessing to many a poor householder,
who finds it impossible to buy at current
rates. Adelaide has far more economical
and better methods of fruit distribution,
and prices there are no more than half
of Sydney’s, though the fruit is of as good,
if not better, quality. The South Aus.
Growers’ Association brings its produce to
co-operative, open markets —fine buildings
covering from four to five acres, perfectly
equipped. There are no agents ; each grower
drives his motor-lorry into the market place,
and sells his stuff to any _ buyer in either
retail or wholesale quantities. The city
and suburbs are served daily by cart or
barrow, and a daily supply of fresh and
excellent garden and orchard produce is
assured. There is not a single Chinese
grower or vendor. The trade is entirely in
European hands, largely German, and is a
very prosperous one.
* * ,* *
“Milo”: North Coast Co-op. Co., Byron
Bay (N.S.W.), put up a record in Decem-
ber. For the five weeks covered by the
December pay, the quantity of butter manu-
factured was only a few hundredweight short
of 1000 tons, and the money distributed
among the co.’s 2000 suppliers was £119,800.
For the whole year Strawberry and Daisy
yielded 10,034,3131 b. of butter. The co.
also succeeded in stopping the squeal of
37,040 pigs. The total distributed among
suppliers of cream and pig was £911,703;
and yet the co. produces less than half the
butter made on the Richmond and Tweed.
Since the North Coast Co. started in June,
1895. the turnover has been £7,755.402. The
family of Daisy and Strawberry has truly
given the Big Scrub a rousing leg-up.
* * * *
“Pick”: I tumble out of my dinghy to
contradict “Bloggs,” who describes the work-
ing fisherman (B. 14/1/T5) as languid,
beery, mercenary and insanitary. “Bloggs”
plainly has never stood waist deep in the icy
sea hauling for hours at the end of a three-
thousand yard drag-net, with the very likely
chance, when he finally heaves it ashore, of
finding nothing but two tons of jelly, fish in
it. I have, and, therefore, I know that a
fisher’s weariness comes not from chronic
lassitude as “Bloggs” flippantly alleges, but
from the back-wracking, bone-chilling night-
time yakker that in record time makes young
men old rheumatic wrecks. It is, further, no
part of the wholesale fisherman’s trade to
clean the fish he yards up. The bargain-
hunting beater-down of prices will find the
Pacific just as adjacent and available for
cleansing purposes as it is to the worn-out
fisher.
A new scheme of the sharking “agent” is
to set himself up as an official from the
N.S.W. Taxation Department, and go round
with sheaves of income-tax forms, which he
fills up for a fee of five shillings. The job
may be worth the money. Still the “agent”
evidently doesn’t believe his “clients” would
think so if he told them the truth.
Rechabites can rejoice in the fact that
the Australian temperance-drink industry
has risen to the occasion. The Defence
Department recently issued a pressing order
for 8000 dozen of aerated waters —presum-
ably for Red Cross purposes. The lot had
to be shipped almost immediately, and one
firm (Rowlands), by working at high pres-
sure, was able to supply the whole 96,000
bottles.
* * * *
Shoddy is in for a colder time in Maori-
land. Any article of footwear which looks
like leather and is generally charged for at
leather prices, but is discovered to be merely
brown paper on the first wet day, will for
the future have to carry a label to the
effect that it’s a fraud. Real leather goods
will not need to be stamped, but anybody
selling, or even having in stock, pulp boots
that are unstamped will be liable to a pen-
alty up to £2O.
* * * *
“Nemesis”: “T. 0.1.” discourses on some
ancient trade signs in Sydney (B. 24/12/T4).
Here are two from Victoria. Some 10 or
12 years ago two Bungs in Fitzroy displayed
these signs outside their respective inns—
“ The Labor in Vain,” portraying a black
brother being scrubbed, and the “Persever-
ance,” showing black brother as a piebald.
The latter hostelry has since been rebuilt,
and the last time I saw it the sign had dis-
appeared. -
* * * *
“Moutere”: Why is the telegraph infant
or insect not required to obtain a receipt
for the telegram he delivers? A few weeks
ago I sent a message and received no answer.
A fortnight afterwards I discovered that
my wire had been shoved under the door of
a temporarily empty house. Of course, I
made a complaint, but all the satisfaction I
got was that the crime was the work of an
“extra” boy, who was no longer available
to be spanked, discharged or otherwise dealt
with. Thus the P.M.G. had taken my six-
pence for work which he undertook to do,
but did not make even a decent attempt
to carry out, and when the offence was
proved his minions neither apologised nor
offered to return my money. It would be
interesting to know how many telegrams
are thrown away by “extra” boys, as well
as by the permanent insects that crawl
about the city. A book of receipts kept for
the signature of those who receive telegrams
would get rid of nearly all the trouble. As
for my sixpence, I intend to demand it of
W. G. Spence the next time I see his
patriarchal beard.
“II.O.”: I first met Harold Hartington at
Geelong. He was there with the object of
ascertaining if the village was deserving of
a visit from his “stupendous aggregation of
histrionic talent.” Clayton asked Harold if
he knew Caleb Sewell, who was due next
week with a stupendous aggregation of hi i
own. It transpired that Mr. Hartington did
know Mr. Sewell, and we soon learned that
Mr. Sewell was a liar, an agnostic, a wife-
beater, a disloyalist and an amateur gard-
ener, who in his time had been guilty of
bigamy, attempted bribery, mixed bathing
and several plots to collect the maternity
bonus by fraud. Still, Harold confessed,
with evident reluctance, that Sewell had one
redeeming feature —he could act. Much as
he (Harold) loathed and despised him, he
was compelled to admit that the fellow was
an artist. Harold’s story was duly circu-
lated, and all but the truly virtuous decided
that they must certainly see such a genius
as he was acknowledged to be even by his
enemy. I attended the first-night perform-
ance and found Caleb’s acting more awful
than that of any mummer I had seen outside
the metropolis. Having cursed Hartington
for a fool, I forgot about him until a month
later, when I was in Camperdown. He was
there and still attacking Caleb’s morals
while eulogising his art. Subsequently I
met Harold at Horsham, and he was on the
same lay. At Murtoa I forced a confession
from him at the point of a loaded decanter.
He was not an actor with a “stupendous
aggregation” of his own, but was Caleb
Sewell’s trusted advance agent; and it was
with Caleb’s knowledge and consent that he
circulated untruths anent that gentleman’s
moral worthlessness, by way of lending force
to equally unveracious rumors concerning
his histrionic worth.
HOW SHE BROUGHT HOME THE SOAP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Business as usual. —British tradesman’s motto.
When England’s siege-guns bellowed
Across the trembling world,
And autumn leaves were yellowed,
And standards were unfurled,
Light-footed, trim and eager,
Fragile, yet full of hope,
The dauntless Wimmens’ Leaguer
Sailed out to buy some soap.
What cared she for the tramcars
That rumbled back and forth,
And all the other d —n cars
That hooted in their wrath!
With colors bravely flying
And grease-paint on her face,
To find the grocer, lying,
She flew with girlish grace.
Then spies and envious neighbors
Left hearth and grate and stove —
They ceased their servile labors
And in curl-papers strove
To see where she was going
And what she meant to do,
In dainty muslins flowing,
With eyes of Saxon blue.
She fluttered on unheeding
The jealous rage and spite;
’Twas soap that she was needing,
And crow-like was her flight;
Indomitable, speedy,
Relentless, proudly gay,
She sought the grocer seedy
In her Britannic way.
The grocer marked her coming,
Observed her eyes of blue,
And asked in accents humming:
“What can I do for you?
“What can / do for you, Miss?”
He buzzed, instinct with hope.
“The weather’s warmish? True, Miss!”
She said: “I want some soap.”
The grocer did not quibble,
But. wrapped some soap with speed,
And did a lightning scribble
In some book grocers heed.
The cash thing pealed its warning,
The change was passed, and lo !
Farewells and good-byes scorning,
She went! He watched her go.
N.S.W.
NELSON W. HOORAY.

POLITICAL
POINTS
AFTER ALL HE HAS BORROWED, TOO!
Last month’s N.S.W. State revenue decreased
£12,649, while the expenditure increased £10,846.
HOLMAN : “ That's strange —it wan't balance!”
The shortest and brightest proposition to
come before the N.S.W. Political Labor
Congress is supplied by Petersham.
Petersham suggests, by way of a new plank,
that the Holman Government should pay
some attention to the old planks.
The curse of drink has struck the
Scaddan Government of Westralia. It pro-
posed that State-owned hotels should be
exempt from the supervision of the State
Licensing Boards —the bodies which dis-
qualify dirty, dilapidated, ill-conducted or
superfluous premises. The idea that the
Ministry was afraid its pubs couldn’t stand
inspection broke the Assembly up. and the
scheme was defeated by two votes.
Apparently the N.S.W. Government will
presently have to run for its life if it pro-
poses to keep ahead of tlie frenzied demand
for the totalisator. Something like a score
of leagues have put this subject down for
discussion at the State Political Labor Con-
gress. Now an agile Labor Minister might
explain to one league bis preference for the
Private Enterprise bookie as against the
socialistic tote, but explaining to 20 leagues
is a large order.
The Gerhog Government played a dirty
trick on the English manufacturers who bad
money due from Germany when the war
broke out. But in dealing with tlie Com-
monwealth Treasury in the little matter
of Papuan salaries, bow honest, bow
prompt, was the German 1 What a shining
example of rectitude! Likewise what an
artless admission that tlie Government in
Berlin still thinks of a day when Australia
will be glad to cast off the “hateful British
yoke” in favor of the benevolent German
article! The Kaiser’s diplomats may be
stupid, but not quite so stupid as to bilk
the expected fish of a little bit of bait.
The confirmed importer lias some weird
arguments. One of the tribe told Frank
Tudor, Minister for Customs, the other day
that tlie cigar industry in Australia was so
insignificant that if the tariff on cigars was
reduced to the old rates and the industry
slaughtered, the duty on the extra importa-
tions would provide enough revenue to pay
each of tlie technical workers thrown out of
a job £i> a week for 50 weeks in tlie year,
lie forgot to mention the fact that, if the
work wasn’t done in Australia, much money
would he sent out of Australia to pay for
the foreign cigars—money which would
otherwise stay in the country. And he didn’t
explain how any industry was ever likely to
become large if it was slaughtered in its
infancy just because it happened to be small.
Even an importer wouldn’t be stout in bis
old age if he was killed before be became an
importer just because he was a babe.
RAISER BILL COLLECTS ANOTHER
ENEMY.
HOLMAN: “D — n the Raiser!”
Many people in N.S.W. are tearing their
hair over the income-tax returns. The
Government-That-Is-Always-In-A-Hurry has
decreed that everybody who has any kind of
a private revenue, even if it is only 6d., must
fill up a huge form worth perhaps 2d., and
put it in a big envelope worth probably Mi-
ami send it along (postage about 2d.), in
order to show that he or she isn’t liable for
Jd. Failing this, the penalty may be £lOO. It
looked very large and honest and copious when
the Act was passed, but the demand for forms
became serious, and the wear and tear of
clerks became terrible. So informal
announcements began to appear in the
papers (price Id.) to the effect that
people with incomes below £2OO a
year need not fill in the half-acre
of document. Unfortunately the law
still stands, and the possible fine of £lOO
still stands, and verbal assurances or in-
formal promises made by clerks across the
office counter seem dubious coinage. The
“official notification sets forth to the bitter end
that a return must be supplied by “every
person.... deriving income in any way or
from any source in New South Wales.” So
The Bulletin’s youngest office boy got
half a day off and filled in his paper.
Melbourne Argtjs and its correspondents
keep harping on the idea that in this time
of stress wages boards’ determinations
should be suspended, and Fat. instead of
sacking half his employees, should be allowed
to retain them at (say) half the present
wages. But why all this fuss? If he really
wants to minimise unemployment, there is
an easy way open to him. There is nothing
to prevent him working his employees half-
time—week on and week off —at the legal
■wage rates. Fat’s solicitude for the unem-
ployed would look less hypocritical if he
adopted a scheme of that sort.

Kecp'i t lighting in the North Sea shows
that the German ships carry the bulk of
their guns at the stern; the British ships
have most of theirs well forward. The
German ships are evidently built to fire as
they run away ; the British to fire as they
chase the enemy. It is a most suitable
arrangement, and doubtless someone on the
German side will get an iron cross or a tin
eagle for thinking it out.
The N.S.W. State Government was very
gracious again the other day. It announced
that the law of the State which says that a
citizen may not carry firearms on the
Sabbath would not be rigidly enforced. Any
section of the Defence force of the Common-
wealth which takes its rifles out on Sunday
afternoons will therefore be free from
arrest. But the N. S. Wales Government
reserves the right to sool on its bobby if the
Australian proceeds to desecrate Sunday
mornings by making himself ready to defend
the country. Wei], the country deserves
this sort of thing for putting up with the
present absurd system of government.
Maybe Italy’s “lone hand” will have to
be pushed into the war sooner than was
expected; for should the Turks seriously
menace the Suez Canal, then, according to
many Italian statesmen, Italy will have
to take a hand at once. This was pointed
out as long ago as the middle of December
in the national Senate by a distinguished
Dago, who said that any danger to the
Canal was too direct a menace to Italy to
be disregarded. Wherefore many things are
less likely than that the first foreigner to
stand alongside the Australian in battle
will be the deliberate gentleman who at
home sells him the banan’.
One of the surprises when the N.S.W. Labor
Caucus selected a State Ministry at the
beginning of last year was the inclusion of
William George Ashford. Nobody had ever
heard of William George before; but there
was a vague belief that he was, in some way.
connected with “the land,” and, therefore,
eminently qualified to be a Minister for Agri-
culture. Whatever he was he has not been
a shining success. Still, there are compen-
sations even in failure. Thus, during the
discussions that took place on the Wheat
Acquisition Bill, it was Attorney-General
Ilall, and not Ashford, who had everything
to say in explanation and defence of that
Bill; and now that Nick Mutton, solicitor
Drew, the £lOOO, and other matters con-
nected with wheat contracts are being talked
of, Ashford may be glad that he wasn’t
trusted with the official explanations and
defences. His own most startling utterance of
late was that he found himself most pro-
foundly affected by the bagpipes; and as
long as he sticks to oracular remarks of that
kind he will be all right.
For the last four months the German
field guns have been roaring helplessly at
their superior French brothers from a camp
on the Aisne where the warring Ciesar once
wintered in the mouldy long ago. If that
conveys nothing to the mind of the inild-
eyed, hopeful Peace fiend, then he is the
maddest dervish of the crowd.
In Melbourne the need for doing some-
thing to protect those who set out to
acquire their sticks on the hire system is
becoming so apparent that soon there will
be danger of even Peacock or. John Weary
noticing it. Just
now employment in
Melbourne is not
plentiful, and many
who still have jobs
are working half-
time or less. This
is right into the
maw of the time-
payment shark, who
in several instances
has seized furniture
on which many in-
stalments have been
paid, owing to the
hire-purchaser fall-
ing a week or two
behind. A firm that
demands the return
of goods which have
been in part paid for
should be in the same
position as a mort-
gagee—entitled to
sell the stuff by
public auction, but compelled to hand to the
original purchaser the balance remaining
after the debt has been liquidated.
Melbourne’s Women’s Hospital is blaming
the Maternity Bonus for a large increase in
the number of cases of septicaemia treated.
It argues, and no doubt rightly, that the
bonus has induced many poor women, who
without it would have come to the hospital,
to experience their little adventures in their
own homes under the care of a more or less
experienced nurse; and the trouble is that
many of these “nurses” are a good deal less
experienced than more. It isn’t fair, how-
ever, to blame the bonus. The fault lies
entirely with the State Government, which
has been repeatedly asked to insist on the
registration of nurses and permit practice by
qualified ones only. It is even more im-
portant that midwives should be qualified
than doctors, because in many cases they
are the sole attendants, and when the doctor
is called in the mischief is done. Yet
Victoria’s lazy Government allows anyone,
even Ah Slim, to practise as a midwife (or
mid-husband) if so minded and if he. she or
it can get enough victims to make it worth
while.

Melbourne’s Tramway Trust proceeds de-
liberately with its scheme to buy out the
plant of the company when the latter’s lease
expires next year, and at latest had decided
to take counsel’s opinion as to its power.
Heaven knows what use gent, one’s opinion
will be, seeing that if the Trust has the
power it hasn’t the money, and can’t get it
without Parliamentary authority; and no
Parliament would dream of handing over
Melbourne’s tramway system to the eleven
inner councils which guaranteed the cost of
the original lines. The whole outfit —cable
and electric lines and all —must be placed
under one comprehensive body—Greater
Melbourne Council or Tramway Commis-
sion, it doesn’t much matter which.
A PRAYER IN TIME OF HASTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Fitzpatrick, M.L.A., said he disagreed with
Mr. James. Lack of time prevented him from
detailing one tithe of the Labor Party’s iniquities.
There was no difference in political immorality
between the State and Federal Houses'.' They were
full brothers, and were out to debauch the whole
community.-— Report of N.S.W. Liberal meeting.
He’d like to make your heart-strings quake with gory
tales of crime;
Your blood would chill to hear him spill his elo-
quence sublime;
Your minds he’d glut with horror -but there really
isn’t time.
Too short a span for mortal man has been allowed
by fate
Before he spits his final hits at objects of his hate,
And with his spleen not voided clean he passes
Heaven’s gate.
For fifty years his ghastly fears he tells with cries
of woe —
A pantomime of Labor crime, that makes a grisly
show ;
Yet things of dread remain unsaid when it is time
to go.
Go down to Hell and search it well; sweep out its
darkest nook,
And lift the lid from pot and grid where nameless
vices cook,
You scarce could glean the things obscene that meet
Fitzpatrick’s look.
To Deeming down from Nero’s crown, from Borgia
on to Burke,
Crime, lust and hate have choked the State —the
bowl, the rope, the dirk. -v
Now Fitz proclaims our greater shames; but it’s a
lifetime’s work.
Holman and Hughes he’d fain abuse while breath is
in his throat;
The damnedest Hun has never done such deeds as
Fitz would quote,
Did not their crime exceed the time allotted to his
gloat.
Iniquities that burn and freeze the souls of men
away,
Could be revealed were he unsealed for fifty hours
a day ;
Since he may roar but twenty-four, we can but
watch and pray.
The Prayer.
From Labor’s guile and Devil’s wile, from spool; and
succubus,
From Fisher’s views and Billy Hughes, and Helioga-
balus,
From. Ilell-hounds fierce and Cann and Pearce, good
Lord deliver us !
From beasts immense and old-man Spence, and the
monstrous fiend Cau-Cus,
From gangrened cysts and Socialists y from Jims who
miss the ’bus.
From Tudor's sib, and Flibbertigib, good Lord deliver
us!
From Labor laws and the worm that gnaws with a
tooth lascivious,
From Union vote and tiger’s throat, and the prowl-
ing platypus,
From the Bible Beast and Fisher at least, good Lord
deliver us !

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Premier Peacock states that the \ ic-
Ministry is divided on the Tote question, and will
leave the long-overdue Bill to he introduced bv a
private member, so there is but a poor chance of any
Bill passing. The large number of people who would
be glad to see the clean, honest betting machine
established for morality's sake and sport s sake, as
well as for the sake of the public charities, are a
careless, disorganised crowd; whereas the smallei
number of people who object to the tote on
“religious” grounds, or for business reasons, or be-
cause their simple ignorance has been imposed upon,
are well organised and spiritedly led. The book-
makers devote a good deal of money and artfulness
to the maintenance of their business, which, by the
way, would be an easier and better business for the
c'eaner members of the “ring” but for the fraterni-
tvfs of gambling rogues who live by backing ’orses.
These latter are more deeply, but less noisily, inter-
ested in blocking the totalisator than even the fatu-
ous wowsers. They are, perhaps, the most useful o:
Ikey Mo’s allies; and they are certainly the most
destructive foes of ordinary punters, in that they get
the cream of the odds against genuine triers, and are
the authors of all tire swindles whereby ordinary
punters so often fall in.

Not more than a dozen nags from Sydney
are likely to cut in for the principal _ stakes at
Melbourne’s Summer-Autumn meetings, but the
little lot includes some of the best. At Caulfield,
next Saturday, the local quality of horesflesh will
be put to the test of comparison in two or three
events. Should Woorak risk a penalty by going for
the Oakleigh Plate (5J furlongs) the punters who
saw his mad performance in the V.lt.C. Derby will
mostly be afraid to back anything else. With
9st. 31b. up, he will look equal to the job. The colt
is said to have increased his velocity since November,
and in this sprint he can meet the talked-of Flash of
Steel on 101 b. better than their Newmarket Handi-
cap terms. Anyhow, they will both be going for
the Futurity Stakes a week later.
Land of Song has been entered for something
next Saturday, but will probably make his first
Australian appearance in the Futurity aforesaid,
which is a unique variety of w.f.a. race. All doubt
about Land of Song being a possible starter for the
Newmarket was settled last week. He was scratched.
If the reputed top-notcher is to be kept for classy
events, as is stated, the owner only knows why he
wasn’t scratched for the Australian Cup also.

* * -- * *
Another boomed lad from U.S.A., where
the wooden bacon comes from, was laid to rest at
the Stadium (Sydney) on Saturday night. That
was Eddie Miller, who was heralded as having “a
remarkable record as a boxer in the United States.
He has never yet been beaten, and the men he has
met include some of the best at their weight in
America.” The other boy, Waverley Geikie, is a
Queenslander, and he, too, has never been beaten,
although he has not had much experience of the
professional ring. If Miller has lived with some of
the best feathers in U.S., it must have been out of
sheer desire on their part to be obliging—or else
he had forgoten his trade on Saturday night. He
looked like a novice, and, after the calm, deliberate
knock-out handed him, he must have felt a novice.
Undoubtedly Geikie is a born boxer—cool, calculat-
ing and quick-handed. His right-cross, which lie
seems to have made a specialty of, is something to
utterly decline acquaintance with. In the third
round the American was pounded down and took
9sec. After that it was a mere matter of minutes,
and the fourth session was the last. The Banana-
lander shepherded his opponent into position and
quietly sent him along a lullaby. The towel went
in from the stranger’s corner before the count-out,
but it was all the same to Miller.
* *• * *
The Australian Pugs’ .Union is on strike
against the pay-rates of Stadiums, Ltd. Briefly, the
position is that the employers part with 25 per cent,
of the gross takings at the gate to the hands engaged
on the job; the latter contend that, owing to the
cost of living, motor-cars, etc., 30 per cent, is the
least they can do with. The co. points out that
since its formation it has been unable to pay a
dividend, and adds that if the Pugs’ Union persists
in its unreasonable demand Capital will sky the
towel, and the stoush emporiums at Sydney, Mel-
bourne and Brisbane will he closed. The first defi-
nite sign of the conflict is the removal of the name
of Les O’Donnell, and the substitution of that of
Jeff Smith, as the opponent of Harold Hardwick
when that ex-pillar of the amateur ring makes his
first appearance in Sydney as a pro. O’Donnell
belongs to the union, and Smith is a free glove-
laborer. All he desires is the right to live and
the left to wallop. Wherefore he will accept the
bosses’ offer of 25 per cent, and chance unpopularity.
It seems to The Bulletin that if booze is a neces-
sary commodity so is bash; which opens the way
for the Commission to be called m as referee^
Fanny Durack, of Australia, lias beaten
her own world’s swimming record of lmin. 18 l-ssec.
for the 100 metres. It happened on Saturday at the
championship meeting of the N.S.W. Amateur Ladies’
Swimming Association, in the Domain baths, when
the time was clipped to lmin. 16 l-ssee. It was a
great sw'im, but the handicapping was more than
the girl-fish could match. A starter with SOsec. was
first, Miss Durack being fourth. At the Olympic
Games at Stockholm, in 1912, her time was Imm.
19 4-ssec. On Saturday she won the 100yds. cham-
pionship of the State in 1 min. 8 2-ssec. Mina
Wylie was second and Lottie Fevyer third. The
tvinner was not unduly hurried.

“Billy” Longworth is swimming so well
just now that great efforts have been made to
arrange a race between him and Duke Kahanamoku
over the 220yds. distance, and much disappoint-
ment is felt at the apparent hopelessness of bringing
it off. On Saturday, at Lavender Bay, Sydney,
Longworth won the 220yds. championship of Eastern
Suburbs in 2min. 32 l-ssec. Beaurepaire’s Aus-
tralasian record is 2min. 28 2-ssec, and Longworth
has done better than 2min. 32 l-ssec. himself.
Against the Hawaiian he would, fit and well, doubt-
less do much bette r . Kahanamoku suggests the
bath at the ’Frisco Exhibition during the Games as
the best place to decide the argument.
The Tanunda (S.A.) people, mostly of
German extraction, have held their annual meeting
to settle the “kingship” for rifle-shooting. _ The first
two places went to Lake and Fielding, visitors from
Adelaide, and frequent prizewinners in the past.
But Liebelt and Grocke and Nitschke and Dier and
Dald were close up; and the shooting was done on
Mrs. Menner’s land; and Mrs. Heinemann provided
lunch ; and Juttner conducted the band ; and another
Juttner, as president, congratulated the winner, who
was decorated with the customary wreath by Miss
Schulz. Secretary Henzenroeder, who ran the whole
thing, mentioned at the subsequent banquet that any
number of German-named Australians —including
one Kaysar ! —have gone to the front
to fight against much the same sort
of bullying tyranny as drove the best
men out of Prussia three-quarters of
a century ago.
BASEBALL.—HA MOLD FRANKS , of
Leichhardt , pitching.
The remarkable success of Murray Bridge
in S. Australian rowing has attracted attention to
the river as a nursery for oarsmen. The “Bridge”,
style does not blend over-well with that of the
Adelaide clubs, and, as distance prevents joint prac-
tice, it has happened that the Murray Club’s best
crew has liad to row as representing the whole State.
A sporting annual round has now been arranged, City
crews visiting the Bridge for a regatta on Saturday,
and all parties then moving up-river to Mannum for
the Foundation Day holiday. Bridgeport and Tailern
Bend are other river clubs that now compete, and, of
the visitors, one boat this year .came from as far away
as Port Pirie. Excepting for ladies’ races, Murray
Bridge scooped most of the events on both days.
The Rugby League of N. S., • Wales will
have a visit from a Maoriland team this season and
will return the compliment. In the Loanly Isle?
the game is struggling to find a place in the. .-suju
of public esteem, but it isn’t making' a,Conspicuous',
success of the job. Nevertheless, a smart -team is
expected to cross the Tasman and play in N. S.
Wales and Queensland. The N.S.W. Rugby Union,
on the ground that “this is no time or sport,” has
wiped all representative fixtures off its slate, and
the Metropolitan Union will limit the competition
games to just sufficient to keep members physically
fit. As much spare time as possible will be given
over to drill in the evenings and on Saturday
afternoons.
* * * *
“’Oof Ball”: N.S.W. Football Union lias
followed the example of file English Cricket County
Clubs, and will subordinate play to drill. The
League, on the other hand, explains that by keeping
going it helps players and others to the extent of
£lOO a week. It isn’t much of an explanation;
and, anyway, what about the crowds of men who
spend their leisure howling’ on the bank at the
games? What good are they getting out of it?
Federal Attorney-General Hughes tersely remarked
last .week that people of a certain mould failed to
read the accounts of the bloody encounters in Flan-
ders and Poland with the same interest with which
they followed cricket, football and racing. The
truth in this remark stings. The writer realised it
one night recently at a picture-show. The principal
film was a battle story. It was an ingenious fake,
of course, but the frightful meaning of war was
vividly produced. The next item was “Topical
Gazette—Australia: Country Tennis Week, Double
Bay, Sydney.” The startling contrast, the fatuity
of big men rushing after tennis balls, 'while the mind
was still under the influence of the horrors merely
hinted at a few minutes earlier, evidently struck the
audience, for a queer, rustling noise went right
round the building. I suspect that picture-showman
of being a recruiting-sergeant in disguise. I know
we can’t all go to the front, and we must have
relaxation; but cut out the cant that the football
league is patriotically helping the Empire by run-
ning a big season in order to pay some wages, mostly
to men who are already getting wages. The League
hopes to extract £lO,OOO gross as a moderate esti-
mate this year from the public, and £lO,OOO would
equip a battalion, or thereabouts.
X * * *
“Chug-chug”: Why do returned trippers
always try to disparage Australia ? If this country
and its people are no good 'it is quite easy to stay
away. John Ferguson, Scotchbyterian divine, re-
turned to Sydney after a 12 months’ absence, and
at once told us that the serious-mindedness of the
British public respecting the war was in contrast
to the indifference in Australia. “He declares,” said the
S. M. Herald summary, “that all the British boxers
have gone to the front, and he expresses surprise
that boxing is continued in Sydney.” In the same
issue of the same paper appeared a cable from
London which set forth that “Johnny Summers
(ex-eliampion) met the Lonsdale* belt holder, Private
Basham, who recently defeated him for the title,
in a return match at Liverpool. Basham won on
points.” Has Liverpool been bombed out of
Britain? Or is John Ferguson’s statement merely
another instance of the serious-minded handling
the truth carelessly ?
* * * *
The visit of Spalding’s team, in 1889,
first interested Australians in baseball. Since then
the game has had its ups
and downs, but during the
last two or three years has
leaped ahead, particularly
in Sydney. Last season
some 60 clubs and 20
junior . clubs were under the
control of the N.S.W.
Baseball Assoc. The game
is played regularly in the
winter in N. S. Wales, Vic-
toria, S. Australia and Tas-
mania. It is also played in
summer in the Ma State.
The schools in N.S.W. and
Vic. play it, and there is a
junior assoc, in Adelaide.
The visit of the Giants-
White Sox outfit from Uncle
Dollar’s Land in Jan-
uary of last year gave the
thing a big boost, and, in-
cidentally, was an educa-
tion to Australian players,
the tourists being high-
class professional players.
The prospects of the com-
ing winter are good, pro-
vided war does not unduly
interfere. Substantial in-
creases in the numbers
playing are expected, not-
withstanding that many ’bailers have already
swapped club for rifle.
Unless something unforeseen occurs the inter-
state Baseball Carnival (N. S. Wales, Victoria and
S. Australia) will be held in Adelaide this year.
* * * *
In preparation for the football season
the Vic. League lately took the opinions of its
nine clubs (there used to be 10, but ’Varsity has
withdrawn) on certain weighty matters, and will
proceed to carry out the decisions as far as it can.
It was singular that to every proposal put to the
clubs seven agreed and two objected, though they
were in no ease the same two. Also, it was more
than singular that Richmond and Melbourne, whose
average attendances are the poorest, were the two
who objected to pooling the “gates” and paying
players out of the proceeds. South and Carlton
alone objected to having “one ruling body for Vic.
football,” which in effect meant the disappearance
of the Association ; Essendon and ’Wood were against
the League’s quaint scheme of “district” football
(this scheme puts Fern Tree Gully in “Melbourne,”
and Mitcham in “Collingwood”) ; and ’Roy and
Richmond were against abandoning the usual S’Aus-
tralian fixture this season. Also a majority favored
having but nine, teams for the season, as a trial.
The delegates wdte further notified that the S. Aus.
League and the" NjS.W/ Rugby League—there was
no mention of'ith'e'RugbyV.tlnion—had agreed to the
proposed hybrid game, but in the absence of exact
information as to the changes the League postponed
consideration. This was pyobably just as well,
because in view of the capers- of Billy the Mad Goat
and the necessity for correcting them, there may not
be much ’ball this season. Certainly this isn’t the
time to begin to popularise a new game, however
improved it may be.

At Maori'] aprl’s lawn tennis champion-
ship contests, which''took place recently in Dunedin,
there were no surprises like those of last year, when the
alleged champions went a| seven for sixpence. This
year the acknowledged players swept the board.
G. Ollivier, champion in 1911, took the men’s
singles, and with F. Wilding (a brother of Anthony)
also won the men’s doubles. In the ladies’ singles,
Miss A. Gray, last year’s champion, showed that
she was still ditto; while the combined doubles were
annexed by a veteran player from Christchurch, W.
Goss, who had Miss 12. M. Baird for his partner.
Canterbury took the championship banner with 121
points. Auckland was second with 51.
Another body that begs to be excused on
account of war is N. S. Wales Cricket Assoc. The
proposed invasion of Westralia and Tasmania is
definitely off.
The beginning of the eighth round of First-
grade Cricket in Sydney on Saturday was a bean-
feast for the bowlers. Trundling' for Waverley
against Gordon, Flynn closed the day’s business with
eight for 20. He got six for 16 in the first innings
and so far as the second innings went he bagged
two for four. Macartney, who was bowled first ball
(by Shea, who collected three for 34), then took a
hand with the ball and got some of his own back
with an average of five for 38. For University,
against Cumberland, Minnett sent six batsmen baik
for 38 and his ally Gregg got four for 20. Wall, for
Balmain, against Middle Harbor, routed four of the
enemy in the first innings for 24. He was assisted
by Arnott, who could get only four for 29. in
second-grade (second-grade is mostly a total
stranger), Gibbons, North Sydney, downed five
of Mosman for 25. In retaliation, Jacob, of
Mosman, obliterated six for 31, and Chap-
man gave him moral support and Christian sym-
pathy with four for 25. Three for 28 (Andrews, of
Gordon) is hardly worth mentioning, but the con-
tribution by Frances of seven Gordon lives for 46
is received with applause. Ditto, Penman (5-49),
Balmain; Lisle (5-47), Paddington; T. Owen
(3-14), Western Suburbs; Leggo (4-18), Peter-
sham; Flack (4-16), Leichhardt; Wallace (3-9),
University. Kellewav (run out, 138) was the only
batsman to reach three figures.
* * * *
Victorian bowler E. A. McDonald, who-
captured four wickets for 90 against N.S.W., and
11 for 74 against Queensland, is the season’s only-
fast inter-State trundler. It was his first appearance
in Sheffield Shield cricket, and provided he makes
good in the last match (against South Aus.), he
should have more than an ordinary chance of a
seat in the future Australian XI. Just how future
the next Australian -XI will be nobody knows.
* * * *
A. E. J. Collins, smiter of the world’s
record cricket score of 628 —it was at Clifton College
(Eng.), in 1899—was killed lately in the war that
William made.
At a cricket match played on the South
Melbourne ground recently the “Biffs” made 165
runs for the loss of five wickets. Then the Arous
team,-which, as a rule, gives a good account of
itself, fell down dead for three runs. Lockyer (of
the South Melbourne C.C. second eleven) outed five
of them for none, and Franks (of the South Mel-
bourne Footballers’ C.C.) dissolved the other five
for three. Only 36 balls were bowled.
Some ex-Australian XI trundlers keep
up the old traditions in junior cricket. At Penrith
(N.S.W.) the other day Bill Howell gathered 10
wickets for 33 runs against Mount Druitt. Ernest
Jones —one time “the Australian Express”—went
even better at Fremantle (W.A.) by taking 12 for
32 runs.
* * * *
Austin Diamond, the secretary of the
rifle-club movement among cricketers in Sydney, has
represented N.S.W. at both cricket and baseball.
’Way back in 1907 he scored 210 runs v. Victoria,
and' joined the few batsmen who had crossed the
double-century line in Australian cricket. Diamond
has added the ginger of enthusiasm to whatever lie
has touched ; and the care of this comer of the rifle
movement is therefore in good hands.
Championship games have’ been in pro-
gress on N. S. Wales and Victorian bowling greens
during the week. In Sydney, competitors had been
reduced to four in the single-handed pla.v, but not
without strenuous contests in trying conditions.
The scoffer who reckons that bowls are a mild form
of exercise desig-ned to enable old buffers to get up
an appetite for the afternoon’s whisky should have
been on the lawns on one of last week’s humid days,
rolling wood for four or five hours. He would
have come away' wiser and more respectful. I p at
Newcastle, too, championship games have _ been
arousing interest. Hingst,. who won the singles,
belongs to a family of bowlers. Ilis father and
brother have also held the championship.
Walter Bleach, the Victorian bowling
champion, is one of those who have more than one
life in the game. Time after time he has blossomed
forth anew as a record breaker. Almost two decades
have passed since lie first came into prominence, and
in the days of the great Waverley Carnival, when it
was decided to give the fixture to N.S.W. and
Victoria alternately, Bleach was champion at the
Melbourne meeting. Without doubt no bearer of
the title has more deserved it and few are as populai
with fellow-plavers.

This alarming
statement, which
has appeared in
the influential
newspapers of this
country, should
arouse the public
to the fact that
bites from mos-
quitoes and other
insects are things
to worry about.
It is quite pos-

blood is in a poor
state an insect
bite might set up
such serious in-
flammation as to
cause blood-poi-
soning, and —as everyone well knows—blood-
poisoning may lead to even the amputation of
a limb.
One has only to glance at the illustration
given here of the mosquito to realise what
terrifying poison-injecting parts this insect
has. It is absolutely necessary, not only for
the woman who desires to keep her complexion
in its natural condition, but also for the pro-
tection against more serious dangers, to secure
some reliable preparation which will prevent
insects from biting.
The best form of treatment of mosquito bites
is prophylactic, or preventive. The application
of a little of the Nev-a-bite lotion to the
forehead, neck and the instep (when openwork
stockings are worn) reduces the probability of
mosquito-bites to a minimum.

W. E. Smith, Ltd., formed to take over an
old-established printing and stationery busi-
ness in Sydney, as from October, 1910: —
Written
Profits. Dividends. Off. Deficit.
Oct., 1910 £19,068
Apr., 1911 .. £3947 10 p.c. =£3408 15,529
Oct., 1911 .. 5354 10 „ „ 3408 £677 17,260

The share capital is £75,000, and on the
foregoing figures £64,431 of it is intact: the
balance is made up of goodwill, preliminary
exes, and trading debit. The more or less
tangible assets total £86,620; but against
them there are liabilities amounting to
£22,189. The tangible assets include book
debts, £12,563; stock, £35,155; plant,
£22.504; factory (presumably freehold),
£(5904; and liquid items, £5293. After pro-
viding for liabilities there is in these items
£6O 230, which means 16s. per share. But
plainly the assets are not worth 16s. ‘ to
the shareholders as things have been' going
lately. A plant, for instance, which can’t
grind out profits is not worth £22,504 to
keep; and for breaking-up purposes it is
worth far less than £22.504. The whole
question is: Can the management be im-
proved so that profits will result? It is
absurd to suppose otherwise. The business
may perhaps be unable to pay dividends on
watered capital; but on the capital actually
in it and wisely invested, there should be a
fair return. Old W. E. Smith made it pay
all right. No doubt he had his own peculiar
methods. But if this co. is not prepared
to adopt them, too, it has the advantage,
which lie didn't possess, of having a share
connection which certainly would bring
business if this co. could offer samples and
in-ices even approximately as good as other
firms. If the co. doesn’t pay it is almo t
certainly due to bad management. The
directors- —who are capable men, but without
any special knowledge at all of this business
—apparently realise that something is
wrong, and they have experimentally set up
two new lieiids in place of a general man-
ager : there is now a “managing secretary”
and a trade manager. There is no inherent
reason why this scheme should fail.
The directors propose further to clean up
the balance-sheet by writing down capital
to represent unrealisable items. In addition
to the intangible £10,569 shown in the table,
there is £3561 of capital represented by
leasehold, and probably another £lOOO by
fixtures, etc., in these leasehold premises.
The capital will therefore probably be
reduced to £60,000. This will relieve the
co. of the present burden of providing every
half-year for depreciation of these fixtures,
amortising the leasehold, and writing down
the goodwill. Say £5OO a half- year will be
thus available for distribution —if the co.
can make it. And of course the co. should
make that and a great deal more, though, on
the other hand, this is not the sort of busi-
ness from which dazzling results may reason-
ably _be expected. There is too much com-
petition for that —especially the competition
ot the little printer-man who works in his
own shop any hours he likes, and would in
most cases rather take a job to return him
only a shilling an hour than be idle
altogether.

And that the big additions to reserves have
been something more than a bookkeeping
expansion you, can tell from this: —
Dec.,’o4. Dee.,’l4.
Liquid assets.. ..£16,854 £38,134
Other assets . . . . 22,288 31,873

So in 10 years, after paying out the dm,
running from 8 up to 30 p.c., the co. has
added £21,354 to its liquid assets (mostly
public securities), and put another £9500
or so to the non-liquid assets. And there
has been no writing-up of these latter. The
co.’s premises, for instance, stand in the
books at the same price now as they did 10
years ago, though they are certainly worth
more. Not, of course, that they should
therefore have been written up: on the con-
trary, since the building must be replaced
some day, no matter how the value of the
freehold may grow, a replacement fund
ought to be provided out of written-off
profits. Still that is a small matter. The
larger facts as to this co. are that in 10
years it has paid its big dividend and put
by an amount one-fifth larger than its entire
capital, and that the assets representing
capital and reserves are sound, so far as
one can see, and one can see most of the way.
Also the trustee business is one which is
almost bound to expand out of all proportion
to the expansion of the country, while the
business in hand or -practically assured will
carry the co. over some years, and will be
comparatively little affected by the gloomy
time which is ahead of Victoria.

How the German cavalry has fallen to
pieces! A dozen years ago A. G. Hales,
writing up the German army reviews, noted
as a fatal defect the cavalryman’s inability
to “nurse” his horse. Apparently Hales,
who looked at these things with the eye of
an Australian busliman, was not far wrong.
Nordhausen. the birthplace of Lody, who
was recently shot in London as a spy. has
decided to honor his memory by planting an
oak. Maybe Nordhausen realises what hor-
rors German sculptures are, and chose an
oak instead. On the other hand, it may
simply have learned that bronze in the
Fatherland has risen to £l6O a ton.
Mary Gaunt, whose “Woman in China” is
going to be the most-read travel book of the
year, has three brothers making themselves
unpleasant to strangers. One is the second
Australian to achieve rear-admiral’s rank;
another is a commodore; a third is at the
front as Captain of Dragoon Guards. Two
other brothers, following their father, the
late County Court Judge of Victoria, are
barristers. The Argus, by the way, always
refers to the venturesome daughter of the
regiment as Mrs. Mary Gaunt, whereas her
married name is Miller.
General von Emmieli, who lately allowed
himself to be interviewed by a U.S. journal-
ist, assured the U.S. journalist—we have
the U.S. journalist’s word for it —that the
English are excellent fighters. The mild
story doesn’t sound incredible. It was this
von Emmieli to whom the heroic Belgian
General Leman tendered his sword after the
Liege forts had been pounded to pieces;
whereupon the stocky victor bent in the
middle and refused the ironmongery, with
the remark that it had been an honor to
cross swords with so brave a man. Alto-
gether von Emmieli seems to be trying to win
the reputation of being the best-mannered
Hun in Europe. He has few competitors.
The assumption that the typical Aus-
tralian of the future will more or less
resemble the generic aboriginal, even as the
American is reverting to the Red Indian,
gets little confirmation from figures which
Statistician Ivnibbs lately arrived at after
examining the papers of over 120,000
trainees. Of the whole lot 47.5 per cent,
wore brown hair, 27.5 per cent, fair hair,
19 per cent, black or blackish, and 6 per-
cent. were ginger. Forty-two and a half
per cent, used blue eyes, 29 per cent, brown,
19.5 grey and 9 per cent, what “Ouida”
would call amber. In combinations fair
hair and blue eyes accounted for 15.5 per-
cent., brown hair and blue eyes 19 per cent.,
brown hair and brown eyes 15 per cent., black
hair and brown eyes 9 per cent., and brown
hair and grey eyes 9 per cent. There isn’t
much reversion to Binglii about that lot.
Dear B., —I see by the London papers that
the British officers and men have arranged
a series of inter-trench football matches;
also that a pack of fox-hounds has been
brought over from England, while guns are
being freely bought wherewith to shoot the
pheasants which abound in the mangel-
wurzel patches. I hope to proceed to the
front myself in the spring, and being a keen
sportsman, I should be glad of a few tips.
Should I wear the pink coat and buttons of
my local hunt club, or would it be in better
taste if—being in a ‘ foreign country—l
turned out in a plain dark coat and bowler?
Are the Flanders pheasants driven, or does
one walk them up? In the latter case what
about dogs? Should I bring them or would
it be cheaper to get them on the Continent?
Also what facilities are provided for keeping
sporting dogs in the trenches? I have often
heard of the Rhine salmon, and have fasted
that most horrible of savories, Rhine lax.
What is the salmon-fishing like on the
Dusseldorf-Cologne-Frankfort line, which is
where our armies are to break through, ulti-
mately, I understand? Are Australian flies
any good for the Rhine, and. if not, are flies
classed as loot under the Hague agreement
if forcibly seized from a Cologne trader who
has no further use for them? What do
they charge for the best Stadium seats at
Brussels, Liege. Dusseldorf, Magdeburg and
Berlin —this being the route I propose to
follow? And do the promoters make any
reduction for soldiers in uniform? Do the
German girls—but perhaps I have asked
enough for the present. Awaiting an early
reply.—Yours eagerly, Call an Park.
From London :
Passed out. Lady Sherbrooke, second wife of the
“Bobby” Lowe who was a conspicuous figure in
Sydney in the ’4o's, She was Miss Caroline Sneyd
when she became his secretary and amanuensis, and
she made herself so useful in those capacities that
■ Bobby resolved to secure her services for the . re-
mainder of his stay on earth. The lady promptly
elicited uncomplimentary remarks by the misdirected
energy with which she trotted her blind and septu-
agenarian husband around London social functions
and festivities. She wasn’t going to hide her light
as Lady Sherbrooke under a bushel! The killing of
a favorite nephew in action caused a shock that
proved fatal. Her death has evoked many reminis-
cences of the brilliant, eloquent and irascible Bob,
the most interesting of them contributed to the Pall
Mall Gazette by J. F. Hogan, another ex-Aus-
tralian who rose to some eminence in the Commons,
and who wrote a book on Lowe’s Australian career.
“Gunner”: An impromptu demonstration
of the effects of lyddite occurred at an ammu-
nition factory in Yorkshire recently, and
advertised tlie ghastly possibilities of this
death-dealer if it could only be dumped in
bulk on to an enemy. One of three maga-
zines in which the high-explosive shells were
stored blew up. and this was the extent of
tlie casualty list: Killed, 7; injured, 20
(there were only 30 workmen about at the
time) ; factory wrecked; two large iron
beams burled 800yds.; every window within
a mile shattered; and a bole left in the
ground big enough to bury tlie Past. _ The
men who manufacture lyddite practically
live in the firing-line. The shell consists of
a forged-steel case into which melted picric
acid is poured ; and all sorts of precautions
have to be taken to prevent the acid com-
bining with lead, soda and other materials
which would produce sensitive picrates and
disaster. With the increased protection
given to troops in the field, the lyddite shell
has become less used, but it is still the most
deadly thing that can be hurled against an
army in the open or behind light conceal-
ments.
“Hon. lieggie": “Fruit for the Masses I”
shouts Sydney Telegraph, like a benevolent
earl distributing relief at the village soup
kitchen. In Heaven’s name, who are the
(’lasses in this democracy of ours that don’t
want reasonably cheap fruit? 1 know several
amiable lady readers of the Telegraph who
would cut me dead for ever if I dared to
suggest that then belonged to the Masses.
And yet they are clamoring for cheap fruit
just as loudly as their washerladies. who, in
their turn, reckon themselves some Class.
When Austria has been beaten fiat, Ger-
many may find a last use for the remains by
making them carry the blame for the whole
catalogue of atrocities. Ellis Barker, in the
Nineteenth Century, writes : “Austria has
always ruled with barbaric methods. The
atrocious acts of which Germany has been
guilty in Belgium and France were taught
by Austria.” And lie asserts that, before
leaving London, Prince Lielmowsky, referring
to poor old Franz Joseph’s country, said to
a friend: “Germany goes to war with a
corpse hanging round her neck.” Since then
it has added Turkey to the necklace. For
companions William has now therefore the
Sick and the Dead.
One horrible feature of the present war
is that the fighting follows the lines of
densest population. According to I)r. Bar-
tholomew’s map the country is thicker with
people along three-fourths of the French
battle front than anywhere else in France
except in the immediate environs of Paris
and Lyons. All the Belgian fighting has been
in districts containing in normal times more
than 500 people to the square mile. Again,
the Russian battles, with the exception of
those in East Prussia, which is rather
a thin and hungry land, have raged where
homes are most numerous. Near Lodz and
from that city north and south to the Polish
frontier the population is as thick as in
Devonshire and Sussex, and it is the srtme
east and west of Lemberg; while for a hun-
dred miles or thereabout east of Cracow and
in practically the whole of the province of
Bukovina there are more people to the square
mile than in any county of England outside
the great industrial areas. Even the Servian
section of the scrap has been fought in that
portion of the Balkan Peninsula where men
and women and little children, not to men-
tion pigs, are more plentiful than elsewhere
except at Bucharest and within 20 miles <>f
Constantinople. Nowhere in the wesrern
half of Ireland is the population as dense as
at the most thinly-peopled parts of any of
the European battle-fronts.
“Coot”: The case of William Ebenezer
Reed, a modern Enoch Arden (B. 14/1/T5).
reminds me of Charles Hamilton, who died
intestate at Windsor (Vic.) in 1898. _ A
Trustee Co. obtained letters to administer
Jus estate, on the motion of the dead man’s
brother. When the co. came to investigate
regarding the next of kin it discovered that
Hamilton’s wife had deserted him some 15
years before, and had disappeared. After
advertising, it obtained an order of the
Court presuming her to have predeceased
her husband. Thereupon the estate was cut
up between Hamilton’s brothers, sisters,
nephews and nieces. Six years later the
officially-deceased lady turned up and
demanded her share of the estate of the dear
departed. The co. of course simply showed
her the Court’s assertion that she was a
corpse. As it happened, the last of Hamil-
ton’s nephews just about that time attained
the age of 21, and, being a sport, he directed
that his share of the estate should be paid
to his mucli-alive (and recently-married)
aunt. That ended the bother.
“Cathopresbycongreganglitist” : A contro-
versy has arisen, “When Christians fight, are
they Christians?” It is a tough problem.
A foundation plank of the Christian platform
lays it down that if you are smitten on the
right whisker it is your duty to turn your
left to the smiter. Clearly the candidate
who doesn’t do this is not entitled to his
diploma as a Christian. On the other hand,
the man who in time of pressing trouble
doesn’t put up a scrap isn’t a Christian either
for long—lie is a corpse. So the only way to
be a Christian would seem to be to go into a
lonely desert where there is nothing to
quarrel with you. and smite yourself alter-
nately on the right jaw and the left in a
mild kind of way that won’t make you dislike
yourself enough to retaliate. That is how
ancient hermits solved the problem, and it
is about the only way it can be solved.
“10-2-4” : The master-characteristic of the
German since the war began is an optimism
bordering on insanity. In October and
November they were all satisfied that the
Kaiser would be in Paris by Christmas.
Two months ago General Liman von San-
ders, chief of the German mission to Turkey,
told the Hungarian paper, Pesti Hirlap,
that the Russians couldn’t hold out much
longer and France already had had enough.
About the same time an intelligent German
business man got into difficulties in London
over liis hotel bill. He had never been in
arrears before, and put down his embarrass-
ment to the war. Jn a month, lie said, lie
would be able to pay. The magistrate asked
why. and the bland answer was that, by that
time Germany would be in the courtyard of
the Bank of England. Do they believe all
this bellowdrama, or is the whole nation
skiting absurdly to keep its courage up?
“0.K.” : British patriotism screams for the
“capture of German trade” as the most prac-
tical means of bursting up the German dream
of World Conquest.; and all the while Aus-
tralian supper parties eat and drink an revoir
to Australian soldiers at the restaurants of
guttural aliens, who mutter “Germany must
vin in der long r-run.” Inoffensive German
workers at the wharves and elsewhere are
boycotted by their former mates, whilst
offensive German employers of German vait-
ers are fattening on the patronage of luxuri-
ous Australian feeders. These people woulc.
patronise all-Australian fooderies if they
were tempted ; but nobody makes a patriotic-
appeal to their stummicks. They trade with
the enemy at the restaurants for ' the same
reason that they buy their oysters and fruit
from a Dago and their vegetables from a
Chow. Australians are the least patriotic
people in the world as regards their food
purchases, because they have never troubled
to think about the matter, and nobody has re-
minded them in a business-like manner ot
their foolish indifference,

IN BANANA LAND
“Acorn” : The Australian doesn’t have 'to
import everything. Organs, for instance.
Recently, the Brisbane City Tabernacle de-
sired one of these instruments, and in a
burst of patriotism, turned the foreigner
down. In some doubt, the job was given to
a local firm, and the result, in volume of
s.ound and quality of tone, has cut the
ground from under the importing maniac’s
hoofs.
Gilbert White, the Bishop of Carpentaria,
just chosen to be Bishop of Willochra, in
S. Australia, leaves the quaintest “cathe-
dral” in the Commonwealth—and one of the
quaintest anywhere. It is at Thursday
Island, and, like all other buildings at
“Thirsty,” is of wood and iron. The
“windows” are French doors, so that the
place can be the better thrown open to the
evening breeze. The building was put up as
a memorial to those who were drowned in
the Quetta disaster, and all about the walls
are the coral-encrusted copper parts of port-
holes and other relics of the wreck.
“Manx”: “Queenslander” says (B.
28/1/T5) that the Commonwealth Govern-
ment has made a mess of oil prospecting in
Papua, and remarks that “a boring plant in
Queensland is expected to average 3000 ft.
a year.” Will it interest “Queenslander”
to know that with many oilrigs in America
300 ft. has been drilled in a day? One
recent Oklahoma tally was 2119 ft. in three
weeks. The Australian has never drilled
for _ oil, and he shouldn’t be asked to try
until an American rig with an American
crew has given him a demonstration. What
were Dr. Wade’s qualifications, anyhow?
He is an English chemist and geologist, but
on what oilfield did he gain experience as
an oil-well engineer? When he recommended
and installed on the Government oil lands
of Papua a slow and. inefficient rotary sys-
tem of boring, instead of the rapid and
effectual jumper drill, he at once raised
doubts about his knowledge of petroleum
mining. If the Commonwealth has “made a
mess of oil prospecting in Papua” the mess
is due to its first error of engaging an Eng-
lish geologist and not an American drill
man.
AFTER THE FIRE.
SMALL BOY ( who has been poking among the ruins): “ Hey , Missus, I've found the
key of yer door."
From a Courier article of 50 years ago:
i No one who walks the streets with his eyes open
can be insensible to the increase of immorality
that is evidenced on every hand. Lais and Aspasia
flaunt brazen in all our public places.
Strange that when we are 60 the world is
always wickeder than it was when we were
25!
Affluent Arnold Weinholt, ex-member for
Fassifern in the Assembly, has a passion for
big game. He resigned his seat in the State
House to fight Andrew Fisher in the Wide
Bay electorate. Trampled flat there he went
to Africa to pursue his hobby, and has come
back, crippled from a mawling by a lion. A
man who is so unfortunate with lions should
stick to the insect life of State politics.
The Bananaland brand of Binghi showed
to advantage the other day. At a pub out
towards Sundown, a “wanted” man held the
bar with a loaded rifle while the publican
obediently and without argument handed
out free poison to a full house. The im-
promptu and honorary bushranger promised
a supply of lead to the first man who
showed trouble. Everyone took his word
for it except Binghi. Watching his oppor-
tunity our sun-burnt brother grabbed the
gun, shot the hon. bushranger dead, and
then turned and ran as if 10 flaming devils
had been loosed on him. He hasn’t returned
yet.
During last session the Assembly passed
400 miles of railway—about 2 i million
pounds worth. The bulk of this won’t earn
grease for many years, but the Commis-
sioner is expected to make the lines com-
mercially successful all the same. He must
make the railways pay, but keep down fares
and freight. He must provide interest on
capital, and yet build lines of which he dis-
approves. He must push out suburban lines
and yet do nothing to interfere with Badger’s
trams. And if he can’t run political lines
on commercial principles, he is a fraud and
they want to kick him out.
The Ruins of Baalbek.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Not in our stones is our full story told,
Grey in the twilight’s pall, gold in the gold
Of the pure washings of the Syrian suns
Where from our feet green-sown El Bekaa
runs. '
Baal was our god, and Helios, and here
The pale Christ kept his Godhead many a
year
Until the Arab sheik, the hawk-eyed man,
Steered through the shifting sands a caravan,
And with the cunning born of need and
stress
Fashioned a fortress of our mightiness.
Here where Diana ! shone in silver dress
Above some leaf-crowned, splendid naked-
ness,
Bacchus leapt through in pining revelry
And swung the large-limbed woman at his
knee.
This door was Jupiter’s. And yet again
The white moon lifts above the shadowy
plain,
But the great gods are gone. An Emperor
came.
They scrawled a mural tablet with his name,
Man’s memory on man’s memory to impress,
Where gods have gone to long forgetfulness.
Our courts are strewn with columns. Deep
and cool
Were the still waters in the hallowed pool
That cleansed the victim for the sacrifice.
Fate tossed once more. The Christian held
the dice.
He built a church above the altar stains.
The church is gone, The empty pool
remains.
Carved in the clusters of a woven vine
Blown poppies ’mid the fruits' and wheat-
■ ears twine:
Fauns and bacchantes leer from giant doors!
And sly grey lizards snort in broken floors.
And sometimes like a dream of' summer trees
Comes the thick whispering of sun-drunk
bees.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 6, 1915.
My Dear Tiiargomindah,—
The Osterley took a host of Queens-
landers southward, a good many of them
being from the Darling Downs. Mrs. Alex.
McPhie (a Toowoomba matron), Mr. and
Mrs. Charlie Lethbridge (the latest Too-
woomba wedded pair) and Mr. and Mrs.
Macaulay Turner (also newly-wed) were
among the ship’s congregation.
Mrs. A. H. Barlow and her portfolioed
husband have just returned from Tasmania,
where they helped themselves to a little cool
weather. Mr. Pagan, of the railways, and
his popular missus are other Queenslanders
who are revelling in Apple Island climate.
Beatrice Marshall, elder daughter of the
Brisbane Town Clerk, died this week.
To-day, with the mercury almost at frying
point, numbers of women and girls filled the
city and sold asters —bunches, basketsful,
ciumps or single blooms—for the enrichment
of the Belgian fund. The weather coridi-
tions dried up the blossoms early, but the
fountain of Brisbane’s charity kept spouting
coins till a late hour.
The National Council of Women is again
getting up steam after a lengthy pause. Its
initial exertions will be the arrangement of
an At Home in thanksgiving for the return
of the Council’s president, Mrs. Kingsbury.
She went all the way to England and the
adjacent tourist resorts.
Mrs. Knox Carson, first known in Bris-
bane as Miss Williams, a pretty English
girl who turned the heads of our best-pre-
served bachelors and finally married Knox
Carson, has had news of the death of her
brother Leo in action in Belgium at the end
of January. He visited Brisbane some years
ago and made many friends.
Mabel Brady and Betty Cockburn, two of
our best-dressed girls, rolled home by the
Osterley on Monday. Miss Brady has been
away . two years, and Miss Cockburn half
that time. They both are sporting the latest
thing in skirts—the species with the long
tunic. Miss Brady has brought, for the im-
provement of our millinery education, a
sample of the Tommy Atkins toque—a little
black velvet thing, becoming only to those
who possess regular features, and quite out
of place on a bearded lady or a person with
an up-turned nose and a double chin. Miss
Brady has none of these misfortunes. The
voyagers say that when they left England
the Tipperary cap, a jaunty thing worn over
one eye, and fluttering two little black tails
or ribbon, was being worn by women who
like to fly their patriotic colors at the mast,
so to speak.
Miss Brady went to see the artists’ corps
dulling in London, and to her intense joy
beheld the tuneful Kennerley, of the Butt
family, clad in dungarees and a sweater,
laboriously going through his martial paces.
Another Australian girl who recently re-
turned to Brisbane had an unusual piece of
excitement in John Bull’s country. She
went to inspect a big camp, and snapshotted
freely as she wandered around. Then she
got into a motor-car to go home, but two
drum-majors or rifle-rangers hurried after
her and politely but firmly led her to the
commandant. He finally “warned” her and
let her go, which shows the luck some
thoughtless people havd.
Mollie Ferrier and Julian Mackay (of
Giro station, N.S.W.) murmured the'usual
“I wills” on Wednesday afternoon at St.
John’s Cathedral. The church was packed,
principally with the bride’s school friends.
They rolled up almost to a girl. The four
bridesmaids—Miss Mackay (the groom’s sis-
ter), Jean Goertz, Peggy Ferrier and the
younger Ross Munro—wore beautifully soft
dresses of shell-pink crgpe de chine and lace,
and big black hats tricked out with pink
roses.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
"Machete” : The youthful pirate who tried
to steal a Sydney launch and break for the
Islands (B. 4/2/’l5) was a poor imitation
of a trio who made a similar attempt to
get out of Brisbane two years ago and streak
for the Carolines. They stole a powerful
motor-launch, provisioned her, equipped her
with arms, ammunition and petrol (all
stolen), and got clear away to sea. They
made only one error. They forgot to steal
lubricating oil, and when they tried to make
up the deficiency with dripping from the
larder the chapter of adventures closed. They
made the Queensland coast under canvas,
and reached the lockup under escort.
“Paddy Melon”: “Wunbo” (B. 28/1/T5)
is on the right track about the official “pro-
tection” of Queensland aborigines. Last
year, at a station on the Diamantina, a
pamp of aged blacks were practically starv-
ing. It was flood time and their means of
obtaining tucker was cut off by miles and
miles of water. The manager of the station
informed the proper authority at Birdsville,
but no notice was taken. In the meantime
the station was supplying the unfortunate
blacks with tucker. The manager next
wrote to headquarters, and was curtly in-
formed that Birdsville would inquire. The
satellite at Birdsville evidently got orders
to visit the camp, for he made the trip, duly '
inspected and departed; Some time later
the station manager was informed that the
blacks at the time of the visit were in robust
health and well supplied with food, and that
belp was unnecessary. Now, I saw them on
several occasions, and there was never any-
thing in the way of food handy, except the
small ration of flour supplied by the station.
Yet this same station had to pay 10s. per
week for every boy that was employed, and
the money was supposed to be available for
the upkeep of aged blacks. As a matter
of fact, the latter get nothing in the Diaman-
tina country, except one solitary blanket
ner annum, which may cost the Government
ss.
The Licenses Reduction Board in Victoria
has finished eight year§’ work and closed 800
pubs in over-beered districts; and the com-
pensation paid has been £400,000. But it
hasn’t been paid by the Government. Under
the wise Victorian law “the Trade” has to
find the coin, on the principle that the
remaining houses score from the closing of
the others.
In the new Arbitration Bill which will be
before the N.S.W. Parliament this session
the prohibition of strikes goes overboard.
The Government has decided to give up the
struggle to qompel men to refrain from
dropping tools and wages, and to merely
provide for regulating the foolishness. The
strike prohibition in the existing Act was
a farce, anyhow. Those who wanted to
strike went on striking as cheerfully as ever,
and getting fined, and cheerfully ignoring
the. fines. Now the proposal is to recognise
strikes as things that, though evil, are in-
evitable, and to try to avoid them by pro-
viding that they shall be illegal unless
declared in a proper fashion after arbitra-
tion has failed and a secret ballot has been
taken. Hereabouts the scheme becomes
hazy. Say a strike has been declared
illegal and fines inflicted. Is there anything
in the Bill to prevent Holman, a year or so
later, explaining that the Union has paid
Is. Sd. off the penalty and that the odd
£99 Bs. 4d. has been remitted? If there
isn’t the prospects of the new prohibition
are not more encouraging than the actual
experiences of the old prohibition.

THE PHANTOM CARD TRICK.
Five cards are shown. Anyone
mentally selects three. The five
cards are now dropped in a hat. Per-
former picks out the two cards not
selected. Someone .puts hand in hat
to bring out the three selected cards,
but they have vanished, the hat being quite
empty. Very bewildering and easy to work.

taken of communications insufficiently stamped.

tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

William O’Halloran, one of the oldest
natives of Tasmania, born in Launceston
in 1844, has just died. lie was son of a
soldier —in his early days the British soldier
was in charge in Tasmania. Having laid
down his arms Mick became landlord of the
Crown Hotel, 3 i Launceston, where his son
William was born. In the long ago there
were railway riots in Launceston, and it
was then that W. OTlalloran joined the
Police Force. He wore the uniform for
40 years, and was then given a job in the
Post Office, where he remained till he was
70. .One of his dozen or so sons, a journal-
ist on the Johannesburg Star, is now fight-
ing in German South Africa.
AT THE COHNER.
A fierce patriotic argument
Ensued the other night
Between a Britain and a german
Which ended in a fight.
Onward in'the distance waned
A policeman on his beat,
When they posed for fistic combat
At the corner of our street.
They struck pugilistic attitudes
And struggled to hit out
But were too intoxicated
To put up a good bout.
An excited crowd did cluster round
And did wildly shriek and hurray
When the german’s gallant Mrs.
Did wade into the fray.
Armed with an umbrella
She slashed her poor dead, Beat
To the joy of the spectators
At thb corner of our street.

The Rand wick (N.S.W.) Orphanage,
which cares for its infants right up to the
point where they are apprenticed to the
most suitable trades, has a proud record.
There has not been a death in the asylum
for live years, and only four in 26 years. Yet
partly because Holman hates to touch the
profits of the bookmaker or stand on the
corns of the wowser, who is the bookmaker’s
best friends and partly because some gilded
citizens have made their patriotic subscrip-
tions an excuse for closing down on the
charities, this deserving institution is com-
pelled to act as though it were a blind
beggar and hold out its cap for £303, the
amount of its deficiency for the year.
