The Inky Way.
The Sydney newsboys’ strike, or lockout,
recalls that systematic selling of newspapers
in the street is not a very old institution.
Lionel Brough, of the acting family, claimed
that he was the first to arrange for street
selling. That was when he was on the
business staff of one of the London dailies,
about the middle of the nineteenth century.
The Amalgamated Press, Limited, which
is the official business style of the Hanns-
worth family and its cronies, has been hold-
ing its annnal meeting in London. Profits,
£329,272 14s. 8d. The usual dividend is to
be paid “at a time when so many cos. have
declared no dividend at all. The directors
are not afraid of the war.” They didn’t have
to worry about paper supplies, having
established their own mills in Newfoundland,
thereby securing “enormous supplies of the
raw material which our business requires
for the production of its many journals.”
* * * *
Says a cable: “Gladys Cooper, the well-
known actress, has been awarded £1250
for libel by innuendo, without mentioning
names.” There is also the libel, which men-
tions a name without intending the person.
The classic instance is the case of Artemus
Jones. The Paris correspondent of the Sun-
day Chronicle furnished a fanciful account
of a motor festival at Dieppe:—
Upon the terrace marches the world, attracted by
the motor races. Whist! there goes Artemus Jones
with a woman who is not his wife.
There was a real Artemus Jones. lie was
a barrister practising on the North Wales
circuit. Moreover, by an ungodly combina-
tion of circumstances lie was at Dieppe. He
was then unknown, but the case which
resulted has made him immortal, for it went
right on to the House of Lords, and the
paper had to pay £1750 damages and all the
costs.
* * * *
“Hoe”: The story of the “Ilarmsworth
enterprise” (B. 21/1/T5), in securing the
sole English-language rights of the historic
French “Yellow Book” had a sequel. When
the Times charged 2s. for the translation
(despite the fact that none of the other
publications in the rainbow series cost more
than 44d., and the British “White Book” was
socialistically published at Id.), several
French newspapers wrathfully pointed out
that the prohibitive price was limiting the
circulation of the book, and thus the French
case would be almost lost to the public. On
that the British Foreign Office took the copy-
right by the horns and published a new
translation at Id. Three days after the
socialistic penny production had smashed its
monopoly, The Times, instead of taking the
Foreign Office to law for infringement of
copyright, issued the French “Yellow Book”
as a free supplement to its ordinary issue!
The only people who didn’t enjoy the comedy
were those who had indulged in two bobs’
worth of Private Enterprise.
“P.D.V.”: Considering how very modest it
is, the S. Aus. country press has provided
Parliament with an * a ordinary number
of recent members. No doubt the biggest
man ever to pass out of a little country
raggery to Parliament was Fred Holder,
who ran the Burra Record, and was the
first and greatest Speaker of the Common-
wealth Parliament. Jake Solomon, another
ex-Premier, ran the Northern Territory
Times. Of present men J. A. Southwood,
who can be as unpleasant to his opponents
as anybody in the Labor party since E. A.
Roberts’s day, is editor of the Kadina Plain
Dealer. The lately-deceased Roberts, by
the way, had also some press experience,
but wasn’t brought up to that job. Craw-
ford Vaughan is a former editor of Adelaide
Quiz. William J. Denny edited the
Southern Cross. James Hutchison was
editor of the Weekly Herald. James V.
O’Loghlen, besides running the R.C. paper,
also owned a now-defunct organ at River-
ton. Ephraim II. Coombe ran the Gawler
Bunyip. Willie Ponder was connected with
the Ivapunda Herald. W. J. C. Cole was
editor of the Laura Standard. Donald
Campbell had the Times, at Millicent.
Ash, one of the brilliant men Kingston
took under his law-wing, was also
a Sou’-eastern journalist before he took to
law and politics by way of a libel action.
# •* * *
“Trotter”: Reuter’s Co. has just had its
50th birthday, but Julius Reuter had been
boring away for 14 years previously. He
came from Cassel to London with little
money, but the great idea of getting news
quickly and scattering it among subscribers
—pure, naked news, with no opinions. His
first great startler was when he published the
speech of Napoleon III. to the Austrian
ambassador, foreshadowing the war of ’59.
After that he kept toppling success on suc-
cess, as Blowitz did afterwards ; and his com-
pany soon became the representative English
news agency. For this foreigner stuck close
to England, with a sure instinct that she was
on top. Queen Victoria, with her plain
nous, soon noted the co.’s promptitude and
accuracy, and the best help it ever got was
when she ordered that every important
message must be telegraphed at once to Wind-
sor, Balmoral, Osborne or wherever she might
be. Even at this day, when there are many
other fine agencies, it is very often a winner.
Thus its telegram about the capture of
Mafeking reached London before Roberts
knew it in South Africa, and his first infor-
mation was in a telegram conveying Reuter’s
information from London. Altogether there
are 1200 shareholders now on the register of
the co., of whom all but 20 are English.
Years ago Reuter was responsible for much
of the foreign news sent to Australia; but
when the city dailies formed the Cable Syn-
dicate Reuter’s dropped out of that line. It
still, however, supplies the bulk of the coun-
try papers. Before it came on the scene the
Buckinbong Argus used to collar what little
of the Syndicate’s cables it wanted. Reuter
bought from the Syndicate the right to the
cabies in districts where there is no paper
owned by a member of the Syndicate, and for
a few shillings a week the Buckingbong
Argus can now buy from Reuter the right
to the cables, and, moreover, can have them
delivered at the door. And that’s how the
old sign, “Reuter’s Telegram,” is still pre-
served in Australia.

The Resurrection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
swore that it was Henry, but
I That statement could not blind
Those who were present on the day
When Henry stayed behind.
Though for eight weeks had he been hid
From ancient friends and dear,
We knew, who cheered tiie parting boat,
He stayed upon the pier.
Jones waved a paper in the air,
And fixed his finger on
A cable re the splendid death
Of one named Robinson.
“Old Henry, boys; Died at the front!
He’s hardly gone away,
And here, lie’s killed upon the spot!
The foremost in the fray !”
We filled and drank a silent toast;
But e’er the drinks were gone.
“Of course ,” said Smithers, basely, “it
Is not our Robinson !”
Then Brown, with anger in his eye,
The last lone drop consumed :
“That is a base remark that moves
My British blood !” he fumed.
“Well,” Binks remarked, “it’s my idea —
I may say tint I know —
That it can not be Robinson,
For Henry did not go!”
Then Jones said, in stern, bitter tones,
Unto the breathless ring
That he would not stand by and hear
Of Treason to the King !
A moment Blithers stayed the storm —
(A tactful man was he)
With words that all were glad to hear —-
“Well, what’s it going to be?”
Still, as a flood, a moment stemmed,
Breaks bounds and straight is gone,
Commenced again the loud debate
About our Robinson.
And some declared it must be he
Who had been basely shot,
And others grimly sucked their beer
And snorted, “Tommyrot!”
They scoffed at Henry’s heart and pluck,
Till Jones in anger said
He was a cad who said such things
Of the heroic dead.
The argument grew worse and worse,
Till ev’ry man saw red ;
Each faction sneering drew apart
And cut the other dead.
’Twas Smithers struck the fatal blow;
He said that it was more
Than one of British blood could stand,
And smote Binks on the jaw.
The crowd became a mingled heap,
And as we tore and fought
Each man who came into the bar
Was in the whirlwind caught.
The barmaid shrieked as glasses fell,
But still the fight went on,
Till from the mangled heap emerged
Our Henry Robinson ! ! !
And those who’d said that he was dead,
And those w 7 ho’d shouted “ Rot!”
Broke into cheers and made of him
A hero on the spot.
I don’t know why we cheered him so,
Led by the sceptic Binks.
Unless it was that Robinson
Was paying for the drinks.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Settled Out of Court.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
u A NYBODY lost a dorg?” inquired Mr.
Maunder, holding up a small, round
metal disc. “’Cos I found this ’ere
a minute or two ago at the corner o’ Silver-
street. ‘Society Doin’s’ ’Oliday Club ; num-
ber one ’undered an’ forty-five.’”
Mr. Sharp glanced up from his paper.
“Society Doin’s, eh? That’s a rum go,
Jerry. I was jest readin’ 1 the rag w’en you
come in. Let’s see if I carn’t find nothink
about a ’Oliday Club. Oh, ere it is ! ‘Anyone
desirous o’ becomin’ a member of our ’Oliday
Club should send every week a postal note or
stamps fer one shillin’ to this orfice, on re-
ceipt o’ which a badge o’ membership, bearin’
a distinctive number, will be forwarded to ’is
address. The editor will announce every week
in the columns o’ Society Doin’s which
badge-’older is entitled to a free ’oliday, and
a sum o’ ten pound will be paid over on the
production of the badge at this orfice, Num-
ber 75 Dook-street; or, if more convenient,
the badge may be forwarded under registered
cover, when a cheque for ten pound will be
sent by return. Please note : Ten pound give
away each week. The prize last week was
awarded to Mr. ’Ener.y Mills, badge number
84, ’oo, our readers will be interested to learn,
’as bin a sufferer from loco —loco —locomo-
tion attacks yer’ ”
“Wot in the name o ’eaven’s that, Sam?”
asked the awestruck Mr. Maunder.
“Oh, it means as ’ow ’e’s been knocked
down and punctured by one o’ them noo-
fangled motor-car cabs wot goes without
’orses,” explained Mr. Sharp hurriedly.
“. . . . ‘attacks yer fer many years. We offer
’im our ’artiest congratulations.’ ”
“Wot, ’cos ’e were knocked down be a
cab?” said Mr. Maunder, incredulously.
“Silly bit ’o sorce, I call it.”
“ ‘The prize this week,’ ” continued Mr.
Sharp, still reading, “ ‘ ’as bin awarded to
Mr. Frederick Sharp, Badge-’older one
’undered an’ forty-five, address 22 Buckle
Row, Port Melbourne.’ ’Ere, Jerry, wot
did yer say was the number o’ that blessed
ole soup ticket o’ yers?”
“One ’undered an’ forty-five,” replied Mr.
Maunder.
Sam reflected for a moment. “The paper
on’y come out this mornin’,” said he, “and
?t ain’t quite 12 o’clock yet. I reck’n it’s a
bit risky. Still it’s worth tryin’. Curious
thing, ain’t it; same name as mine—Sharp?
And, funnier still, my second name’s Fred-
erick, though I’d a’most fergot it.” He care-
fully folded up his copy of Society Doings,
and placed it in his coat pocket. “Give us
’old o’ that badge, an’ put yer ’at on, Jerry.
Reck’n we'll vo an’ give them society blokes
a look in.”
“If you can git £lO out of ’em, I don’t
mind givin’ yer one fer yerself, Sam,” said
Mr. Maunder generously.
Society Doings, to quote its own rather
ambiguous words, was a journal with a cir-
culation second to none. “Nulli Secundus,”
in Gothic characters, stared the reader out
of countenance when he turned to the table
of contents. The same proud boast was
writ large on the face of the decidedly trucu-
lent-looking gentleman who received Messrs.
Maunder and Sharp in an office which was
certainly second to none for stuffiness and
general inconvenience.
Nothing daunted by the forbidding mien
of the society specialist, Sam produced, for
inspection and verification, his current copy
of the journal, together with the member-
ship badge.
“Name of Frederick Sharp, 22 Buckle
Row, Port Melbourne. Number one ’undered
an’ forty-five, boss,” he announced genially.
The journalist took the proffered badge and
subjected it to a microscopic scrutiny ; then
he reached for a big leather-bound book, and,
opening it, ran a finger down a page or two
before he found what he wanted.
“Oh, here we are,” he said. “Frederick
Sharp, 22 Buckle Row. Yes, that seems all
right. Are you the party?”
Sam nodded. Jerry looked on with a
covetous eye, as the maker and breaker of
social reputations unlocked an iron cashbox
and took from its recesses a couple of crisp
bank-notes.
“Suppose you’ve no banking account,” he
remarked curtly. “So I won’t bother about
writing a cheque. Here’s a receipt form;
sign your name in full. Got a duty stamp?”
Sam, breathing very hard, his elbows
squared on the counter, pen seriously ink-
logged, passed the question on to Mr. Maun-
der. “Got a jewty stomp, Jerry?”
Mr. Maunder sniggered feebly, but made
no response.
The journalist, fumbling in his cashbox,
brought forth a duty stamp.
“One penny for the stamp,” he demanded
abruptly.
“Don’t yer ’ear, Jerry? One penny fer
the stomp,” grunted Mr. Sharp, as with his
little finger he smeared out the large S A M
of Sam and substituted the even larger capi-
tal F of Frederick.
Mr. Maunder, searching diligently, struck
treasure trove in the hin-pocket of his pants
—a couple of battered halfpennies, to wit —
just as Sam came to the end of his labored
signature.
The censor of Society’s Doings handed
over the bank-notes. “Wish you a pleasant
holiday,” he said casually. “Good-day.”
Sam and Jerry turned to depart. Never
before had either of them so ardently
desired the free and open air of Heaven.
But even as they reached the door, it was
flung open violently from the outside —to
the grievous peril of Jerry, who was in the
van —and a red-faced, portly youth of some
thirteen summers, burst into the office.
“Me name’s Sharp,” he yelped shrilly.
“Frederick Sharp ; and I’ve lost me” ——•
Sam stood stock still, as if suddenly petri-
fied, his mouth agape, his eyes the eyes of
one who has looked upon the dreaded basilisk
of fable. As he subsequently affirmed, he
knew that he was up against trouble, “and
no bloomin’ error.” It was at this critical
juncture that Jerry displayed those powers
of resource and initiative that, had he been
born under a luckier star, would have
undoubtedly made him a leader among men.
His large, red hand descended heavily upon
the intruder’s shoulder, and checked at the
fountain-head a far too ready flow of speech.
“You’ve lost yer manners —that’s wot
you’ve lost, my lad,” he said severely.
“Runnin’ in on me an’ yer pore father an’
this ’ere gen’leman without so much as by
yer leave. Now, none o’ yer back answers,
young Fred, ’cos I won’t ’ave it, an’ so I
fell yer.” He swung round on the astonished
social reformer. “Me friend’s son an' my
gaw’son, sir,” he explained ; “though I could
wish as ’ow 7 sech was not the case, con-
siderin’ ’is conduck. He come along to tell
’is pore dad that ’e’s lost ’is situation in the
’olesale drapery, an’ small wonder, knowin’
as I do the bitter trial he is to all as lays
’emselves out to give ’im a ’elpin' ’and. ’Old
yer tongue ! D’yer’ear me?” He shook the
maligned youth so fiercely by the collar that
signs of incipient strangulation supervened.
“Well, you can clear out of this, the lot
of you, as soon as you like.” suggested the
journalist brusquely, as he prepared _to
resume his interrupted labors of “cleansing
the Augean stables of society,” vide his lead-
ing article of last week but one.
Sam pushed Jerry and his unhappy victim
out into Duke-street, a quiet, unfrequented
by-way. But whether it was that, like many
another highly-strung and imaginative per-
son, Mr. Maunder had thoroughly persuaded
himself that the figment of his fancy was
irrefragable fact; or whether, like an over-
zealous actor, he was so carried away by his
part that he found it quite impossible to
descend all at once to every-day existence, is
a hard question to answer. A sort of magni-
ficent frenzy seemed for the time being to
have taken possession of the ancient man ;
and he did so maul, cuff and rumple the
shock head of his protesting cantive that
Sam felt it incumbent upon himself to inter-
pose. “Let ’im alone, carn’t yer, Jerry?
You’ll ’ave a crowd round us in less’n two
ticks.” he remonstrated.
“Wot’s the boy bin doin’ of, old ’un?”
inquired a man who happened to be passing
by at the moment. Two or three other
foot passengers also paused and regarded the
proceedings with some interest. v
“Doin’ of?” shouted the enraged Mr. Maun-
der. “Why, ’e’s bin sorcin’ ’is pore father
’ere, wot’s too broken-’arted to ’it ’im back.
Lost ’is situation in the ’olesale ’ardware
trade owin’ to bad habits; and instead o’
bringin’ the week’s wages wot they give ’im
in 100 o’ notice ’ome with ’im, ’e’s spent it
all in righteous” (probably riotous) “livin’.
Twelve bob, it were. Oh, ’e’s a wrong ’un,
’e is, all right. ’Old still, or I’ll be the death
on you, I will, my lad !”

RITA MOYA discourses on
NATURAL BEAUTY,
and How to obtain it.
To-day every woman of taete and refinement avoids the use of cosmetics or made-up
toilet preparations which give an artificial appearance. The fresh, natural complexion that
few women have, and every woman desires, is best obtained by use of original ingredients
already at hand or which can be obtained from any reliable chemist. My advice is to avoid
the use of most made-up face creams, rouge and beautifying preparations. They are usually
obvious and often disappointing. If you will get only the proper original ingredients you will
be satisfied with the result. Insist on having what you ask for. If the chemist does not
happen to have it in stock, he can immediately get it from his wholesaler.
Oood Shampoo Important. —The hair should
be allowed to breathe, and the greasy film
around each strand must be removed with a
mild non-alkaline shampoo. Soaps should be
tabooed. The very best solution for the pur-
pose can be made by dissolving a teaspoonful
of stallax granules in a cup of hot water. It
stimulates the scalp to healthy action, and at
the same time leaves the hair in that soft,
fluffy condition so much admired. Any chemist
can supply you with an original packet of
stallax, sufficient to make 25 or 30 shampoos.
Magic for Superfluous Hair. —It is astound-
ing the number of women who suffer from
unsightly growths of hair on the face, and it
will come as a piece of good news to know
that there is a simple substance known as
powdered pheminol which will remove it im-
mediately and permanently. Mix a small
quantity into a thin paste with a little water,
and apply to the objectionable growths. In
two minutes all trace of the hair will have
entirely vanished, and your skin will be as
soft and smooth as a child’s.
To have Smooth, White Skin all through the
Winter. —Does your skin chap or roughen
easily, or become unduly red or blotchy ? Let
me tell you a quick and easy way to over-
come the trouble and keep your complexion
beautifully white, smooth and soft. Just get
some ordinary mercolised wax at the chem-
ist’s, and use a little before retiring as you
would use cold cream. The wax, through
some peculiar action, flecks off the rough, dis-
colored or blemished skin. The worn-out
cuticle comes off just like dandruff on a dis-
eased scalp only in almost invisible particles.
Mercolised wax simply hastens Nature’s
work, which is the rational and proper way to
attain a perfect complexion, so much sought
after, but very seldom seen. The process is
perfectly simple and quite harmless.
For Dry, Irritating and Dandruffy Scalps.
can compare with the following
recipe for the above condition, and until you
have rectified any trouble in this direction it
is useless to expect the hair to grow healthy
and vigorous. Mix a package of boranium
with J-pint of bay rum, shake the bottle well
and allow to stand for 30 minutes, then add
i-pint cold water and strain. This simple
home remedy will stimulate the hair roots to
their normal functions, resulting in a beauti-
ful growth of new hair.
Rouge Not Necessary.—lf you must add a
little color to the cheeks please do not use
rouge; it is always so painfully obvious that
women of refinement have now discarded it
for ever. There is a little-known substance
called colliandum obtainable at most chem-
ist’s, which can be used in its place. It gives
a perfectly natural tint and defies the closest
scrutiny, besides having the undoubted ad-
vantage of not rubbing off.
The Powder Puff Passes.—-A persistently
shiny nose, or a dull, lifeless complexion
drives many a woman to cosmetics and conse-
quent despair. And all the time a simple
remedy lies at hand in the home. Get from
your, chemist about an ounce of cleminite,
and add sufficient water to dissolve it. A
little of this simple lotion is Nature’s own
beautifier. It is very good for the skin and
instantly gives the complexion a soft, velvety,
youthful bloom that any woman might envy.
It lasts all day or evening, renders powdering
entirely unnecessary and absolutely defies
detection.
Miscellaneous Hints. —For lips inclined to bs
rough or harsh use a soft stick of prolactum.
An ideal complexion soap is one called
pilenta. It contains no free alkali, and is as
near neutrality as science can make it.
To make the eyelashes grow long, dark and
curling, massage them gently with mennaline.
It is quite a harmless substance, and will not
injure the eyes.

“Leggo me collar, then! You ’it one o’
yer own size, carn’t yer....’00 are you
callin’ me father? ’E ain’t me father.. ..I
wouldn’t hold to ’im. .. .1 never seen neither
of yer ugly mugs before —not in all me life,”
gasped the purple-faced juvenile, speaking
in sections. “An’ you jest gimme my
£lO wot you swindled me out of —I see
’im put my ’oliday badge in ’is pocket w’en
’e thought I wasn’t lookin’.”
“He’s got a bad, cunnin’-lookin’ sort of a
face fer a young boy,” remarked one of the
spectators. “You take my advice, my lad,
and go ’ome quietly with yer pore ole grand-
father” (Mr. Maunder winced slighily), “or
else you’ll be cornin’ to the gallus one o’ these
fine days.”
“He ain’t me grandfather. I never saw
’im before, not in all me life,” howled the
indignant youth.
“ ’Ave you searched ’is boots, mister?”
asked another onlooker, addressing Jerry
with the air of one proffering good counsel.
“I ’ad a boy once—terribly artful boy, ’e
<vas, and fer munce and munce, ev’ry Satur-
dee, reg’lar as clockwork, ’e used to ’ide ’arf
a crown in ’is boot an’ make out that he’d
never got the rise ’is boss promised ’im.”
“Oh, carm on, Jerry,” expostulated Mr.
Sharp, in an agitated aside. “Drop the kid,
do, and let’s clear, quick ’n’ lively.”
But Mr. Maunder was not to be denied.
Retaining a vice-like hold of his victim’s
collar, with one huge hand he rapidly, and
with the skill born of long practice, went
through the wriggling youth’s pockets, re-
gardless of kicks and recriminations. At
the end of the operation, he was the richer
by three half-crowns.
“Now, you come straight ’ome, my lad,
an’ I’ll give yer the liveliest ’idin’ ever you
’ad,” he said with decision.
“That’s the way to tork to boys,” ob-
served a spectator admiringly. “The velvet
glove in the iron ’and. ’E ain’t too old fer
the strap, Mister.”
“Cert’ly not,” said Mr. Maunder firmly.
“Wait till I git at ’im with my braces.”
He set off at a dignified pace, holding
the volubly protesting Master Sharp by the
arm. Sam, shivering with fury, and mutter-
ing the direst threats of vengeance to come,
accompanied the disciplinarian and his
charge. The little knot of onlookers
reluctantly dispersed, shaking their heads
sagely.
“Wodger goin’ to do now, Jerry?”
demanded Sam, gritting his teeth savagely.
Mr. Maunder grinned a senile grin. He
was face to face with the stern realities of
existence once again.
“Gawd knows, Sam. If I could on’y find
a nice deep drain ’andy I’d drop ’im in.
Reck’n we ’ad to keep the game up afore
all them people; but wot to do with ’im
now, I’ve about as much idea as wot you
’as. If I let ’im go ’e’ll track us ’ome as
like as not an’ then bring the cops. ’E
looks wicked enough for anythin’. An’ if
I don’t let ’im go I’ll ’ave to drag ’im ’ome,
which’ll be as bad.”
“Well, if you like to walk ’arf over Mel-
bourne ’limpin’ that ’ere squawkin’ limb o’
Satan along with you, you can please yer-
self, but don’t arst me to keep you com-
p’ny,” retorted Sam, regarding the now
lachrymose juvenile with intense disfavor.
“Fer ’Eaven’s sake don’t go back on a
bloke, Sam,” besought Mr. Maunder in
some alarm. “You jest give over tryin’ to
kick yer elders an’ betters, my young
sparrer-’opper, or else I’ll ’and you out wot
for. Blest if ever I come acrost sech a
’eadstrong. vindickertive boy, no, not in all
my born days.”
“Serve yer right,” said Sam shortly, as
the tears of anguish, tapped by a well-
planted kick on the shins, welled up in
Jerry’s eyes.
“You lemme go then,” screamed the
captive, exultantly conscious that he had
at last succeeded in getting a good solid one
home on his tormentor.
Jerry shook him by the neck, much as a
terrier shakes a rat. “I’ll be the death on
you in less ’n ’arf a minute,” he vowed for
about the twentieth time.
“Wot are you doin’ to that boy, you great
coward? If you don’t drop ’im I’ll drop
you, soon as look at you,” said a deep, de-
termined voice that should by rights have be-
longed to a man. But it was the attribute
of a big woman with an apron over her
head, and a bottle of beer peeping coyly from
its newspaper wrappings, tucked under each
arm.
With a shrill whoop of triumph, Mr.
Maunder’s protege made a supreme effort
aDd broke away from his stern mentor’s de-
taining hold.
“Give it to ’em ’ot, mother,” he shouted.
“They done me outer ten pound ’oliday
money, an’ the ole bloke with the injer-
lubber mug ’as bin through me pockets an’
pinched the seven an’ six wot you give me
to pay ole Four-eyes ’is_ week’s rent with!”
“ ’Old yer tongue, you young viper, and
wait till yer spoken to,” was the fond
maternal response, accompanied by a back-
hander that not only sent the luckless youth
spinning into the gutter, but seriously jeo-
pardised one of the’ beer bottles. Then she
turned on Mr. Sharp, the high color of
whose naturally rubicund face had changed
to a sickly greenish hue. “Well, if this ain’t
a stroke o’ luck.” she remarked grimly. “I
’eard you was in Brisbane. Sam.”
Mr. Sharp grinned guiltily. “Thought
you’d gone west,” he muttered. “I posted
the last remittance wot I sent to the address
you give me in Perth.”
“That was a year an’ a ’alf ago,” re-
torted the lady; “and I ain’t touched a
copper o’ yers ever since. Wot yer goin’ to
do about it, might I kindly arsk? That
there boy o’ yers ain’t kep’ fer nixes, an’ the
young rin never sticks to a job more’n a
week. Takes arter ’is good-fer-nuthin’
father, I s’pose.” She finished with a sniff
militant.
“Called ’im Freddy arter me! That was
kind an’ thoughtful, I must own; though I
shouldn’t ’ardly ’ave knowed ’im, not arter
all these years,” said Sam, making a feeble
attempt to draw a red herring across the
track of the virgo’s wrath. “He were in
petticoats last time I saw ’im” —
“’Oo’s yer friend?” interrupted the lady
of the beer bottles, regarding Mr. Maunder
with a fixed and hostile stare.
Jerry shuffled his feet uneasily. Mr.
Sham welcomed this heaven-sent opportunity
of side tracking the financial question.
“Why, that’s ole Jerry Maunder,” he ob-
served with a ghastly parody of his usual
free-and-easy manner. “A pal_o’ mine. Jerry,
this ’ere’s my missus. We ain’t bin playin’
speaks these last few years.”
Mr. Maunder cleared his throat huskily,
and in an agony of apprehension sought to
evade the matron’s vengeful eye.
“Pleased to meet you both, I’m sure,”
said Mrs. Sharp with a vinegary smile.
“And, an.v’ow, I wants ten pounds out o’ yer,
Sam. We may as well come inside, instead
o’ yappin’ here. I live just round the cor-
ner—-22 Buckle Row.”
“Ten pounds—s’welp me!” began Mr.
Sharp in genuine consternation.
“Oh, I wasn’t born yesterday,” put in his
wife tartly. “It’s ten pounds down or a
policeman, whichever you prefer. You can’t
marry a girl and then leave ’er to toil and
moil fer ’erself and the son wot you was
responsible fer, and can’t deny it, seein’ as
’ow be ’as all of ’is father’s vices and none
of his poor mother’s virtues. Come inside,
and you too, Mr. Monday, or whatever yer
name is.”
Mrs. Sham’s living room could hardly be
called a luxuriously-appointed apartment,
but besides the two bottles of beer afore-
mentioned, it contained a full bottle of gin,
and the lady, to do her justice, had a due
regard to the rules of hospitality. But per-
haps for the first time in his mis-spent life,
Mr. Maunder refused liquid refreshment.
“Never mind; all the more fer us,” was
Mrs. Sharp’s curt comment. “Now then
Sam, ’elp yerself.”
Sam helped himself —liberally.
Seated comfortlessly on the edge of an
uneasy chair, momentarily apprehensive of
the advent of the banished Master Sharp, Mr.
Maunder listened in speechless indignation,
firstly, to the heated recriminations of thp
newly-united couple; secondly, to their
general rapprochement, an edifying consum-
mation entirely brought about by the soften-
ing influences of that seductive mixture
“Dog’s Nose” ; thirdly and lastly, to a com-
plete entente cordiale, cemented by the pro-
duction and unconditional handing over by
Mr. Sharp of two crisp five-pound notes.
“Now, ’Tilda, I ’one yer satisfied,” said Sam.
Mrs. Sharp nodded. “I alius said you was
a gen’leman w’en in licker, Sam,” she re-
marked. Rising with a stateliness somewhat
marred by an inadvertent lurch, which neces-
sitated a saving clutch at the table, she de-
posited the notes in a cracked vase that
stood on the mantelpiece. Then she returned
to her seat, but her eye for the unconvivial
Mr. Maunder was soured and jaundiced.
“Settin’ there like a ’eathen Tndoo image
—addle-’eaded ole fowl!” she muttered, with
a complete disregard for the mating of her
metaphors.
“Never mind ’im,” said Sam, contemptu-
ously. “ ’Ole Jerry don’t count. Give us a
kiss’ ’Tilda, to show there ain’t no ill-
feelin’.”
The endearments that followed so nau
seated the high-minded Mr. Maunder that
he rose from his seat and leant heavily
against the mantelpiece, as if for support.
He stood there a moment, then made
majestically for the door.
“I ain’t a-goin’ to stop ’ere no longer.”
he announced firmly. “I can see I ain’t
wanted. Wish yer joy, Sam.” He sniffed
loudly. “If there’s one smell in this world
wot I do ’ate and detest, it’s gin,” he added
vindictively; then the door closed abruptly
upon his ancient, shambling form.
Jerry had been back at number eleven
Silver-street two or three hours before Sam
put in an appearance, rather .red about the
eyes, and looking distinctly woebegone.
The old man greeted the prodigal coldly.
“I’m afraid you’ve bin livin’ a double-faced
life, Sam,” he said sternly. “You bin
deceivin’ me as well as that pore long-
sufferin’ wife o’ yers ; while as fer yer dear
little son an’ hair—Gawd ’elp us!”
Mr. Sharp tossed his hat across the room.
“If the ole girl ’adn’t a-bin so shicker I’d
never ’ave got away from ’er,” he growled.
“Well, a fine mess you’ve made o’ the bis-
ness, ain’t you, Jerry Maunder. But fer
yer dam’ foolishness we’d ha’ bin a fiver
each in pocket this blessed minute.”
Mr. Maunder grinned. “Orright, don’t
git narked, mate,” he said soothingly. “It
all stops in the fam’ly. And if I don’t
begrudge my little bit to ’elp bi’iug some
’appiness an’ sunshine into that there pore,
lonely, neglected woman’s life, I’m sure you
didn’t ought to. Besides” (he rattled the
loose silver in his pocket), “we’re three ’arf
a crowns to the good—leastways, I am.”
Sam turned a thundercloud visage on his
comrade.
“You jest ’and over them three ’arf crowns
at onst, Jerry,” he said fiercely. “They
belong to my pore boy wot you so croolly
robbed ; an’ wot’s more, I mean to ’old ’em
in trust fer ’im until he comes of an age
to unnerstand the value o’ money. Now
are you a-goin’ to fork out, or ’ave I gotter
make you?”
Jerry struggled manfully with his emo-
tion. He pulled the cash from his pocket
and flung it on the table with a fine dramatic
gesture. “Take it,” he said bitterly. “You’re
the bloke wot never rounds on ’is cobbers,
ain’t yer? Buy Guv’ment securities with
it, an’ wotever you do, don’t fergit to add
on the hinterest w’en you come to settle
up with that lively young ’opeful o’ yers.”
Sam picked up the money, and laughed
a rude, derisive laugh. “I can do better
than Guv’ment securities, Jerry,” he re-
marked. “I’m goin’ to invest it in a
brewery wot I knows of. So long; see
you later. Sorry I carn’t arst yer to come
along with me. on’y one ’as to be partic’lar
about one’s comp’ny w’en there’s bisness
doin’. Eh?” He paused, his hand on the
door-latch. “I’ll ’ave to sample the beer
first, to see if I think the speculation’s worth
while,” he said, with a cunning wink.
“Ketch ’old, mate, ’ere’s a ’oliday badge
fer yer—number one hindered an’ forty-
five.”
Left alone, Jerry sighed a requiem sigh
in memory of a long partnership now dis-
solved for ever. Then he felt in his breast
pocket, and having reassured himself con-
cerning the safety of the two bank notes
that reposed therein, he took a farewell
glance round the little room, and stole
cautiously away into the fast-gathering
dusk.
Mr. Tiberius Galilee discounted the notes
at a special rate of twenty-five per cent.

The
Importance
of
Efficient
Insulation!
The importance of efficient insulation, in many cases, is not realised. Great
care is shown in the selection of a steam plant to ensure the greatest economy,
yet the insulation which is of equal importance is in many cases partly neg-
lected. If you strive to make a saving in the steam consumption of your
engine, why not make certain that it is not wasted again through faulty in-
sulation? The claims we make as to the exceptional efficiency of Newall’s
Coverings‘are fully borne out by actual test, and, further, by the enormous
sales, including repeat orders, and we do not know of a single instance where
any other covering has been used once Newall’s have been tried.
The Ultimo and White Bay Power Houses are insulated throughout with
Newall’s Coverings, and this material has also been adopted exclusively by the
N.S.W. and Victorian Governments. These are facts whith will tend to fur-
ther prove our claims. The innumerable advantages of this material over all
other forms of lagging are described in interesting booklet, which we will be
pleased to mail you along with samples.
